# D. 49 - The rewards of the blessed

***The rewards of the blessed***

After the Master has considered what pertains to the general judgment, in this part he begins to consider the rewards and punishments that follow the general judgment. And it is divided into two parts. In the first, he determines the rewards of the good. In the second, the punishments of the wicked, at Distinction 50: *here a question arises*.*Sententiae* IV 50.1 (284). 1.  

Now the first part is divided into three parts. In the first, he describes what will be the quality of the beatitude, or blessedness, of the saints, which will be given to them as a reward after the general judgment. In the second, he shows how every human appetite tends to its attainment: *concerning blessedness, it is also usual to ask whether all wish it*.*Sent*. IV 49.1 (280). 3.  

In the third, how those who possess beatitude participate in it in different ways: *it is usual to ask whether someone of greater merit, such as Peter, will know something concerning God which someone of lesser merit, such as Linus, will not know*.*Sent*. IV, 49.2 (281). 1.  

Concerning this he does three things. First, he shows the differentiation that will occur in the saints’ beatitude on the part of knowledge. Second, on the part of joy or delight: *it is also usual to ask whether they are unequal in joy*.*Sent*. IV, 49.3 (282). 1. Third, he inquires about the increase of beatitude that will occur from the resumption of the body: *it is usual to ask next whether the blessedness of the saints will become greater after the judgment than in the meantime*.*Sent*. IV, 49.4 (283). 1.  

Here five questions arise. First, concerning beatitude. Second, concerning the vision of God, in which beatitude principally consists. Third, concerning delight, which completes beatitude formally. Fourth, concerning the “dowries,” which are included in beatitude. Fifth, concerning the crowns of special distinction,Aquinas uses *aureola*, “little crown,” to express an added distinction or honor awarded to some of the blessed in addition to the *aurea*, “golden crown” or “halo,” which represents the essential prize of beatitude. whereby beatitude is perfected and adorned.  

Question 1  

***Beatitude***

***Concerning the first, four questions arise:***

first, where beatitude should be sought;  

- second, what it is;
- third, whether all desire it;
- fourth, whether all participate in it equally.
- Article 1

***Where beatitude should be sought***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the first question we proceed thus.Parallels: cf. similar discussions at *ST* I-II, q. 2, aa. 1–5; *SCG* III, chs. 28–32; *Sent. Eth*. I, lec. 5. It seems that beatitude*Beatitudo* can be also translated as “blessedness” or “happiness.” consists in goods of the body. For it is impossible that what is asserted by most people is totally false, as the Commentator says in the book *On the Soul*; and the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7, *a saying generally expressed among the people never dies completely*.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 13, 1153b27. But the greater number of people are inclined to seek physical pleasures and bodily goods as their end. Therefore, the end of human life consists in bodily goods. But we call the end of human life beatitude. Therefore, beatitude should be sought in goods of the body.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, the more final an end is in order of attainment, the more prior it is in intention and desire. But man desires bodily good before he desires spiritual good, since we are led, as by the hand, from the love of bodily things to the love of invisible things, as Gregory says.Gregory the Great, *Homilies on the Gospels*, Homily 11, n. 1 (CCSL 141:74). Therefore, bodily good is our ultimate end. But such an end is beatitude. Therefore, beatitude should be sought in bodily goods.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, the more common a good is, the more divine it is, as is clear from the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 2, 1094b6–10. In context, this statement is not set down as a general principle but is asserted to be true of the state or political society. But bodily good is more common than spiritual good, because bodily good extends itself even to plants and brute animals, while spiritual good does not. Therefore, bodily good is superior to spiritual good; and so beatitude is to be sought more in bodily goods than in spiritual ones.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, beatitude is held by all to be virtue’s end. But virtue has its end not only in spiritual goods, but also in bodily ones; for through the virtue of temperance and other such virtues, man is preserved from harmful things even in regard to his body. Therefore, beatitude is to be sought not only in spiritual goods but also in bodily ones.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, according to the Philosopher in the *Physics* 2,Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 2, ch. 6, 197b4. happiness and fortune seem to have to do with the same things. But the goods of fortune are bodily goods. Therefore, the goods in which beatitude and happiness consistsThough Thomas mentions both *beatitudo et felicitas*, he uses a singular verb, *consistit*, as if the two were meant to be synonymous. are bodily.  

Obj. 6: Furthermore, man is constituted of soul and body. Therefore, man’s good ought to be common to soul and body. But spiritual good cannot be shared by the body, whereas bodily good can be shared by the soul, inasmuch as the soul takes pleasure in bodily things. Therefore beatitude, which is man’s good, consists more in bodily goods than in spiritual ones.  

On the contrary (1), what belongs to man according to his body can be common to him and to the other animals. But the other animals cannot possess beatitude. Therefore, beatitude should not be sought in goods of the body.  

Furthermore (2), beatitude is man’s highest good. Therefore, it is to be sought among the foremost goods of man. But goods of the soul are nobler than goods of the body, just as the soul is nobler than the body. Therefore, beatitude is to be sought in goods of the soul.  

Furthermore (3), the ultimate measure is the one that is not measured in any way. Therefore, whatever cannot be good without being measured cannot be the ultimate measure in human things, nor can it be the ultimate end, which is beatitude, since the end is the measure that imposes the proper mode on things ordered to an end. But bodily goods are neither praiseworthy nor good except insofar as they are measured with the measure of virtue, as is evident from what the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 2.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 2, ch. 2, 1104a11–18. Therefore, beatitude cannot be found in bodily goods.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that beatitude consists more in things belonging to will than in things belonging to intellect.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 4; *ST* I, q. 26, a. 2, ad 2; *SCG* III, ch. 26; *Quodl*. VIII, q. 9, a. 1; *Comp. theol*. I, ch. 107. For beatitude is the highest good. But good, precisely as good, is the object of will, not of intellect. Therefore, beatitude consists more in an act of will than an act of intellect.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, delight is required for beatitude, according to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 7;Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 13, 1153b9–21. this is why in Greek the word for beatitude is taken from the word for joy. But delight is in the will or affection. Therefore, so is beatitude.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 10,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7, 1177a11. happiness or beatitude consists in the act of the noblest virtue. But charity is the most excellent of all virtues, as is clear from the Apostle in 1 Corinthians 13. Therefore, since charity is in the will, that is where one should look for beatitude.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, as the soul commands the body, so does the will command the intellect, and, consequently, it is superior to it. But beatitude is considered to reside more in goods of the soul than in those of the body, because the soul is superior to the body. Therefore, by the same reasoning, it should be considered to reside more on the side of will than on the side of intellect.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, man’s beatitude consists in perfect union with God. But man is united to God more perfectly by will than by intellect. This is why when commenting on the phrase *mobile and acute* (the *Celestial Hierarchy*, chapter 7), Hugh of St. Victor says: *love towers above knowledge, and is greater than understanding. For God is more loved than understood; love enters in, where knowledge remains outside*.Hugh of St. Victor, *Commentary on The Celestial Hierarchy of Saint Dionysius the Areopagite*, Bk. 6, exp. of ch. 7, PL 175:1038. When Thomas cited this text earlier (*In III Sent*. d. 27, q. 1, a. 4, arg. 10) the wording follows what is found in the PL ed.: *Plus enim diligitur quam intelligitur*. Here, at least in the Parma ed., the wording differs: *Plus enim Deus diligitur quam intelligatur*. Therefore, beatitude consists in love more than in knowledge, and in will more than in intellect.  

On the contrary (1) is what it says in John 17:3: *this is eternal life, that they know thee the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom thou hast sent*. But eternal life is beatitude itself. Therefore, beatitude consists in knowledge.  

Furthermore (2), commenting on 1 Corinthians 15:24, *when he delivers the kingdom to God the Father after destroying every rule and every authority and power*, a GlossDerived from Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 1, ch. 10 (CCSL 50:56). says: *this means, when he has conducted the faithful to the contemplation of God the Father, in which is found the end of all, everlasting rest and joy*. But this is a description of beatitude. Therefore, beatitude consists in intellectual contemplation.  

Furthermore (3), the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, chs. 7–8, 1177a11ff. shows that man’s beatitude consists in the act of a contemplative virtue. But this pertains to intellect. Therefore beatitude consists most of all in understanding.  

Furthermore (4), according to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 1,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7, 1097b14–16. the good in which there is sufficiency *per se* is happiness or beatitude. But this is found in knowledge, as is clear from John 14:8: *Lord, show us the Father, and we shall be satisfied*. Therefore, the same as above.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that beatitude consists in an act of the practical intellect rather than of the speculative intellect.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 5; *Sent. Eth*. X, lec. 10–12. For the more common a good is, the more divine it is, as is clear from the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 2, 1094b6–10. But the good of the speculative intellect belongs solely to the one who contemplates, whereas the good of the practical intellect can be shared by many.Through the execution of a practical plan. Therefore beatitude consists more in the practical than the speculative intellect.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, beatitude is man’s ultimate perfection. But when something’s perfection is the cause of something else, it is greater than when it exists perfect in itself. This is why in Chapter 3 of the *Celestial Hierarchy*, Dionysius says that of all things the most godlike is to be made a coworker with God in the governing of others.In this line, *reductio* has to be taken as broadly as possible, to include the three tasks of purification, illumination, and perfection that Pseudo- Dionysius argues to be the work of God in creation and the work he shares with creatures capable of undertaking it. See *The Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 3, 165D–168A. Now, through the speculative intellect a man has perfection in himself, whereas the practical intellect is the cause of others being perfected. Therefore, beatitude consists more in the practical than in the speculative intellect.  

Obj. 3: Further, the end is like those things that are for the sake of the end. But beatitude is the end and reward of virtue, as is said in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 9, 1099b16–17. Now virtues consist more in activity than in knowledge, because merely knowing does little or nothing for virtue, as is clear from the *Ethics* 2.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 2, ch. 4, 1105b6. Hence beatitude consists more in the practical intellect than in the speculative.  

Obj. 4: Further, man’s beatitude consists in what is noblest in man. But the practical intellect is nobler than the speculative, since it lays down the law for the speculative intellect; for it is by the law-maker’s prudence that it is ordained how someone should learn the speculative disciplines, as is clear from the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 2, 1094a27–b1. Therefore, beatitude resides more in the practical intellect than in the speculative.  

On the contrary (1), since beatitude is the ultimate end, it is sought for its own sake, not for the sake of something else. But practical knowledge is ordered to something other than itself as its end, namely to a given work, as it says in the *Metaphysics* 2.Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 2, ch. 1, 993b20–23. Now it is not so with speculative knowledge, which is sought for its own sake, as is clear from the *Metaphysics* 1.Thomas may not have a single text in mind but more the general perspective of the first book, which concerns the distinctively human quest for answers to wonder-born questions: Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1 (980a22–27, 981b14–24); ch. 2 (982a14–16, 982a30–b8, 982b11–28). Therefore, beatitude resides more in the speculative than in the practical intellect.  

Furthermore (2), our beatitude consists in being joined to God. But we are joined to God not through the practical intellect, but through the speculative. Therefore, beatitude consists more in the speculative than in the practical intellect.  

Furthermore (3), the notion of beatitude includes permanence, sufficiency, and delight. But in the act of the contemplative intellect man is more sufficient in himself than in the act of the practical intellect, for which he requires many exterior aids. Nor is there an action in which man is able to persist so long as in contemplation, because it is so far removed from corporeal instruments, whose weakness brings about weariness. Nor is there any delight so pure as that of contemplation, because no contrary is mixed in with it; for it does not even have a contrary. Therefore, beatitude most of all consists in an act of the speculative intellect.  

Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that beatitude can be had in this life.Parallels: *In IV Sent*. d. 43, a. 1, qa. 1; *ST* I-II, q. 5, a. 3; *SCG* III, ch. 48; *Sent. Eth*. I, lec. 10 and 16. For Matthew 5:3 says: *blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven*, and similarly: *blessed are they who suffer persecution*; and other sayings of this kind. But all these things can be had in this life. Therefore, there is beatitude in this life.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, if beatitude could not exist in this life, no one would know about it unless he had some knowledge of the other life. But many philosophers completely ignorant of the future life have treated of beatitude. Therefore, beatitude is possible even in this life.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, man’s present life is more perfect than the present life of any other animal. But the present life of other animals includes the attainment of their ultimate end. Therefore, man’s present life, too, includes beatitude, which is man’s end.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, each and every thing naturally desires its end. But from the desire for the end arises a desire for the things that lead to the end. Now, those things without which one cannot attaind the end are especially ordered to the end. But man’s death would be ordered to beatitude this way, if man could not have beatitude in this present life. Therefore, man would naturally desire death; which is perceived to be false by experience, and contradicts the authority of the Apostle, who says in 2 Corinthians 5:4: *not that we wish to be unclothed, but further clothed*. Therefore man’s beatitude exists in this life.  

On the contrary (1), no one can be blessed who does not have what he does well to desire.i.e., as he ought to will it. But every blessed person well and ordinately wills never to be able to be deprived of his beatitude. Therefore, anyone who does not have this security is not truly blessed. But no one in this life has such security, since the very happiness that one can have in this life must be taken away at least by death. Therefore, beatitude cannot be had in this life.  

Furthermore (2), no one has already arrived at the ultimate degree of life if there remains anything left for him to desire, since beatitude possesses sufficiency in itself, as even the Philosopher says.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7, 1097b14–16. But no matter how perfect someone in this life may be in knowledge or virtue or any other perfection, there still remains something for him to desire, such as knowing many things that he does not know; indeed, even the goodness of his present perfection, as long as this life goes on, cannot be certain, since even the wisest and most perfect men may end up in madness through bodily infirmities. Therefore, beatitude cannot be had in this life.  

Furthermore (3), everyone holds that beatitude is the greatest good. But the greatest good is what is thoroughly unmixed with evil. Therefore, since regardless of how much someone is wise and perfect in virtue, this life cannot be without evils (as is manifestly apparent when one looks at the various accidents and misfortunes that befall man), it seems that beatitude cannot exist in this life.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, since it is what is naturally desired by all men, ‘beatitude’ designates the ultimate end of human life. Now the end of anything is its proper activity, or it arrives at its end through its proper activity. Now, since the principle of something’s proper activity is its proper form, and the proper form of man as man is the rational soul, it necessarily follows that man’s beatitude consists either in the very acts of the rational soul, or in those things that man is related to through the acts of the rational soul. But these things are called goods of the soul, which is why even according to the pagan philosophers it is necessary to locate beatitude in goods of the soul. However, that some people located their beatitude in goods of the body arises from the fact that they were ignorant of what they themselves really are; for they did not recognize what was better in them, which formally completed their being, but they only saw in themselves what appeared on the outside;viz., the bodily aspect. and accordingly they sought their beatitude in external goods.  

Reply Obj. 1: It is not necessary that the opinion of many be true simply speaking, but only that it be true in part. And indeed, the multitude of men who located their beatitude in goods of the body do have a true opinion in this respect: whatever they judge best for themselves, they consider their beatitude to be; and their opinion is true to this degree, namely that the best for man is man’s beatitude. However, their opinion need not be true when it comes to locating of beatitude in goods of the body, since this opinion proceeds from a false root, because they judge themselves to be chiefly what they are as regards the body.  

Reply Obj. 2: The fact that physical good is desired before spiritual good only happens incidentally. For our knowledge proceeds from the more general to the particular, as is clear from the *Physics* 1;Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1, 184a16–b13. and so in the beginning of our lives we know nothing about the end of man except something general—that it is a certain best thing to aim for. And as we know, so do we desire. Thus, we judge that good to be among those goods that first come within range of our knowledge. However, the first things in our knowledge are sensible things; this is why in the beginning we judge sensible goods as the highest goods, but eventually, when our knowledge is more mature, we come to have a distinct knowledge of man’s end, by discerning it clearly from others; and at that point we hunger for the highest good where it is truly to be found, namely in spiritual realities. But the argument proceeded as though appetite for bodily good preceded appetite for spiritual good due to the nature of these things.Rather than due to our knowing bodily good before spiritual good.  

Reply Obj. 3: Something can be said to be common in two ways. In one way, by predication; but what is common in this way is not found numerically the same in the different things [to which it is common]; and this is how the body’s good has commonality. In another way something is common according to a participation of numerically one and the same thing; and in this way a more common good is more divine. And this commonality can most of all be found in those things that pertain to the soul, since what is the common good of all things, namely God, is touched through the soul; and so the argument does not follow.  

Reply Obj. 4: There are two kinds of ends: an activity’s end and an intention’s end. So, then, the body’s good can be the end of virtue, in the sense of a certain terminus or effect of virtuous activity; but it cannot be the end on which virtue’s intention rests. The reason is that virtue is a perfection of the soul, which is nobler than the body, and nothing acts for the sake of what is baser than itself; thus it cannot be that the intention of virtue rests in the body’s good.  

Reply Obj. 5: Happiness and fortune are said of something in two ways. In one way, as of a subject; and in this way happiness and fortune pertain to the same thing, because neither can exist except in rational beings. In another way, as of an object or matter; and taken this way it is impossible that happiness and fortune pertain to the same thing, because fortune has to do with things done for the sake of something, when something unintended happens, whereas happiness or beatitude is intended by all, and it is not ordered to any thing else. This is why, although bodily things are called goods of fortune with respect to its matter, it does not follow that happiness consists in bodily goods.  

Reply Obj. 6: Although a human being consists of soul and body, he has his specific being from the soul, not from the body, because the form of anything whatsoever is the principle of its specific being. For this reason man’s beatitude consists, both chiefly and in point of origin, in goods of the soul. For just as the body is for the sake of the soul as for an end, and matter for the sake of form, as is clear from *On the Soul* 2,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 2, ch. 4, 415b15–20. so too goods of the body are ordered to goods of the soul as to an end; consequently, beatitude cannot chiefly consist in goods of the body.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that *beatitude is in the will* can be understood in two ways. In one way, it can be understood to mean that beatitude is the will’s object; and this way beatitude—since it is indeed the ultimate end, and the notion of the good is gathered from the end, which is the will’s object—must be held to be in the will. In another way, it can be understood to mean that beatitude is an act of the will; and in this way, beatitude cannot be in the will, for beatitude implies man’s ultimate end. Now man’s ultimate end can be taken in two ways: an end within himself, and an end outside himself. Within him, as a thing’s activity is said to be its end, since every thing exists for the sake of its activity. On the other hand, the end outside him is what he attains through his activity.  

Now, it is not just any activity that can be called an end, but that one which first unites the agent to the end outside of it; and I say this is so whenever a thing has its end outside itself, for then it is necessary that the end within be ordered to the end outside, so that the end outside is as the ultimate end; and the end within, which is activity, is ordered to that, even as we see that natural things, through the activities they have, attain (according to a certain assimilation) to the divine goodness, which is their end outside.  

But it is not possible that the very action of the will be anything’s ultimate end, for since the will’s object is the end, this very action (namely, to will), and any other act of will, is nothing other than to order some things to an end; therefore it presupposes another end. Consequently, if the willing itself be said to be willed, one must presuppose that something is willed before this. For one cannot understand there to be in any power reflection upon its own act unless its own act, in which this reflection happens, has first found its terminus in its proper object, which is something else than the very act of that power; otherwise it would be necessary to proceed to infinity. For if the intellect understands itself to understand, it must understand itself to understand something; and if you say that it understands itself to understand the act of understanding itself, it will be necessary to posit still another act of the same sort, and so on to infinity. It is evident, therefore, that the very act of understanding cannot be the intellect’s first object, and for the same reason, neither can the very act of willing be the will’s first object. Since, therefore, the will’s first object is the ultimate end, it is impossible that any act of will be the will’s ultimate end.  

Nor can it even be said that the attainment of the end outside is directly through an act of will; for there is understood to be an act of will before the attainment of the end, as a certain motion toward the end, and an act of will after the attainment, as a certain resting in the end. But the only way the will can now be at rest in the end toward which it was previously tending is that the one willing has a different relation to the end than he previously did, or vice versa.i.e., the end changes relative to the will. Therefore, what causes the person willing to relate to the end in such a way that the will rests in it is the ultimate end within, which first brings about union with the end outside—for example, if someone’s end outside is money, the end within will be possession of money, through which the man stands related to the money such that his will is at rest in it. Since, therefore, the ultimate end, quasi-external, of the human will is God, no act of will can be the end within; but what will be the ultimate end within is that act by which a man first stands related to God in such a way that the will is at rest in him. But this is the vision of God according to intellect, because through this vision there comes to be a sort of quasi-contact between God and the intellect—since every object of knowledge is in the knower insofar as it is known—[and thus it brings the will to rest in him,] even as physical contact with a pleasant corporeal thing brings desires to rest. And therefore man’s ultimate end is in an act of the intellect; and so beatitude, which is man’s ultimate end, consists in the intellect.  

Nevertheless, that which is on the side of the will—namely, its being at rest in the end, which can be called delight—is a quasi-formal completion of the notion of beatitude, as being something additional to, yet consequent upon, vision, in which the substance of beatitude consists; thus to the will is attributed both the first stance toward the end, insofar as it desires the attainment of the end, and the last, insofar as it is now at rest in the end attained.  

Reply Obj. 1: This argument shows that beatitude is in the will as the will’s object, but not as if beatitude were an activity of will.  

Reply Obj. 2: Delight is required for beatitude as a quasi-form that completes beatitude, because *delight perfects activity as a sort of supervening end, like the beauty that lies over young people*, and which adorns their youthfulness, as is said in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b31–33.  

Reply Obj. 3: Charity is said to be a virtue higher than others in the wayfaring state to the extent that it is charity that orders all of them to God; and also, the ultimate resting in God in heaven will belong to charity. Nevertheless, this fact does not make charity’s act the very substance of beatitude, but either a certain inclination to beatitude, as in the wayfaring state, or the resting in beatitude, as in the heavenly state.  

Reply Obj. 4: An action of the body cannot reach the level of attaining a good of the soul, but the act of intellect can reach the level of attaining the good which the will desires; and therefore it is not the same.  

Reply Obj. 5: Man is more perfectly united to God through affection than through intellect, inasmuch as the union that occurs through affection comes as an addition to the perfect union that occurs through intellect, perfecting it and adorning it; yet nevertheless the first union must always be through intellect. Nonetheless, love inclines one to seek the perfect union before intellect perfectly unites, though not before intellect knows the union in some way, because one cannot desire something entirely unknown. It is in this sense that Hugh says *knowledge remains outside, where love enters in*.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said, as is clear from what was said just above, that man’s beatitude resides in an act of intellect because through this act is the first union of man to his ultimate end outside himself, which the intellect is joined to insofar as it knows it. Now, what is known by the practical intellect cannot be the ultimate end outside man because the knowledge of the practical intellect is ordered to what it knows as cause to effect. The effect of something cannot be its ultimate end, however, because the perfection of a cause does not depend on its effect, but vice versa. And so, beatitude cannot consist in an act of the practical intellect, but only in an act of the speculative intellect, This is why all practical knowledge is pursued for the sake of some other desirable thing, whereas speculative knowledge is desired rather for its own sake.  

Reply Obj. 1: The good that the speculative intellect is joined to by knowledge is more common than the good that the practical intellect is joined to, insofar as the speculative intellect is more separated from the particular than the practical intellect, whose knowledge is perfected in activity, which has to do with singulars. But it is true that the attainment of the end, which the speculative intellect arrives at, is, as such,i.e., as that act of knowing by which the intellect is united to the object that it knows. proper to the one attaining it, whereas the attainment of the end intended by the practical intellect can be proper as well as common, inasmuch as someone by practical intellect directs both himself and others to an end, as is evident in the governor of a multitude. But from the simple fact that someone contemplates, he directs himself individually to the end of his contemplation. However, the end of the speculative intellect itself so far surpasses the good of the practical intellect that its individual attainment exceeds the common attainment of the good of the practical intellect; and therefore the most perfect beatitude consists in the speculative intellect.  

Reply Obj. 2: It is more perfect to have a perfection and to pour it from oneself into another than only to have it in oneself. For the comparison of these perfections cannot be understood in another way, because something cannot have the perfection of being a cause unless at the same time it has in itself that perfection of which it is the cause. It happens, however, that something is of greater perfection insofar as it is perfect in itself than according to its being a cause of another, when it either does not communicate to another the same perfection that it has, or communicates the same perfection but not to the same extent as it has it, just as the perfection in God as he exists in himself, is considered greater than his perfection as he is the cause of others; and so, the perfection of the contemplator, inasmuch as he is perfect in himself in contemplating, is greater than that of a craftsman inasmuch as he makes a knife. But the one who, by his contemplating, would establish other contemplatives equal to himself, would be more perfect than someone who could only contemplate by himself alone.  

Reply Obj. 3: An end and the things ordered to an end do not need to be alike as if they were of a single genus; but there must be between them a conformity of proportion, such that the things ordered to an end be proportioned to leading one into the end; and in this way the virtues are conformed to beatitude, since they lead into beatitude by way of disposition and merit.  

Reply Obj. 4: The speculative intellect is nobler, simply speaking, than the practical, since it exists for its own sake, whereas the practical is for the sake of its work; nor does the practical intellect set down a law for it, for the speculative intellect is not directed in judging the truth of things according to some law set down by the practical intellect; but the practical intellect sets down a law for the sake of the speculative intellect, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 6,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 6, ch. 1, 1138b26–28. when it ordains how someone ought to arrive at the perfection of the speculative intellect; and by this very fact, the practical intellect is shown to be at the assistance of the speculative.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said that, as in natural things motion is related to its terminus, so in voluntary things appetite is related to its end, and things that are sought on account of an end are related to the attainment of the end. And so, just as when a natural thing arrives at its terminus, its motion ceases, so too with the will: when it has what it seeks, its desire ceases, having been converted into love or delight. Therefore, since it is the end to which all desires are referred, beatitude must be the kind of thing that, when one has it, nothing further remains to be desired. Now, everyone naturally desires to exist and to persist in the good that he has; and so everyone holds that beatitude is the kind of thing that unmoveably and perpetually.  

But people have reached various conclusions about the immobility and perpetuity of human beatitude. For some said that absolute perpetuity was not included in the nature of human beatitude, but only a perpetuity with respect to man’s life. Nor should this perpetuity be considered as the immobility that takes away potentiality for being changed, but only that which takes away the act of being changed. And this was the opinion of Plato, as is touched upon in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 10, 1100a10ff. For he argued that the man is blessed in this life, whose beatitude continued all the way to his death. But because the condition of a man, no matter how perfect, can be changed in this life, and we cannot have sure judgment of future contingents, therefore we cannot know of any man before his death whether his perfection would continue all the way to his death, but at the end of his life one would be able to know if it had been continued even to the end; and so, no one can be called blessed until his death.  

However, the Philosopher disproves this position,Ibid. because it is unfitting to argue that someone ought to be called blessed when he no longer exists and that he cannot be called blessed when he does exist. Therefore, if beatitude is in this life, if someone is blessed, he is blessed while he is alive; but when he is dead, he will not be blessed; and thus he is more able to be called blessed when alive than when dead. Hence the Philosopher in the same passage states a different opinion concerning beatitude or happiness—namely that beatitude, in its perfect notion, has absolute perpetuity and immobility. But it is impossible for man to possess beatitude according to its perfect notion; what is possible is for a man to have some participation of it, however little, and owing to this participation, to be called blessed; and so, a blessed man need not be perpetually and immutably blessed, simply speaking, but only in keeping with the condition of human nature; this is why he adds: *blessed, however, as men*. Such immutability belongs to a man when the habits of virtues are firmly rooted in him, so that he cannot easily be deflected from acting virtuously.  

But that position, too, does not seem to be reasonable. For it is commonly held by everyone that happiness, or beatitude, is a good of the rational or intellectual nature; and so where the rational or intellectual nature is found in its essence, and not only by participation, one must also posit beatitude in its essence, and not by participation. And so, since in man not only is there some overflow of intellect—as in the brute animals there is a certain overflow of reason inasmuch as they participate somewhat in prudence, as appears in their instincts —but there is also in him reason or intellect in its essence, it must be posited that at some time he can attain true beatitude, and not merely a participation of beatitude; otherwise the natural appetite of the intellectual nature that is in man would be frustrated. Now true beatitude cannot be posited in this life on account of the various inconstancies man is subject to; and so it is necessary for the beatitude that is the end of human life to be had after this life. And this, indeed, all philosophers conceded who held that the soul, which is the form of the human body, is an intellect in its essence; for they posited an immortal soul. But those who held that the soul, which is the form of the human body, is not an intellect in its essence, but rather a reflection of an intellect that is separate, and one common to all, hold that the soul, which is the form of the body, is corruptible, and does not arrive at perfect beatitude, but only to a participation of beatitude, of such a sort as was spoken of above. But this opinion is absurd, as was demonstrated in Book II, Distinction 17, Question 2, Article 1. And therefore we concede that man’s true beatitude, simply speaking, is after this life. Nevertheless, we do not deny that there can be some participation of beatitude in this life, according as man is perfect in the goods of reason—chiefly of speculative reason, secondarily of practical reason. It is about this earthly happiness that the Philosopher reaches his conclusions in the book of *Ethics*, neither asserting nor denying another happiness that is after this life.  

Reply Obj. 1: As was said in Book III,See *In III Sent*. d. 34, q. 1, a. 4. the *beatitudes* in that passage designate acts of perfect virtues, according to which man has a certain participation and likeness of future beatitude, which is true beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 2: Those philosophers who, speaking of beatitude, did not posit a future life were not speaking of true beatitude, but of a participation in beatitude, as was said.  

Reply Obj. 3: The end of human life surpasses the end of the other animals’ life more than the one life surpasses the other life; and the life of man is nobler by the very fact that it is ordered to a nobler end. Hence human life need not include its ultimate end, as the life of other animals includes their end.  

Reply Obj. 4: Although man naturally desires the end, nevertheless he does not naturally desire those things that lead to the end; but he desires these by rational appetite, by deliberating about them and choosing them. Nor is it unfitting for something in itself worthy of hatred to be desirable with a view to an end, even as the amputation of bodily members is desirable on account of health;When it can only be preserved by that means. and in this way death, from which everyone naturally flees, is desirable on account of beatitude, as is said in Philippians 1:23: *my desire is to depart and be with Christ*.  

Article 2  

***What beatitude is***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the second question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 1. It seems that beatitude is something uncreated. First of all, because of what Boethius says in *On the Consolation of Philosophy* 3,Boethius, *On the Consolation of Philosophy*, Bk. 3, Prose 10 (CCSL 94:54). that beatitude is divinity itself; and for this reason he concludes that every blessed person is a god.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, whatever is said by way of superabundance applies only in one case. But God is the highest good, since he is the cause of all goodness; beatitude is also the highest good, since it is desired by all as the ultimate end. Therefore, beatitude is God himself alone.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, what is desirable for its own sake has the notion of the enjoyable, as is evident from Augustine. But beatitude is appetible for its own sake, as is evident from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7, 1097a36. Therefore beatitude is to be enjoyed. But God alone is to be enjoyed, as is evident from what Augustine says in *On Christian Doctrine* 1.Augustine, *On Christian Doctrine*, Bk. 1, ch. 5 (CCSL 32:9). Therefore, God alone is beatitude.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, it belongs to the notion of beatitude that there be sufficiency in it. But in God alone does human desire find sufficiency. For this reason Augustine says in the *Confessions*: *our heart is restless until it comes to thee*.*Inquietum est cor nostrum donec perveniat ad te*—a slight misquotation of Augustine’s famous sentence, which ends: *donec requiescat in te* (*Confessions*, Bk. 1, n. 1 [CCSL 27:1]). Aquinas gets the line right in five other places in his writings where he quotes it, making it plausible that we are dealing here with a miscopy that a future critical edition will set to rights. Therefore, God alone is beatitude itself.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, everything that is in us is in us either essentially or accidentally. But beatitude is not of the essence of man, for in that case, every man would be blessed, and all the time. Therefore, if it is something in man, it will be an accident. But an accident cannot be the end of a substance, since substance is nobler than accident. Therefore, it is impossible that beatitude be something in us; and so it will be something uncreated.  

On the contrary (1), just as by participation in justice men are called just, so by participation in beatitude men are called blessed, as is evident from Boethius in *On the Consolation of Philosophy* 3.Boethius, *On the Consolation of Philosophy*, Bk. 3, Prose 10 (CCSL 94:54). But justice is something created in us; therefore, beatitude is as well.  

Furthermore (2), happiness with the addition of a number of goods is more choiceworthy than happiness alone, as is evident from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7, 1097b16–19. This passage of Aristotle could be interpreted—and with greater plausibility—to be saying the opposite of what St. Thomas says here. Cf. *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 2, 1172b27–35, and *Magna Moralia*, Bk. 1, chs. 2–4. But no matter how much is added to God, he is no more choiceworthy. Therefore, happiness is something other than God; and so it is something created in us.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that happiness is not an act.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 2 and q. 55, a. 2, ad 3; *SCG* I, ch. 100; *Sent. Eth*. I, lec. 10; *In Metaphys*. IX, ch. 8. For, according to Boethius in *On the Consolation of Philosophy* III,Boethius, *On the Consolation of Philosophy*, Bk. 3, Prose 2 (CCSL 94:38). *beatitude is a state made perfect by the gathering together of all goods*. But ‘state’ does not name an act. Therefore, beatitude is not an act.  

Obj. 2: Further, we are the cause of our own acts. If, therefore, beatitude consisted in an act of ours, we ourselves would be the cause of our own beatitude, which is absurd.  

Obj. 3: Further, if anything in us is the ultimate end, it will be especially what is never sought on account of anything else. But delight is not sought on account of another, but only on account of itself. Therefore beatitude consists most of all in delight. But delight is not an act, but a passion. Therefore, happiness is not an act.  

Obj. 4: Further, there can be different acts arising from one agent and according to a single habit. Therefore, if beatitude were an act, the same blessed person would have many different beatitudes, which is absurd.  

Obj. 5: Further, the beatitude of the wayfaring state is a certain likeness of the beatitude of heaven. But the beatitude of the wayfaring state cannot be called an act, for if that were so, those who are sleeping would lose their beatitude. Therefore, the beatitude of heaven is not an act.  

On the contrary (1), the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 1Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7 (1098a16–18) and ch. 13 (1102a5). that happiness is an activity according to perfect virtue.  

Furthermore (2), whatever a thing exists for is its ultimate end. But every thing exists for the sake of its own activity, as is said in *On the Heavens* 2.Cf. Aristotle, *On the Heavens*, Bk. 2, ch. 3, 286a8: “Everything which has a function exists for its function.” Therefore, the activity proper to man is his ultimate end; and so his beatitude is a certain act.  

Furthermore (3), a man is blessed through arriving at the greatest likeness to God. But man is most likened to God by actually acting, because in this way he is maximally in act and removed from potentiality. Therefore, man’s beatitude consists in an activity of his.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that man’s beatitude is not the same thing as eternal life.Parallel: cf. *ST* I, q. 18, a. 2, esp. ad 2. For as is said in *On the Soul* 2,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 2, ch. 4, 415b13. *for living things, to live is to be*. But beatitude, according to the Philosopher, does not consist in mere being, but in activity. Therefore, man’s beatitude is not a *life*.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, the notion of life consists in motion, as is evident from the Commentator on the *Book of Causes*. But beatitude includes in its notion unchangeability. Therefore, beatitude is not the same thing as eternal life.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, beatitude is something communicable to man. But eternity of life is not something that can be communicated to him, since what is made in time is not capable of being eternal. Therefore, eternal life is not the same thing as beatitude.  

Obj. 4: Further, whatsoever endures perpetually participates, in a way, in eternity of life, if ‘eternal’ be taken in a broad sense to mean ‘perpetual.’ But the damned endure perpetually, sent into eternal fire, as is evident from Matthew 25:41. However, they have not beatitude, but the highest misery. Therefore, beatitude and eternal life are not the same thing.  

On the contrary (1), whatever is the end of human life is beatitude. But eternal life is the end of human life, as is evident from Romans 6:22: *the return you get is sanctification and its end, eternal life*. Therefore, beatitude is eternal life.  

Furthermore (2), man’s ultimate beatitude consists in the vision of God, as is evident from what was said above. But this is what eternal life consists in, as is evident from John 17:3: *this is eternal life, that they know thee the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom thou hast sent*. Therefore, beatitude is the same thing as eternal life.  

Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that beatitude is the same thing as peace.There are several texts on peace that could be listed as partial parallels, e.g., *ST* II-II, q. 29, a. 2, ad 4; *Super Ioan*. 14, lec. 7; *Super Phil*. 4, lec. 1. First of all, because of what is said in Psalm 147:14: *he establishes peace in your borders*.Ps. 147:3, iuxta Vulg. The Psalmist’s mention of *fines* (borders, boundaries) allows for reading the verse to be saying: God has established peace as the “end” of the city. Since the end is already known to be beatitude, peace must be referring to the same thing. But the end of the city of God, which is being spoken of there, is beatitude. Therefore, beatitude is the same thing as peace.  

Obj. 2: Further, Augustine says, in the *City of God* 19: *we may say that the end of our goods is peace, just as we may say that it is eternal life*.Augustine, *The City of God*, Bk. 19, ch. 11 (CCSL 48:674). But eternal life is the same thing as beatitude. Therefore, so is peace.  

Obj. 3: Further, what is naturally desired by all and for the sake of which other things are done would seem to be man’s beatitude. But all desire peace, and do whatever they do for its sake, as Augustine says,As there are many places where Augustine says that all things desire peace, it is by no means clear if Thomas has a specific text in mind. and Dionysius.See Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 11 (PG 3:947ff.). Therefore, peace is beatitude itself.  

Obj. 4: Further, peace signifies a certain rest. But the end of every change is rest, as can be shown by examples among natural things. Therefore, peace is the end of the whole of our changeable life; and so peace is the same thing as beatitude.  

On the contrary (1), according to the Philosopher,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 7 (1098a16–18) and ch. 13 (1102a5). beatitude is a certain activity. But peace does not name an act, but only immobility. Therefore, peace is not the same thing as beatitude.  

Furthermore (2), peace refers most of all to appetite or the will, because it is a certain resting thereof. But beatitude resides especially in the intellect, as was said above. Therefore, beatitude is not the same thing as peace.  

Quaestiuncula 5  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that beatitude is not the same thing as the kingdom of God.There is no exact parallel to this article. There are, of course, many discussions in Thomas’s writings of the political concepts he employs here. For ‘kingdom,’ which is named from the rule of a king, seems to pertain to the governing that provides for things. But providence consists in the coordination of things that are directed toward the end.And so, does not bear upon the end as such. Therefore, ‘kingdom of God’ does not seem to pertain to beatitude, which is the ultimate end.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 12, n. 2 (PG 3:970). says that *the kingdom is the distribution of every end and law and order*. But this distribution pertains not only to men but also to all created beings to which God distributes these things, whereas beatitude belongs solely to rational creatures. Therefore, beatitude is not the same thing as the kingdom of God.  

Obj. 3: Further, ‘kingdom’ only implies a perfection in the person who rules. But there is only one who stands at the head in the ruling of a kingdom, as is evident from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 8.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 8, ch. 10, 1160b1. Therefore, the kingdom of God only designates a perfection in God himself. But beatitude names a perfection not only in God, but in all the blessed. Therefore, beatitude is not the same thing as the kingdom of God.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, beatitude does not suffer the admixture of any evil. But in the kingdom of God some evils can be present; hence it is said in Matthew 13:41 that at the end of the world the angels will gather up all scandals from the kingdom of God. Therefore, the kingdom of God is not beatitude itself.  

On the contrary (1), is what Augustine says in Sermon 22 *To the Fathers in the Desert*Pseudo-Augustine, *Sermons to the Fathers in the Desert*, Serm. 22 (PL 40:1272); cf. *On the Sermon on the Mount*, Bk. 2, ch. 10 (CCSL 35:126). that the first three petitions in the Lord’s Prayer pertain to future beatitude. Now, one of these is *thy kingdom come*. Therefore, the kingdom of God pertains to beatitude.  

Furthermore (2), beatitude is what is promised to the saints as their reward. But the kingdom of God is so promised, as is clear from Matthew 5:10: *blessed are those who are persecuted for righteousness’ sake, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven*. Therefore, the kingdom of heaven is beatitude itself.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, the good that all long for is being, as is evident from Boethius in *On the Consolation of Philosophy*.Boethius, *On the Consolation of Philosophy*, Bk. 3, Prose 11 (CCSL 94:58–59).  Therefore, the ultimate object of desire for all things is perfect being, to the extent that it is possible in their nature. Now, everything that has being from another has the perfection of its being from another, since the more truly any thing is connected to the principle of its being, the more perfectly it receives being. Hence, lower bodies, owing to their great distance from the first principle, have corruptible being, as is clear from *On Generation and Corruption* 2.Aristotle, *On Generation and Corruption*, Bk. 2, ch. 10, 336b30. And so everything that has being from another has two kinds of ultimate end: one outside itself, namely that which is the principle of the desired perfection; another within itself, namely its very own perfection, which is brought about by union to its principle. Thus, since beatitude is man’s ultimate end, there will be two kinds of beatitude: one that is within man himself, namely, that ultimate perfection of himself at which it is possible for him to arrive, and this is created beatitude. But the other is outside himself, and by his union with it, the created beatitude is caused in him; and this is uncreated beatitude, which is God himself.  

Reply Obj. 1: Just as God himself is good by his essence, whereas all other things are goods by participation, as is said in the book *On the Hebdomads*,Boethius, *On the Hebdomads* (PL 64:1311–14). so too, God himself alone is beatitude by his essence, whereas others are blessed by participation. For this reason, created beatitude is a certain participation and likeness of uncreated beatitude. Yet it is not beatitude essentially, as if making men blessed by its essence; and thus is to be understood the citation from Boethius.  

Reply Obj. 2: Created beatitude is not the highest good simply, but the highest good among human goods; but God is the highest good simply, and so the argument does not follow.  

Reply Obj. 3: There are two ways something can be an object of love. An object can be loved in the manner of benevolence, when we will someone’s good on account of himself, as we love our friends, even if nothing should come to us from them. Otherwise, an object can be loved with the love of concupiscence; and this is either a good that is within us, or because something becomes a good for us from it, as we love pleasure, or wine inasmuch as it produces pleasure. Now whatever is loved with a love of concupiscence cannot be the ultimate object of love, since it is referred to the good of another (namely, the person who has concupiscence for it). But whatever is loved by a love of benevolence can be the ultimate object of love. Therefore, the created beatitude that is within us is loved only by a love of concupiscence; and so we refer the love of it to ourselves, and consequently we refer it to God, since we ought to refer even ourselves to God; and thus, it cannot be the ultimate object of love. Nevertheless, it is the ultimate object of concupiscence by the very fact that it is the greatest good that comes to us from union with God, and so it is said to be sought for its own sake or desired for its own sake; for either term indicates something ultimate among the things that are loved with a love of concupiscence. For although God himself is desired with a love of concupiscence, it is the same to desire God with a love of concupiscence and to desire the greatest of goods that come to us from God, just as it is the same to desire wine with a love of concupiscence and to desire the effect of wine in us, say, pleasure.  

Reply Obj. 4: In God alone is found sufficiency as in the principle of every good that the appetite seeks; but in those to whom it is granted by God to have all goods sufficiently, a sufficiency is found by the divine gift.  

Reply Obj. 5: Although every accident as such is less noble than a substance, nevertheless an accident can be worthier than a substance in some certain respect. For an accident may be considered in two ways. In one way, as it inheres in a subject, from which it derives the notion of being an accident; and in this way, every accident is less worthy than a substance. In another way, it may be considered in its order to something outside the subject; and in this way a certain accident can be nobler than a substance, inasmuch as through it a substance is joined to something nobler than itself; and this is how created beatitude, and grace, and other such things, are something nobler than the nature of the soul in which they inhere. There is also another consideration according to which an accident is nobler, namely, inasmuch as a substance is compared to an accident inhering in it as potency to act.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that, as is evident from what was said before, a thing’s ultimate end, taken as existing in the thing itself, is what joins a thing to its external end, which is its principle of perfection. Now things can be joined to God, who is their ultimate end, in two ways. In one way, by way of likening, so that a thing is said to be most of all joined to God when it is most of all like God; and in this regard, each thing’s ultimate end must be that in it according to which it is most likened to God. But each thing approaches likeness to God just insofar as it is in act, whereas it departs from that likeness insofar as it is in potency; and so that through which a thing is most in act is its ultimate end. In another way, things can be joined to God by reaching God himself—a union that, in fact, is possible only to the rational creature, who is able to be joined to God himself by knowledge and by love, precisely because God is the object of his activity but is not the object of any non-rational creature’s activity.  

Now, no matter how one considers man’s ultimate perfection, which is his end, it must be located in the genus of act. For if we consider the manner of union with God that is common to all created things, since a thing is more in act insofar as it is acting than insofar as it is merely capable of acting, the ultimate perfection of each and every thing will be its perfect activity; hence, a thing is said to exist for the sake of its activity. Likewise, if we consider the union proper to the rational creature, man’s ultimate perfection consists in activity, for a habit is only united to its object through the mediation of an act; and therefore beatitude must be located in the genus of act. Nevertheless, this second consideration bears upon the consideration of beatitude more closely than the first, since beatitude belongs only to the rational creature.  

Reply Obj. 1: In every order, all the things that are ordered toward an end work together for the ultimate end; hence, since the perfect activity of man is his ultimate end (as is the case with any given thing), all human goods work together for his perfect activity: for example, the habits by which his acts are informed work together for the perfect activity of man in this life, and other natural goods work together for the end as principles of acts, and external goods as instrumentally necessary for perfect activity. Thus, therefore, the collection of all goods is considered to be beatitude insofar as all goods collected together are at the service of the most perfect activity of man, which is essentially beatitude itself. All the goods named, too, are ordered to heavenly beatitude, inasmuch as we merit through their proper use; and while not all of them, endure in beatitude in themselves, nevertheless they remain in those perfections that replace them, just as vision replaces faith and a sufficiency of eternal goods takes the place of temporal riches.  

Reply Obj. 2: In an act two things are to be considered: the substance of the act and its form, from which an act has perfection. Therefore, as regards the substance of an act, its principle is a natural potency; but as regards its form, its principle is the habit from which the act proceeds. If, therefore, that habit is an acquired one, we will be entirely the cause of our act; if, on the other hand, that habit is an infused one, the perfection of the act will be from the external cause that causes the habit. Now, an act of ours is not held to be our beatitude except by reason of its perfection, due to which it is most nobly joined to the end outside; and thus not we, but God is the cause of our beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 3: There are two kinds of delight. One precedes the attainment of an end; and this is capable of being ordered to something else, for it can be ordered to perfect activity, insofar as we undertake more diligently what we delight in. The other delight is what follows upon the attainment of an end; and this is brought about by the activity that joins the agent to his end. Thus this latter delight is not beatitude itself, but a certain perfection and form of beatitude,“A form of beatitude” in the sense of something completive, something that fills or rounds out the good of beatitude the essence of which consists, absolutely speaking, in a higher act of a nobler power. as was said above.  

Reply Obj. 4: A multitude of acts proceeding from the same habit with respect to the same object is only caused by the interruptions of time; and so in perfect beatitude, where there will be no interruptions but a continuous activity, there will be only a single beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 5: As was said above, the beatitude of the wayfaring state is not perfect beatitude but a certain participation of it; and so it is not always in act, but sometimes is merely in habit. Hence, a person asleep cannot be called blessed as regards that which he has right at that moment, but only insofar as the activity itself is in him habitually. For in the way in which effects are present in their causes, so activities are present in their habits.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that ‘life’ may be said in two ways. In one way, as meaning the very being of living things; since, as is said in *On the Soul* 2,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 2, ch. 4, 415b13. *for living things, to live is to be*. Now, something is said to be alive from the fact that it is able to move itself according to some action; hence, plants are said to live from the fact that they move themselves in regard to increase; and animals still more, in that they move themselves in regard to place and move themselves to sensing; and in man yet more, in that he is able to move himself to willing and to understanding. However, the perfection of any power is its act; thus, in the second place, the name ‘life’ is carried over to signify an activity to which something moves itself, as sensing is called animal life, and understanding is called human life; and according to this way of speaking, each thing considers its life that activity which it most of all intends, as if its entire being were ordered to that activity; and it is in keeping with this way of speaking that people say: “so-and-so leads such-and-such a life;” and thus it was that the Epicureans held beatitude to be a life of pleasures. Now, activity is not subject to the measure of time except insofar as it is bound up with motion, which is why activities that are not connected with motion but with the terminus of motion are not measured by time but in an instant, as is evident with illumination. And so, if there is an activity that altogether transcends motion, such an activity will not be measured by time, but by a measure above time.  

Now, the vision of God, which is held to be man’s beatitude, cannot be an action measured by time in and of itself, since it is not successive. Nor can it be measured by time on the part of the one seeing or what is seen, since both are outside motion. For this reason, it can be measured neither by time nor by the instant, which is the terminus of time. It cannot even be measured by an age,An “age” is an intermediate measure between time and eternity. For the full meaning of the term, see Book II, Dist. 2, Q. 2. for an age, insofar as it is distinguished from eternity, pertains to immutable creatures, whereas this vision exceeds the natural power of created things, since no created being by its natural endowments is capable of arriving at it; and therefore its proper measure is eternity itself. And so, the vision of God, which is beatitude itself, is eternal life itself.  

To the first, therefore, the response is evident from what has been said.  

Reply Obj. 2: Although life is first recognized by motion, since it is through motion that the living are first distinguished from the non-living, nevertheless the name ‘life’ is extended further to all activities that are not from some external mover, as willing, understanding, and other such things. This is why the name ‘motion,’ too, gets carried over to such activities, as when we call understanding a ‘movement of the intellect’ or willing a ‘movement of the will.’  

Reply Obj. 3: Eternity is not communicable to man in such a way that it becomes an adequate measure of him, or of something within him, as the argument would prove. Still, it can be communicated to man according to a certain participation, so that, just as man is made a partaker of divine activity in seeing God, so he is made a partaker of eternity, by which the divine activity is measured; and it is thus that man’s very activity can be called eternal life.  

Reply Obj. 4: The eternal life the saints will have is spoken of according to a participation of eternity, not only in the fact that it will not end (in which manner even the punishment of the damned is called ‘eternal’), but further with regard to the removal of all change, not only in act, which an ‘age’ too excludes, but even in potency. For by clinging to God, the saints will obtain such stability from the divine gift that they cannot be changed—a stability God has by his nature, by reason of which he is eternal.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said, as Augustine says in Book 19 of the *City of God*,Augustine, *The City of God*, Bk. 19, ch. 13 (CCSL 48:679). that *the peace of all things is the tranquillity of order*—from which it is clear that the notion of peace is taken from the fact that nothing impedes the right order; for tranquillity excludes the disturbance of an impediment. Now, peace especially regards that order by which the will is ordered to something. For this reason, we say that a man has peace only when there is nothing that impedes the order of his will to something he wants; and in like manner, we say that a city has peace when there is nothing that disturbs the city’s right order, which is from the will of the city’s governor; and to the extent that even inanimate things are said to have natural desire, we say that a given thing has peace when it is not impeded from following the inclination that it has to its end, which it naturally desires. Now, the activity in which beatitude consists must be an unimpeded activity; for any impediment would detract from its perfection. Hence delight, too, which follows upon perfect activity, is caused by an unimpeded activity, as is said in the *Ethics* 7;Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12, 1153a13–15. and thus peace is required for beatitude—not that it is the very essence of beatitude, but it is like a removal of whatever could impede beatitude. Now, from the very fact that one desires something, one’s appetite shuns its contrary; consequently, from the very fact that all naturally desire beatitude, they also naturally flee from impediments to beatitude, and this is what it means for them naturally to desire peace.  

Reply Obj. 1: Peace is said to be the end of the city of God in the sense of being the nearest disposition to the end, which is inseparable from the end itself; and not as if it were the end *per se*.  

To the second the response is the same.  

Reply Obj. 3: All desire peace, not as an end, but as that without which the end cannot be possessed.  

Reply Obj. 4: A thing’s ultimate end is not rest, but the thing’s activity, and this activity cannot be perfect unless the thing itself be perfect. Motion, however, is the act of an imperfect thing; and accordingly, rest, which motion terminates in, is nearer the end than motion itself. And thus, motion is ordered to rest as to an end.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 5  

To the fifth question, it should be said that the name ‘kingdom’ is taken from the rule exercised by a king. But ruling is an act of providence, which is why someone is said to have a kingdom when he has others under his providence. Thus, men are said to be ‘in the kingdom of God’ when they are perfectly subject to his providence. Now, it belongs to providence to order things to an end. But the things that are ordered to an end are both those that are distant from the end, insofar as they are led to it, and the things that have already attained the end, insofar as they are preserved in its possession. And since the end is the rule of providence, those are under providence perfectly who are already stationed in the very end; and therefore among them nothing can be foreign to the order of providence. Others, however, who stand at some distance from the end, are under divine providence more or less perfectly to the extent that they are nearer or further from the end; and those are nearest to the end who are on the way to the end. Now the way of arriving at the end is *faith working through love*.An indirect citation of Gal 5:6.  

For this reason ‘kingdom of God’ is said antonomastically, in two ways: at times to mean the congregation of those who walk by faith, and in this way the church militant is called the kingdom of God; at other times to mean the company of those who are already established in the end, and in this way the church triumphant is called the kingdom of God. And according to this latter meaning, to be in the kingdom of God is the same thing as to be in beatitude. Nor is there any difference between the kingdom of God and beatitude taken this way, except as the common good of the whole multitude is distinguished from the singular good of each and every member.  

Reply Obj. 1: Beatitude is under providence not as if it were ordered by providence to some end, but because other things are ordered by providence to it.viz., beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 2: Dionysius sufficiently deals with the things that are required for a kingdom in the sense of a multitude governed by providence. For this activity, three things are required: namely an end, order to an end, and a rule of order. Now a rule of order exists in two ways: one in the person ordering (and this is law, from which the rectitude of order proceeds); the other in the one who is ordered, which makes him keep to the rectitude of order (and it is this rule that causes ordering to an end, whether the rule in question be a form, a virtue, or something else of the sort; and this is what Dionysius terms *adornment*). But although these four can pertain to all creation, since all things are ordered by God to an end according to the law of eternal wisdom, and to all things are given powers by which they tend to an end, still, in a special way, they pertain to rational creatures, who are made to arrive at the ultimate end itself in the most noble manner, and who know the notion of order and of end.  

Reply Obj. 3: Kingdom implies perfection both in the one who sits at the head, and in the one who is subject. But it exists in the one who sits at the head as in the principle of perfection, because he imparts his rectitude to others; while it exists in the subject as one receiving perfection from another. However, the saints who are in heaven are subject to divine providence in this way: they are made providers either for themselves or even for others, inasmuch as all other things will be subject to them. And so they pertain to the kingdom of God not only as being under a king, but also as being kings, who yet will be under the one supreme king. This is why it says in Revelation 5:10: *thou hast made them a kingdom and priests to our God, and they shall reign on earth*.  

Reply Obj. 4: In this passage ‘kingdom of God’ refers to the church militant, not to the church triumphant.  

Article 3  

***Whether all desire beatitude***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the third question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I-II, q. 5, a. 8. It seems that not all desire beatitude.We have chosen to render *appetunt* as “desire” for the sake of elegance of language. However, this translation should not be taken as implying that St. Thomas is talking about emotions. The “appetite” for beatitude is a rational appetite, the appetite that follows not on sensory perception but on the perception of reason. For no one wills what he is ignorant of, as is evident from Augustine in *On the Trinity*,Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 8, ch. 4 (CCSL 50:275). and from the Philosopher, who says in *On the Soul* 3Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 10, 433b10. that the object of will is the apprehended appetible. But not all know beatitude, since many have gone astray in regard to beatitude. Therefore, not all desire beatitude.  

Obj. 2: Further, whoever desires beatitude, desires beatitude essentially in itself. But beatitude itself is the vision of God, and not everyone desires this. Therefore, not all desire beatitude.  

Obj. 3: Further, what cannot be apprehended cannot be desired. But that two contraries should exist at the same time cannot be apprehended, as is evident from *Metaphysics* 4.Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 4, ch. 3, 1005b29. Therefore, contraries cannot at the same time be objects of appetite. But some desire things contrary to beatitude, such as those who desire sins. Therefore, not all desire beatitude.  

Obj. 4: Further, it is a sin to cling to changeable things, despising the unchangeable good. But beatitude is an unchangeable good. Therefore, a person who sins despises it; and thus, not all desire beatitude.  

On the contrary (1) is what Boethius proves in *On the Consolation of Philosophy* 3: a longing for the true good is planted in the minds of all men.In this instance the reference is indeed to the whole of Book 3 of Boethius’s *On the Consolation of Philosophy* (CCSL 94:37ff.). But the true good is beatitude. Therefore, all desire beatitude.  

Furthermore (2), to each thing its own end is appetible. But beatitude is the ultimate end of human life. Therefore, all men desire beatitude.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that someone can desire misery.Parallels: The principle that no one can turn away willingly from beatitude, or willingly desire misery, is invoked numerous times by St. Thomas. In the *Scriptum* itself, see *In II Sent*. d. 7, q. 1, a. 1, ad 1; *In II Sent*. d. 25, a. 2. For every rational power extends to opposites. But the will is a rational power. Therefore, it extends to opposites. Now, misery is what is opposed to beatitude. Therefore, if someone can desire beatitude, he can also desire misery.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, if someone is not able to want misery, it is impossible for him to want it. Therefore, it is necessary for him not to want it. But necessity implies coercion or prohibition, as Anselm says.Not Anselm, but Eadmer, in his *De similitudinibus Anselmi*, ch. 53, PL 159:631. Therefore, the will would be coerced, which is opposed to its freedom.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, as beatitude is appetible to all, so is being. But certain people want not to be, as is evident in those who kill themselves. Therefore, certain people are also able to want misery.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, the will is concerned with the end, as is said in the *Ethics* 3.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 3, ch. 2, 1111b26. But the end is the good or the apparent good, as is said in the *Physics* 2.Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 2, ch. 3, 195a25. Therefore, not only the good but also the apparent good can be desired. But what is evil can be an apparent good. Therefore, misery, no matter how evil it may be, could still be willed by someone.  

On the contrary (1), Augustine shows in *On Free Choice of the Will*,See Augustine, *On Free Choice of the Will*, Bk. 1, ch. 14 (CCSL 29:231–32). that no man is capable of wanting to be miserable.  

Furthermore (2), the intellect is related to the first intelligible as the will is related to the first appetible. But the intellect is incapable of assenting to the contrary of the first intelligible, which is that an affirmation and a denial cannot be true at the same time, as is proved in the *Metaphysics* 4.See Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 4, chs. 4–8. Therefore, the will is incapable of assenting to misery, which is contrary to its first appetible.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that no one merits by desiring beatitude.The principle used to solve the problem—namely, that since appetite for beatitude involves not only the general inclination of natural appetite but also a determinate act of rational appetite, it is therefore an act for which man is responsible, and so can be a meritorious act—is a principle of frequent occurrence in St. Thomas. It is, in some sense, reducible to the claim that whoever acts from charity gains merit, owing to the power of the divine gift already possessed in the will. For by natural endowments we do not merit. But the appetite for beatitude is natural to man, otherwise it would not be common to all. Therefore, no one merits by desiring beatitude.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, since merit and demerit concern the same thing, no one merits in regard to what he is incapable of avoiding, just as neither does he sin, as Augustine says.Augustine, *On Free Choice of the Will*, Bk. 3, ch. 18, n. 50 (CCSL 29:304). But man is not capable of not having appetite for beatitude. Therefore, no one merits by having such an appetite.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, no one can merit eternal life by his own powers, as Pelagius held. But man can desire beatitude through his own powers. Therefore, he does not merit by desiring beatitude.  

On the contrary (1), a person desires beatitude and God in the same way. If therefore no one merits by desiring beatitude, no one will merit by desiring God, which is absurd.  

Furthermore (2), charity is the principle of meriting. But charity has to do chiefly with the end. Therefore by the motion of the will toward the ultimate end we merit the most; and so we merit in desiring beatitude.
