Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that not everything that a person wills is willed for the sake of beatitude.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 1, a. 6; *ST* I, q. 60, a. 2; *SCG* I, chs. 100–101. For no one wants pleasure for the sake of anything else, as is said in the *Ethics* 7.Aristotle expressly makes this point at *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 2, 1172b20–23; perhaps Thomas confused this text with Bk. 7, ch. 12, 1153a8–12, where it is stated that some pleasures are simply ends. But pleasure is not beatitude itself, as is evident from what was said above. Therefore, not everything that someone wills does he will for the sake of beatitude.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, what can be desired for the sake of beatitude is ordainable to beatitude. But many want some things that are not ordainable to beatitude, indeed that turn one away from it, as is the case with sinners. Therefore, not everything that someone wills does he will for the sake of beatitude.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, anything that has no appearance of good in itself cannot be desired with a view to beatitude. But at times something is desired that has no appearance of good, as is evident from Augustine in his *Confessions*,Augustine, *Confessions*, Bk. 2, ch. 4 and ch. 6 (CCSL 27:22–23). where he says: *I stole what I already had enough of, and indeed of better quality; nor did I want to enjoy the thing that I sought by theft, but I sought to enjoy the very theft and sin. . . . and so I look for what delighted me in the theft; and behold, there is no appearance of anything delightful*. Therefore, something can be desired which is not desired for the sake of beatitude.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, death cannot be ordered to beatitude in any way, except perhaps with hope of a life to be gained after death, since beatitude only belongs to the living. But certain people have willed to suffer death whether at their own hand or from another, without any hope of a future life. Therefore, not everything that is willed is willed for the sake of beatitude.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, man’s will is shown to be right by the fact that it is ordered to the right end. Therefore, if whatever is desired is desired for the sake of beatitude, every human will is right—which is false.  

Obj. 6: Furthermore, whatever someone actually wills something for the sake of, must have been actually thought about. If, therefore, whatever someone wills he wills for the sake of beatitude, in every desire beatitude will have to be actually thought about—which seems to be false. Therefore, the same conclusion follows as before.  

On the contrary (1), in anything that is desired for its own sake, and never for the sake of another, the motion of desire comes to a halt. But whatever the motion of desire stops at is the ultimate end. If, therefore, something other than beatitude were desired for its own sake, and never for the sake of something else, something else than beatitude would be the ultimate end, which cannot be the case. Therefore, every other desired thing is referred to the desire for beatitude, whether mediately or immediately.  

Furthermore (2), DionysiusSee Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 11 (PG 3:947ff.). says that all things desire peace, and do whatever they do for its sake. But peace is ordered to beatitude, as is evident from what was said before. Therefore, whatever is desired is desired for the sake of beatitude.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, in every order of moving things and movers, the secondary movers must be ordered to the end of the first mover through a disposition impressed on them by the first mover, as is evident when the soul moves the hand and the hand moves a stick, and the stick strikes, which is the end intended by the soul: the stick and the hand tend to an end intended by the soul owing to the fact that the soul impresses a disposition on them mediately or immediately. But there is this difference between natural motions and violent motions: in violent motions the impression left by the first mover in the secondary movers is beside their nature, and so the activity consequent upon such an impression is difficult and laborious for them. But in natural motions, the impression left by the first mover in secondary movers is a natural cause in them, and so the activity resulting from this impression is suitable and sweet (and so it is said in Wisdom 8:1 that *God disposes all things sweetly)*. For every single thing, by the nature divinely implanted within it, tends to whatever it is ordered to by divine providence, according to the demands of the impression received. And since all things proceed from God insofar as he is good, as AugustineAugustine, *On Christian Doctrine*, Bk. 1, ch. 32 (CCSL 32:26). says, and also Dionysius,Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 4 (PG 3:694ff.). therefore all created things, according to an impression received from the Creator, are, each according to its own mode, inclined by appetite to the good, so that a certain circularity is found in things: for, having gone forth from the good, they tend toward the good.  

However, this circularity is brought to perfection in certain created beings, while in others it remains imperfect. For those creatures that are not ordered so as to attain the first good itself from which they proceeded, but merely to acquire some sort of likeness of it, do not perfectly exhibit this circularity. But only those created things that are able to attain, in some way, the first principle itself do; and this belongs solely to rational creatures, which are able to gain God himself through knowledge and love—in which gaining their beatitude consists, as is evident from what has been said. And therefore just as any other thing naturally desires its own good, so any rational creature naturally desires its own beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 1: Just as the visible is twofold—*per se*, like color, and *per accidens*, like a manWho, having some color, is thus visible.—so too the appetible, which is the object of will, can be taken in two ways: *per se* and *per accidens*. The *per se* object of will is the good; but the *per accidens* object is this or that good. And as the good taken generally is the *per se* object of will, so the highest good is the ultimate end of the will, speaking *per se*; but this or that good is designated as the ultimate end of the will and its chief object as though accidentally. Therefore, beatitude is known to all insofar as the chief *per se* object of the will lies in it, but it is not known to all as regards what is accidental to the *per se* object of the will. For all know the perfect good and desire it when they desire beatitude; but for this perfect good to be pleasure or riches, virtue, or something else, is *per accidens*; and so it is not not surprising if there are many errors concerning this matter.  

Reply Obj. 2: Although the vision of God is beatitude itself, nevertheless it does not follow that whoever desires beatitude desires the vision of God; since beatitude, as such, implies the *per se* object of will, but not the very vision of God, just as someone may desire something sweet, yet not desire honey.  

Reply Obj. 3: Although some desire things that are in reality contrary to beatitude, still, in their own estimation, these things are not contrary to it, but rather lead to it.  

Reply Obj. 4: Someone who sins by clinging to changeable goods as his end takes the changeable things themselves as if they were the foremost appetible and the perfect good; and thus he clings to them out of desire for beatitude. For as it does not follow that someone, in desiring beatitude, desires that which is in reality beatitude, so too it does not follow that someone, in despising what beatitude is according to the truth of things, despises beatitude; just as it does not follow that one who does not recognize a man does not recognize white, although the man is white.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that the activity of a secondary cause is always founded upon the activity of the first cause, and presupposes it; and so every activity of the soul must proceed on the supposition of what is implanted in the soul from the impression of the first agent, namely of God. And thus we see, on the side of the intellect, that the soul cannot proceed to understand anything except on the supposition of those things whose knowledge is innate to it; and on account of this it cannot assent to something that is contrary to these principles that it naturally knows. And it must be the same way on the side of the will. Therefore, since from the impression of the first cause, namely of God, the soul has within itself that it wills the good, and it desires the perfect good as ultimate end, it is impossible that its contrary should become an object of desire; and therefore no one is capable of wanting misery, or evil, except incidentally, which does happen whenever he desires something evil that he apprehends as a good.  

Reply Obj. 1: A rational power extends to opposites as far as things subject to it are concerned, and these are those things that are determined by the power itself. It cannot, however, extend to the opposites of those things that are determined for it by another; and so the will cannot extend to the opposite of what is determined for it by divine impression, namely to the opposite of the ultimate end. However, it can extend to opposites in regard to things it determines for itself, like things that are ordered to the ultimate end, the choice of which pertains to the will.  

Reply Obj. 2: Coercion, since it implies violence, and likewise prohibition, do not pertain to that necessity which is attendant upon a thing’s nature, since everything violent is contrary to nature; and so, since it is natural that the will is necessarily borne to beatitude, this necessity does not imply coercion in it, nor any diminishment of freedom.  

Reply Obj. 3: Nothing prevents something that is desirable in itself from being non-desirable when something else is added on to it. For this reason, even being, which is desired by all, and is good in itself, is rendered evil and worthy of hatred due to something added on to it, such as being in sorrow or misery; and so, *per accidens*, non-being is desired—not indeed, insofar as it lacks being, but insofar as it takes away that evil which was rendering being hateful. Now, to be lacking an evil is itself a good; and so he who seeks non-being, desires it as a good. But misery can never be taken as a good, because ‘misery’ names the notion of complete evil; and therefore no one can want to be miserable. In contrast, ‘non-being’ does not name the very notion of evil, but only something that is evil; and so, something added to it that has the notion of good can make it desirable, as is evident from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 9,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 9, ch. 4, 1166b11–13. and from Jerome in the gloss on Jeremiah 20, verses 14–18.Jerome, *Commentary on Jeremiah*, Bk. 4, n. 28 (CCSL 74:194–95). But what Augustine says—that no one desires non-being—is to be understood as speaking *per se*.  

Reply Obj. 4: One is incapable of judging that white is black, although one could judge that something that is white, say a man, is black; and similarly one can judge that something that is good, is an evil, but it is not possible to judge that goodness itself is badness, or that badness is goodness. And since misery implies in itself the notion of an evil, therefore it cannot be judged to be a good, and because of this it cannot be desired.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that the good, which is the will’s object, is in things, as the Philosopher says in the *Metaphysics* 6;Implicitly in Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 6, ch. 4, 1027b24–26; cf. *Metaphysics*, Bk. 4, ch. 4, 1008b3–31, and Bk. 5, ch. 1, 1013a17–22; *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 10, 433a15–21, 433a26–31, 433b15–18. and so the will’s motion must have its term in a thing existing outside the soul. Now, although a thing, so far as it is in the soul, may be considered according to a common notion prescinding from a particular notion, a thing outside the soul cannot exist according to a common notion alone, without the addition of a proper notion.This means that while ideas in the mind can be abstract, things known outside the mind must be particular. And thus, however much the will is borne to the good, it must be borne to some determinate good; and likewise however much it is borne to the highest good, it must be borne to the highest good under this or that notion. Now, although it belongs to the will by its natural inclination to be borne to beatitude according to a common notion, yet to be borne to such-and-such a beatitude comes not from natural inclination, but from the discernment of reason, which discovers that the highest good of man consists in this thing or in that; and so whenever someone desires beatitude, natural appetite and rational appetite are actually joined in that appetite for beatitude. And on the part of natural appetite there is always rectitude, while on the part of the rational appetite sometimes there is rectitude (namely, when beatitude is sought where it truly exists), and sometimes there is perversity (when it is sought where it does not truly exist); and thus, in desiring beatitude, someone can either merit (presupposing grace) or demerit, according as his desire is either right or perverse.  

Reply Obj. 1: In the desire for beatitude, together with what is natural, there is always, in addition, something voluntary and not merely natural; and it is in this way that the notion of merit or demerit can exist there.  

Reply Obj. 2: Although no one can avoid desiring beatitude in some way, still he can avoid desiring it in the way in which it is supposed to be desired; and thus he can merit or demerit.  

Reply Obj. 3: Man by his own powers is insufficient when it comes to desiring beatitude with a perfect desire, such as would suffice for meriting; but he has this sufficiency from the gift of divine grace, as is evident from 2 Corinthians 5:5: *he who has prepared us for this very thing is God*, that is, he who makes us desire true glory, according to the Gloss.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said that, according to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 7,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 8, 1151a16–17. the end stands in the order of desirable things as a principle stands in the order of intelligible things. Now, since what is first and greatest in any genus is the cause of those that are after it, so knowledge of the principles in speculative matters is the cause of the knowledge of all other such matters; and likewise, desire for the end is the cause of seeking all other things that are toward the end. Hence, since beatitude is the end of human life, whatever the will wants to bring about is ordered to beatitude. This is clear also from experience; for whoever desires something desires it insofar as it is judged a good. But by the very fact that someone has something that he judges good, he reckons himself nearer to beatitude, since the addition of a good to a good makes one approach nearer to the perfect good, which is beatitude itself. And therefore any and every desire is ordered to beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 1: Pleasure is among those things that are required for beatitude, since it is a formal aspect of beatitude. And so someone’s very desire for pleasure is ordered to beatitude, insofar as the pleasure itself is some likeness, whether near or remote, of that delight that perfects beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 2: Something may be ordered to beatitude through desire in two ways. In one way, as that which is sought so that through it a man may attain beatitude, as someone wants to undertake works of virtue so that through this he may merit beatitude. In another way, when someone wants something because it has some likeness to beatitude. For the result of the will’s seeking something is that it also desires whatever that thing’s likeness is found in, even if it cannot have the main thing desired; and in this way all who desire sins are still longing for beatitude and turning toward an imitation of God, as Augustine says in his *Confessions* 2,Augustine, *Confessions*, Bk. 2, ch. 6, n. 13 (CCSL 27:23). speaking thus: *pride imitates your loftiness, since you, O exalted God, are the one above all; ambition, what does it seek except honor and glory, while you are deserving of honor before all*; and he brings in the other vices in like manner.  

Reply Obj. 3: In that theft, as Augustine says in the same place,Ibid., n. 14 (CCSL 27:24). there was something that had an appearance of good, namely acting against the law, in which a certain shadow of freedom appeared. This is why he says: *what, therefore, did I love in that thievery, and how was I imitating you, my God, [though] viciously and perversely?* And he answers, saying: *or was it not pleasing to act against the law at least by trickery, since I could not do so by power, in order that I, being a slave, might imitate freedom by doing with impunity what was unlawful, in a dark likeness of omnipotence?*  

Reply Obj. 4: People who expose themselves to death without hope of a future life do so in two different ways. In one way, for the sake of the activity of virtue, as those who willingly choseIt is true that choice is already an act of will, but “willingly chose” brings out perhaps the emphasis in the verb *praeelegerunt*. to undergo death in order to save their homeland or to shun an unworthy deed; and according to their judgment, they did indeed order this to beatitude—not a beatitude to be gained after death, but one that was to be achieved then, in the very work, because to do a perfect work of virtue, which was done by enduring death, was the thing they most of all desired, the thing in which they placed their beatitude. In another way, people expose themselves to death because they are weary of the misery that they endure, which they reckon they can evade by death. Now, to evade misery and to desire beatitude come to the same thing. And so it stands that the desire of those who want to undergo death is ordered to beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 5: For the will to be right, two things are required. One is that it be aimed at a due end; the other, that what is ordered to the end be proportioned to the end. Now, although every desire has reference to beatitude, still it happens, in this or that way, that a desire may be perverse; since the very appetite for beatitude can be perverse, when it is sought where it does not exist, as is clear from what was said before; and if it is sought where it truly exists, it can also happen that what one desires on account of the end is not proportioned to the end, as when someone wants to steal in order to give alms, intending in this way to merit beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 6: Just as with things understood it is not necessary that in all consequent demonstrations the first principles be employed in the sense of being actually thought about, but only in the sense that they are present by their power in what is actually thought about, so long as the demonstration is drawn from things that have credibility from the first principles; so, too, although every desire has reference to beatitude, it is not necessary that in every desire one should be actually thinking about beatitude, but the desire for beatitude is present by its power in all other desires, as a cause is present in its effects.  

Article 4  

***Whether all participate in it equally***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fourth question, we proceed thus. It seems that the beatitude of the saints is not going to be greater after the last judgment than before.This is the first text in Distinction 49 that has its “parallel” (q. 93, a. 1) in the *Supplementum* of the *Summa theologiae*, compiled, some have maintained, by Thomas’s *socius* Reginald (see Torrell, *Person and Work*, 147, with n. 14). For the most part, the compiler of the Supplement restrained himself to minor modifications, such as removing the cross-reference to “Distinction 44, above,” and supplying Augustine for the closing *auctoritas* instead of Jerome. Interestingly, he excises qa. 2, skipping from qa. 1 to qa. 3—evidently aware that Thomas has already treated the contents of qa. 2 earlier in the *ST*. For the more something approaches to the divine likeness, the more perfectly it participates in beatitude. But the soul separated from the body is more like to God than the soul joined to its body. Therefore, its beatitude is greater before the resumption of the body than afterwards.  

Obj. 2: Further, a unified power is more powerful than a diversified one. But the soul outside the body is more unified than when it is joined to the body. Therefore, its power is greater for acting; and so it participates more perfectly in beatitude, which consists in act.  

Obj. 3: Further, beatitude consists in an act of the speculative intellect, as is evident from what was said above. But the intellect in its act does not employ a bodily organ; and so, the body resumed will not make the soul understand more perfectly. Therefore, the beatitude of the soul will not be greater after the resurrection.  

Obj. 4: Further, there cannot be anything greater than the infinite; and so, the infinite to which something finite is added is no greater than the infinite itself. But the blessed soul before the resumption of the body has beatitude owing to the fact that it rejoices in the infinite good, namely in God; and after the resumption of the body it will still not have joy in anything else, except perhaps the glory of the body, which is a certain finite good. Therefore, their joy after the resumption of the body will not be greater than before.  

On the contrary (1), commenting on Revelation 8In fact, Rev. 6:9. Cf. Strabo, *Glossa ordinaria* on this verse (PL 114:722); Haimo, *On the Apocalypse*, Bk. 2, on 6:9 (PL 117:1029). where it says, *I saw under the altar the souls of those who had been slain for the word of God and for the witness they had borne*, the Gloss says: *presently the souls of the saints are existing “under,” that is, in a lesser dignity than will be theirs in the future*. Therefore, their beatitude will be greater after death.  

Furthermore (2), as beatitude is paid back to the good as a reward, so is misery paid back to the wicked. But the misery of the wicked after the resumption of their bodies will be greater than before, since they will be punished not only in soul but also in body. Therefore, too, the beatitude of the saints will be greater after the resurrection of the body than before it.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems all will participate equally in beatitude.Parallels: Among the many discussions of this question, see *In I Sent*. d. 44, a. 3, ad 5; *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 2, ad 4; q. 5, a. 2; q. 112, a. 4, esp. ad 2; *SCG* III, ch. 58; *Super Ioan*. 14, lec. 14; *Super I Cor*. 3, lec. 2. The compiler of the Supplement to the *ST* chose to omit this qa. in the “new” q. 93, aware that its subject-matter had already been treated. For it is said in Matthew 20:9 that *all shall receive a single denarius*. Now the denarius there signifies something that all will have in common. But this is nothing other than beatitude. Therefore, each one will have just as much of beatitude as any other.  

Obj. 2: Further, there is nothing beyond the ultimate. But beatitude is the ultimate of those things that can be desired by men. Therefore beyond the beatitude of one man there cannot be something in another man that somehow exceeds the first man’s beatitude; and so the same conclusion follows as before.  

Obj. 3: Further, as a good is desirable to anyone, so a greater good is more desirable. Therefore, if someone were more blessed than another who was less blessed, the first person’s beatitude would be more desirable to the other person, according to a right appetite; yet he would not have it. Therefore, he would not be blessed; since, as is said in the text, no one is called blessed unless he has all that he wants, and wants nothing evil.  

On the contrary (1), it says in 1 Corinthians 15:41, *star differs from star in glory; so it is in the resurrection of the dead*. Therefore, among those who rise, one will have more glory than another.  

Furthermore (2), reward ought to correspond to merit. But some people have more excellent merits than others. Therefore, since beatitude is the reward of virtue, it seems that they will have a greater beatitude.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that these degrees of beatitude ought not to be called ‘dwelling places.’This qa. is brought over to *Supplementum* q. 93, a. 2, with no modification. A parallel, in some respects, is *ST* 1–2, q. 5, a. 2, esp. ad 1. For beatitude carries with it the notion of reward. But dwelling place signifies nothing that pertains to reward. Therefore, different degrees of beatitude ought not to be called dwelling places.“Dwelling places” translates *mansiones*. The King James and Douay-Rheims simply say “mansions,” while the RSV has “rooms” and the New American Bible, “dwelling places.” Any of these, it seems, would be acceptable translations, but since “mansion” in contemporary English refers only to an oversized domicile of the wealthy class, it seems odd to use this word, whereas etymologically “dwelling places” is closer to the root of *mansio*.  

Obj. 2: Further, ‘dwelling place’ seems to signify a place. But the place where the saints will be made blessed is not bodily but spiritual, namely God, who is one. Therefore, there is only one dwelling place; and so different degrees of beatitude cannot be called dwelling places.  

Obj. 3: Further, as in heaven there will be people of diverse merits, so now they are in purgatory, and they were in the limbo of the fathers in former times. But in purgatory and limbo, no dwelling places are distinguished one from another. Therefore, neither in heaven ought dwelling places to be distinguished one from another.  

On the contrary (1), there stands what is said in John 14:2: *in my Father’s house are many rooms*—which AugustineSee Augustine, *On the Gospel of John* 14:1–3, tract. 67, n. 2 (CCSL 36:495–96). expounds as the various dignities of the rewards.  

Furthermore (2), in any city there is an orderly distinction of dwelling places. But the heavenly homeland is compared to a city, as is clear from Revelation 21. Therefore, different dwelling places must be distinguished there, according to diverse degrees of beatitude.  

Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that different dwelling places are not distinguished according to different degrees of charity.Parallels: This qa. is brought over to *Supplementum* q. 93, a. 3, with no modification; cf. *ST* I, q. 12, a. 6 and q. 62, a. 9. The more general point that a saint’s subjective participation in God’s blessedness corresponds to his capacity or receptivity, which was co-determined by God’s grace and the saint’s exercise of charity *in via*, is a commonplace in Thomas’s works. For it says in Matthew 25:15: *he gave to each one according to his own power*. But the power proper to anything is its natural force. Therefore, gifts of both grace and glory are distributed according to different degrees of natural power.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, in Psalm 62 (61):12 it is said: *thou dost requite a man according to his work*. But what is rendered is the measure of beatitude. Therefore, the degrees of beatitude are distinguished according to a diversity of works, and not according to a diversity of charity.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, the reward is owed to act, and not to habit; hence it is not the strongest who are crowned, but the ones who enter the fight, as is clear from the *Ethics* 1Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 8, 1099a4. and in 2 Timothy 2:5: *he will not be crowned unless he strive lawfully*. But beatitude is the reward. Therefore different degrees of beatitude are not according to different degrees of charity.Which is a habit, but according to different degrees of active striving.  

On the contrary (1), the more joined someone will be to God, the more he will be blessed. But the measure of union with God follows the measure of charity. There will therefore be differences in beatitude according to differences in charity.  

Furthermore (2), as one thing taken simply follows upon another taken simply, so a greater degree of the one follows upon a greater degree of the other. But having beatitude follows upon having charity. Therefore, having more beatitude follows upon having more charity.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, it is indeed manifest that the beatitude of the saints after the resurrection will increase as regards its extension, since then beatitude will be not only in the soul but also in the body; and even the beatitude of the soul itself will be increased as regards extension, insofar as the soul will rejoice not only over its own good, but over the good of the body.  

It can also be said that the beatitude of the soul itself will be increased as regards intensity. For man’s body can be considered in two ways. In one way, as it is perfectible by the soul; in another way, as there is in it something that resists the soul in the soul’s activities, as long as the body is not perfectly perfected by the soul. Now, according to the first way of considering the body, the union of body to soul adds a perfection to the soul, since a part, as such, is imperfect, and finds completion in its whole. Thus the whole stands to the parts as form to matter, and thus the soul is more perfect in its natural being when it is in the whole—namely in man compounded of soul and body—than when it is separate by itself. But the union of the body according to the second way of considering it impedes the perfection of the soul; and thus it is said in Wisdom 9:15 that *a perishable body weighs down the soul*. Therefore, if from the body everything were taken away through which it resists the soul’s action, simply speaking the soul would be more perfect existing in such a body than separate by itself. But the more perfect something is in being, the more perfect it can be in acting; and so the activity of a soul united to such a body will be more perfect than the activity of a separated soul. Now, this is the kind of body that the glorified body is, since it is altogether subject to the spirit, as was said above in Distinction 44.  

For this reason, since beatitude consists in activity, the soul’s beatitude will be more perfect after the resumption of the body than before. For just as the soul separated from the corruptible body can act more perfectly than when it is united to it, so after it has been united to a glorified body, its activity will be more perfect than when it was separated. Now, every imperfect thing desires its own perfection, and so the separated soul naturally desires union with the body; and because of this desire proceeding from imperfection, its activity which is directed toward God is less intense; and this is what Jerome says:In the Editiones Paulinae text of the *ST*, the quotation is attributed rather to Augustine, with the editors supplying a reference to *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis*, Bk. 12, ch. 35, PL 34:483. that due to desire for the body, the soul is held back from passing into that highest good with its whole intention.  

Reply Obj. 1: The soul united to the glorified body is more like God than when it is separated from such a body, insofar as the composite has being more perfectly. For the more perfectly something exists, the more like God it is, as the heart, since the perfection of its life consists in motion,Literally: “as the heart, the perfection of the life of which consists in motion,” etc. For St. Thomas’s thoughts about the crucial function of the heart’s motion in the animal’s life, see his opusculum *De motu cordis* (ed. Leon. 43:127–30). is more like God when it is moving than when it rests, even though God himself never moves.  

Reply Obj. 2: A power that by its nature belongs in matter is more powerful when existing in matter than when separated from matter, even though absolutely speaking a power that is separated from matter is more powerful.  

Reply Obj. 3: Although the soul does not employ the body in the act of understanding, still the body’s perfection will in a way cooperate toward the perfection of intellectual activity, inasmuch as the soul, from its union with the glorified body, will be more perfect in its nature, and consequently more effective in its activity. And in this respect the very good of the body will cooperate, as if instrumentally, toward that activity in which beatitude consists, even as the Philosopher states in the *Ethics* 1Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 8, 1099a33, and ch. 9, 1099b27. that external goods cooperate instrumentally toward happiness of life.  

Reply Obj. 4: Although a finite added to an infinite does not make a greater, still it makes more, because the infinite and the finite are two, since the infinite, taken by itself, is one. Now, the extension of joy spoken of does not have reference to greater, but to more; hence joy will increase as regards extension, if one compares the joy taken in God and in the body’s glory, to the joy that was taken in God alone. The body’s glory will also contribute to the intensifying of the joy that is from God inasmuch as it is cooperating with a more perfect activity by which the soul is borne into God. For the more perfect a suitable activity is, the greater will be the delight in doing it, as is evident from what is said in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b19.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that since beatitude consists in activity, the level of beatitude is to be gauged according to the level of perfection in acting. Now, the perfection of activity in which happiness consists is evaluated on the basis of two factors: one on the side of the acting subject, the other on the side of the object. This is why the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 10See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b18–20, and ch. 7, 1177a12–21. that the most perfect activity is the one that is of the highest power perfected by the noblest habit (which is on the side of the acting subject) and with respect to the noblest object. Now, the object of the activity in which beatitude consists is altogether one and the same, namely the divine essence, from the vision of which all will be blessed; and so on its side there will not be any level in beatitude. But on the side of the acting subject, the activity of beatitude will not be perfect in the same way, since insofar as the habit that perfects this activity, namely the light of glory, will be more perfect in one than in another, to that extent this activity will be more perfect, and the delight greater; and from this vantage, all the blessed will not be in the same level of beatitude.  

Reply Obj. 1: The denarius represents the one who will be commonly rendered to the blessed on the side of the object of beatitude itself, since God will show his essence to all; but each one will gaze on it in his own way.  

Reply Obj. 2: As fire is the most subtle kind of body, and nevertheless one fire can be more subtle than another, so beatitude, considered according to its kind, is the ultimate of all things desirable to mankind; hence nothing prevents the beatitude of one from being more perfect than the beatitude of another. Moreover, the beatitude of each one is ultimate inasmuch as his desire is wholly terminated in it, even if it is not ultimate in the sense of existing in the ultimate degree of perfection.  

Reply Obj. 3: In the blessed there will be perfect charity; thus anyone there will love God more than himself, and so he will more want what is more suited to God, than what would be better for himself. And thus he will more want divine justice to be preserved by his having less, which is more suited to God, than he will want to have more, which would be better for him.The Parma ed.: *et ideo magis volet divinam justitiam salvari in hoc quod ipse minus habeat, quod est deo convenientius, quam in hoc quod plus habeat, quod esset sibi melius*. We are reading the *in* of *in hoc quod plus habeat* as a mistake caused by unconsciously making this clause correspond with the previous one. The content of the text makes little sense if one interprets the grammar as it stands. The reason why his having less is more suited to God, is precisely that the divine justice is thereby preserved. If the divine justice were preserved in his having more, there would be nothing more suited to God about his having less—none mentioned in the text, that is.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that since local motion is prior to all other motions, for this reason, according to the Philosopher,Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 8, ch. 9, 265b17. the names ‘motion’ and ‘distance’ and everything of the kind are carried over from local motion to all other motions. Now the end of local motion is a place,This works better in Latin: *finis motus localis est locus*. where something remains at rest once it has arrived, and is preserved there; and so, in any motion, the very being-at-rest in the end of the motion we call ‘station’The term is *collocatio*. The meaning here basically refers to something being at rest or stable due to its position vis-à-vis other things, or the place itself in which it is at rest: station, resting place. Thomas seems to be almost equivocating on the usage of *collocatio* and *mansio* when referring to the being at rest (standing, dwelling) and the usage when referring to the place in which something is at rest (station, dwelling place). or ‘dwelling’; and therefore since the name of motion is carried over to the act of appetite and of will, the very gaining of the end of appetitive motion is called a dwelling place, or station, in the end. And this is why different ways of obtaining the ultimate end are called different dwelling places, such that the one house corresponds to the one beatitude, which is on the side of the object, while the plurality of dwellings corresponds to the differences that are found in beatitude on the part of the blessed—as we also see in natural things that everything light tends to the same place up above, but the lighter something is, the closer it comes to that place; and thus they have diverse dwelling places according to differences in lightness.  

Reply Obj. 1: ‘Dwelling place’ does imply the notion of end, as is evident from what was said, and so consequently implies the notion of reward, which is the end of merit.  

Reply Obj. 2: Although there is one spiritual place, there are still different steps for approaching that place; and accordingly, diverse dwelling places are established.  

Reply Obj. 3: Those who were in limbo or who are now in purgatory have not yet come to their end, and so in purgatory or in limbo dwelling places are not distinguished, one from the other; but this is done only in paradise and in hell, where we find the end of the good and of the wicked.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said that there are two principles that distinguish heavenly dwelling places or degrees of beatitude: proximate and remote. The proximate principle is the difference of disposition that will be found in the blessed, out of which there arises among them a diversity of perfection in the activity of beatitude; but the remote principle is the merit by which they attained such beatitude. Now, in the first way, the dwelling places are distinguished according to the charity possessed in heaven—the more perfect it is in someone, the more it will render him receptive of the divine brilliance, and according to this increase the perfection of divine vision will be increased. But the second way the dwelling places are distinguished is according to charity possessed in the wayfaring state. For our actions in this life do not have their power of meriting from the very substance of the actions, but solely from the habit of virtue by which they are informed. Now, the power of meriting is in every virtue from charity, which has the ultimate end as its object; and so, any difference in meriting comes down completely to a difference in charity. And this is how the charity possessed in the wayfaring state will, by way of merit, differentiate heavenly dwelling places.  

Reply Obj. 1: ‘Power’ here is not to be taken solely for natural capacity, but for natural capacity together with an effort toward having grace; and taken in this way, ‘power’ will be something like a material disposition to the measure of grace and glory received. But charity is what formally completes merit toward glory; and therefore a distinction of degrees in glory is taken according to levels of charity rather than according to levels in the power described.i.e., natural capacity and effort.  

Reply Obj. 2: Works only have a claim on the recompense of glory insofar as they are informed by charity; and so there will be different degrees in glory according to different degrees of charity.  

Reply Obj. 3: While the habit of charity (or of any virtue) is not the merit that the reward is owed to, it is nevertheless the principle and the whole rationale for meriting in any act; and therefore diverse rewards are distinguished according to its diversity—although a certain degree in meriting could also be considered by reason of the very genus of a certain act, not with respect to the essential reward, which is rejoicing in God, but with respect to an accidental reward, which is joy over a created good.  

Question 2  

***The beatific vision***

***Next, we inquire about the vision of God; and here seven questions arise:***

first, whether the saints will see God in his essence;  

- second, whether they can see God with the eye of the body;
- third, whether by seeing God they comprehend him;
- fourth, whether some people will see God in his essence more perfectly than others;
- fifth, whether it is necessary for those who see the divine essence to see everything that God sees;
- sixth, whether any created being can arrive at seeing God in his essence from purely natural endowments;
- seventh, whether anyone can see God in his essence in the wayfaring state.
- Article 1

***Whether the saints will see God in his essence***

Obj. 1: To the first question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 1; *ST* I-II, q. 3, a. 8; *De veritate*, q. 8, a. 1; *Super Matt.* 5; *SCG* III, chs. 51, 54, and 57; *Quodl.* VII, q. 1, a. 1; *Quodl.* X, q. 8, a. un.; *Super Ioan.* 1, lec. 11; *Comp. theo.* 104. It seems that the human intellect cannot arrive at seeing God in his essence. For *no one has ever seen God* (Jn 1:18) is explained by ChrysostomJohn Chrysostom, *Homilies on John*, Hom. 15, PG 59:97–98. as meaning that not even *the heavenly essences (I mean the Cherubim and Seraphim) were ever able to see him as he is*. But men are promised nothing but equality with the angels: *they are like the angels of God in heaven* (Mt 22:30). Therefore, neither will the saints in heaven see God in his essence.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, in *On the Divine Names* 1,Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 1 (PG 3:586). Dionysius gives the following argument. Knowledge is only of existing things. But every existing thing is finite, since it is determined to some genus. And so God, since he is infinite, is above all existing things. Therefore, there is no knowledge of him; rather, he is above knowledge.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *Mystical Theology*, ch. 1 (PG 3:998). shows that the most perfect manner in which our intellect can be joined to God is inasmuch as it is joined to him as the unknown. But what is seen in its essence is not unknown. Therefore, it is impossible for our intellect to see God in his essence.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *Epistle I to Gaius* (PG 3:1066). says that *the darkness surrounding God*, which is what he calls God’s abundance of light, *is obscured from all light and hidden from all knowledge; and if anyone who sees God understands what he has seen, he has not seen him but rather something of the things that are his*. Therefore, no created intellect will be able to see God in his essence.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, as Dionysius says in Letter 5 to Dorotheus: *God exists as invisible due to his exceeding brightness*.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Epistle to Dorotheus* (PG 3:1074). But just as his brightness exceeds man’s intellect in the wayfaring state, so also it exceeds man’s intellect in heaven. Therefore, just as he is invisible in the wayfaring state, so also he will be invisible in heaven.  

Obj. 6: Furthermore, since the intelligible is a perfection of the intellect, there must be a proportion between the intellect and the intelligible, between the visible and vision. But there cannot be a proportion between our intellect and the divine essence, since they are at an infinite distance. Therefore, our intellect cannot attain to seeing the divine essence.  

Obj. 7: Furthermore, God is more distant from our intellect than a created intelligible object is from our senses. But the senses can in no way attain to seeing a spiritual creature. Therefore, neither can our intellect attain to seeing the divine essence.  

Obj. 8: Furthermore, whenever the intellect understands something actually it must be informed by way of a likeness to what is understood, which is the principle of intellectual activity determined to such an object, just as heat is the principle of heating. If, then, the intellect understood God, this would have to occur through a likeness informing the intellect. But this cannot be the divine essence itself. For the form and the formed must have a single being, but the divine intelligence differs from our intellect in both essence and being. Thus the form by which our intellect is informed when it understands God must be a likeness impressed by God onto our intellect. But this likeness, since it is something created, cannot lead to the knowledge of God except as the effect leads to the cause. Therefore, it is impossible for our intellect to see God except through his effect. But the vision of God through his effects is not the vision of God in his essence. Therefore, our intellect will not be able to see God in his essence.  

Obj. 9: Furthermore, the divine essence is more distant from our intellect than any angel or intelligence. But, as Avicenna says in his *Metaphysics*,Avicenna, *Metaphysics*, Tract 3, ch. 8. for an intelligence to be in our intellect is not for the essence of the intelligence to be in our intellect. For in this case the knowledge we have of intelligences would be a substance, not an accident. But this is for an impression of the intelligence to be in our intellect. Therefore God, too, is only in our intellect in such a manner as to be understood by us inasmuch as an impression of him is in our intellect. But this impression cannot lead to knowledge of the divine essence. For since it is at an infinite distance from the divine essence, it would degenerate into another species much more readily than if the species of white were to degenerate into the species of black. Now, one in whose sight the species of white degenerates into the species of black, due to the indisposition of an organ, is not said to see white. So, too, our intellect, which understands God only through an impression of him, will not be able to see him in his essence.  

Obj. 10: Furthermore, in things separated from matter, what understands and what is understood are the same, as is clear in *On the Soul* 3.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 5, 430a20; cf. ch. 7, 431a1. But God is maximally separated from matter. Since, then, an intellect that is created cannot attain to becoming an uncreated essence, it will not be able to be the case that it sees God in his essence.  

Obj. 11: Furthermore, in the case of every thing that is seen in its essence, what it is is known. But in the case of God, the intellect cannot see what he is but only what he is not, as DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 7, n. 3 (PG 3:870). and DamasceneJohn Damascene, *On the Orthodox Faith*, Bk. 1 (PG 94:786). say. Therefore, the intellect cannot see God in his essence.  

Obj. 12: Furthermore, every infinite, insofar as it is infinite, is unknown. But God is infinite in every way. Therefore, he is altogether unknown and thus will not be able to be seen in his essence by a created intellect.  

Obj. 13: Furthermore, Augustine says in *On the Vision of God*: *God is invisible by nature*.Augustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (also known as *On the Vision of God*), par. 15 (CSEL 44:311); cf. par. 7. But the natural properties of God cannot be otherwise than they are. Therefore, it cannot be the case that he is seen in his essence.  

Obj. 14: Furthermore, every thing that exists in one way and is seen in another way is not seen in accord with what it is. But God exists in one way and is seen in another by the saints in heaven. For he exists after his own manner, but the saints will see him after their manner. Therefore, the saints will not see him in accord with what he is and so will not see him in his essence.  

Obj. 15: Furthermore, what is seen through a medium is not seen in its essence. But in heaven God is seen through a medium, which is the light of glory, as is clear from Scripture: *in thy light do we see light* (Ps 35 [36]:10). Therefore, he will not be seen in his essence.  

Obj. 16: Furthermore, in heaven God is seen *face to face*, as Scripture says (1 Cor 13:12). But when we see a man face to face, we see him through a likeness. Therefore, in heaven God will be seen through a likeness and so not in his essence.  

On the contrary (1), it says in 1 Corinthians 13:12: *now we see in a mirror dimly, but then face to face*. But what is seen face to face is seen in its essence. Therefore, the saints will see God in his essence in heaven.  

Furthermore (2), *when he appears we shall be like him, for we shall see him as he is* (1 Jn 3:2). Therefore, we will see God in his essence.  

Furthermore (3), commenting on 1 Corinthians 15:24: *when he delivers the kingdom to God the Father*, a Gloss says: *where* (i.e., in heaven) *the essence of the Father and the Son and the Holy Spirit will be seen, which will be granted only to pure hearts, which is the height of beatitude*. Therefore, the blessed will see God in his essence.  

Furthermore (4), John 14:21 says: *he who loves me will be loved by my Father, and I will love him and manifest myself to him*. But what is manifested is seen essentially. Therefore, God will be seen essentially by the saints in heaven.  

Furthermore (5), on the passage *man shall not see me and live* (Ex 33:20), GregoryGregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143A), Bk. 18, par. 54. disproves the opinion of those who said that in the realm of beatitude God can be perceived in his brightness but cannot be seen in his nature, since his brightness is not one thing and his nature another. But his nature is his essence. Therefore, he is seen in his essence.  

Furthermore (6), the desire of the saints cannot be altogether frustrated. But the universal desire of the saints is to see God in his essence, as is clear: *show me thy face* (Ex 33:18), and *show us the Father, and we shall be satisfied* (Jn 14:8). Therefore, the saints will see God in his essence.  

I answer that, just as we assert in accord with the faith that the last end of human life is the vision of God, so also philosophers posit that the ultimate happiness of man is to understand substances separate from matter according to their being. And thus, concerning this question, the same difficulty and diversity of opinion is found in the case of philosophers and theologians.  

For some philosophers have posited that our potential intellect can never arrive at understanding separated substances, as Alpharabius asserts at the end of his *Ethics*, though he said the contrary in *On the Intellect*, as the Commentator relates, commenting on *On the Soul* 3.Averroes, *On the Soul* 3, comment 36. Likewise, some theologians have posited that the human intellect can never arrive at seeing God in his essence. And both are moved to this position by the distance between our intellect and the divine essence or other separated substances. For since the intellect in act is in a certain way one with the intelligible in act, for a created intellect in any way to become an uncreated essence seems to be a difficulty. Hence, too, ChrysostomJohn Chrysostom, *Homilies on John*, Hom. 15, PG 59:98. says: *for how can what is subject to creation see what is uncreateable*? And there is a greater difficulty in this for those who posit that the potential intellect is generable and corruptible as a power dependent on the body, not only with respect to the vision of God but also with respect to the vision of any separated substances.  

But this position is totally unable to stand. First, because it is repugnant to the authority of canonical Scripture, as Augustine says in *On Seeing God*. Second, because of the fact that since understanding is man’s most proper activity, we must assign his beatitude on the basis of this activity, i.e., when this activity is perfected in him. But since the perfection of one who understands, precisely as understanding, is the intelligible object itself, if in the most perfect activity of his intellect man arrives not at seeing the divine essence but at something else, we will have to say that something other than God is what makes man himself blessed. And since the ultimate perfection of any thing is in connection to its principle, it follows that something other than God is man’s efficient principle. But this is absurd to us as well as to philosophers who posit that our souls emanate from separated substances such that in the end we are able to understand them.  

Hence, on our terms, we must posit that our intellect does at some time arrive at seeing the divine essence, and, on the terms of the philosophers, we must posit that it arrives at seeing the essence of separated substances. Now how this can happen remains to be investigated.  

Some, such as Alpharabius and Avempace, said that from the fact that our intellect understands all intelligible objects it attains to the vision of the essence of the separated substance. And they proceed to show this in two ways. The first of these is that just as matter is only specifically differentiated in different individuals to the extent that it is joined to individuating principles, so also the understood form of man is not differentiated to me and to you, except to the extent that it is joined to different imaginable forms. And thus when our intellect separates the form understood from the forms of the imagination, the understood quiddity remains, which is one and the same to different people who understand it. And such is the quiddity of a separated substance. Thus, when our intellect reaches the highest abstraction of any intelligible quiddity, it thereby understands the quiddity of a separated substance that is like it. The second way is based on the fact that our intellect was made to abstract quiddity from all intelligible objects that have quiddity. If, then, the quiddity it abstracts from this singular object having quiddity is a quiddity that does not have quiddity, then by understanding it, it will understand the quiddity of a separated substance that has this kind of disposition due to the fact that separated substances are subsisting quiddities that do not have quiddities. For the quiddity of a simple thing is the simple thing itself, as Avicenna says.Avicenna, *Metaphysics*, Tract 3, ch. 8. But if the quiddity abstracted from this particular sensible object is a quiddity that has quiddity, then our intellect was made to abstract it. And thus, since it cannot go to infinity, it will arrive at a quiddity that does not have quiddity, which is understood to be separated quiddity.  

But this way does not seem to be sufficient. First, because the quiddity of a material substance abstracted by our intellect does not have the same character as the quiddities of separated substances. And thus, through the fact that our intellect abstracts the quiddities of material things and knows them, it does not follow that it knows the quiddity of a separated substance, especially the divine essence, which differs in character most of all from every created quiddity. Second, because, even granted that they have the same character, when the quiddity of a composite thing is known, this does not entail that the quiddity of a separated substance is known, except on the basis of the highest-level genus, which is substance. But this knowledge is imperfect, unless one arrives at the proper characteristics of the thing. For one who knows man only inasmuch as he is an animal only knows him in a qualified sense and potentially. And much less does he know him if he only knows the nature of substance in him. Hence to know God or other separated substances in this way is not to see the divine essence or the quiddity of a separated substance. It is, rather, to know it through its effect and as if in a mirror. And thus another method for how we understand separated substances is laid out by Avicenna in his *Metaphysics*, namely, that separated substances are understood by us through the intentions of their quiddities, which are certain likenesses to them not abstracted from them, since they themselves are immaterial, but instead impressed by them on our souls.  

But this way, too, does not seem to us to be sufficient to constitute the vision of God that we seek. For it is obvious that everything that is received in another is in it after the mode of the receiver. And thus a likeness to the divine essence imprinted by it on our intellect will be there after the mode of our intellect. But the mode of our intellect falls short of the perfect reception of the divine likeness. Now falling short of perfect likeness can occur in as many ways as unlikeness is found. In one way, a likeness falls short when it is participated in by a form with the same specific character but not with the same mode of perfection, as the likeness in what has a little whiteness falls short when compared with what has much. In another way, a likeness falls short even more when it does not attain the same specific character but only the same generic character, as in the case of a likeness between what is crimson in color and what is white in color. Another way, which is an even greater falling short, is when it attains to the same generic character but only by analogy, for example, the likeness of whiteness to man in the fact that both are a being. And this is the way in which the likeness received in a created being with respect to the divine essence falls short. But for the sight to know whiteness, a likeness of whiteness according to its specific character must be received in it, though not according to the same mode of being. For a form has being in one mode in the senses, and being in another mode in the thing existing outside the soul. For if the form of crimson is in the eye, it is not said to see whiteness. Likewise, for the intellect to understand a quiddity, there must be a likeness of the same specific character in it, though perhaps it is not the same mode of being in both cases. For the form existing in the intellect or the senses is not the principle of knowledge according to the mode of being that it has in both cases, but according to the character in which it shares with the external thing. And thus it is clear that in this way God’s essence cannot be understood to be seen immediately through any likeness received in a created intellect.  

For this reason some others, holding that the divine essence is seen only in this way, said that the essence itself will not be seen but rather a kind of radiance, like a ray of the essence. Hence neither does this mode suffice for the vision of God that we seek.  

And thus we must accept another mode, one also posited by some philosophers, namely Alexander and Averroes in *On the Soul* 3. For since in any act of knowing a form by which the thing is known or seen is necessary, the form by which the intellect is perfected for seeing separated substances is not a quiddity that the intellect abstracts from composite things, as the first opinion stated. Nor is it an impression left by the separated substance on our intellect, as the second opinion stated. Rather, it is the separated substance itself, which is joined to our intellect as a form in such a way that it itself is what is understood and that by which it is understood. And whatever the case may be for other separated substances, this is the mode we must accept for the vision of God in his essence. For no matter what other form might inform our intellect, our intellect could not be led through it to the divine essence. But this should not be understood as if the divine essence is the true form of our intellect or as if from it and our intellect one thing simply comes about, as occurs in natural things from natural form and matter. Rather, it should be understood that the proportion of the divine essence to our intellect is like the proportion of form to matter. For whenever two things of which one is more perfect than the other are received in the same receptacle, the proportion of one of the two to the other, namely of the more perfect to the less perfect, is like the proportion of form to matter, as when light and color are received in the transparent and of these light is related to color as form to matter. And thus since the intellective power is received in the soul as well as the in-dwelling divine essence, though not in the same mode, the divine essence will be related to the intellect as form to matter.  

And in the following way it can be shown that this suffices for our intellect to be able to see the divine essence itself through the divine essence. For just as from the natural form by which something has being and from matter, one being simply comes about, so also from the form by which the intellect understands together with the intellect itself, one single thing in the act of understanding comes about. But among natural things, the thing subsisting as such cannot be the form of any matter if that thing has matter as part of itself, since it cannot be the case that matter is the form of something. But if the thing subsisting as such is only a form, then nothing prevents it from being made the form of some matter and becoming the whereby-it-is of the composite, as is clear in the case of the soul.  

Now in the intellect we must understand the intellect itself in potency as being the matter and the intelligible species as being the form. And so the intellect understanding actually will be like the composite of both. Hence, if there is a thing subsisting as such that does not have anything in it besides what is intelligible in it, such a thing will be able to be the form by which the intellect understands. But any thing is intelligible to the extent that it partakes of act, not to the extent that it partakes of potency, as is clear in the *Metaphysics* 9.See Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 9, ch. 8; but this point is made multiple times throughout the book. A sign of this is that we must abstract the intelligible form from matter and all the properties of matter. And thus, since the divine essence is pure act, it will be able to be the form by which the intellect understands. And this will be the beatific vision. And thus the Master says in Book II, Distinction 1, that the union of the soul to the body is a kind of analogue of the blessed union by which the spirit will be united to God.  

Reply Obj. 1: This authority can be explained in three ways, as is clear from Augustine in *On Seeing God*.Augustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 5. It is explained in one way as excluding corporeal vision, by which no one has seen or will see God in his essence, as will be said. In other way, it is explained as excluding the intellectual vision of God in his essence from those who live in this mortal flesh. In the third way, it is explained as excluding comprehensive vision from the created intellect. And this is how Chrysostom understands it. Hence he adds: *the Evangelist calls the knowledge of this most certain and says that the comprehension is as much as the Father has of the Son*.John Chrysostom, *Homilies on John*, PG 59:359. And this is the Evangelist’s understanding, for which reason he adds: *the only Son, who is in the bosom of the Father, he has made him known* (Jn 1:18), intending to prove by way of his comprehensive vision that the Son is God.  

Reply Obj. 2: Just as God, through his infinite essence, surpasses all existing things that have determinate being, so also the knowledge by which he knows is above all knowledge. Hence the proportion of our knowledge to created beings is the proportion of God’s knowledge to his essence. Now two things come together for knowledge: the knower and that whereby the thing is known. But that vision by which we will see God in his essence is the same as the vision by which God sees himself, on the part of that by which he is seen. For just as he sees himself through his essence, so also we will see him in his essence. But on the part of the knower, there is found a difference between the divine intellect and ours. Now, in the act of knowing, what is known follows on the form by which we know. For through the form of a stone we see the stone. But the efficacy in the act of knowing follows on the power of the knower, just as one who has stronger sight sees more acutely. And thus in that vision we will see the same thing that God sees, namely his essence, but not as efficaciously.  

Reply Obj. 3: In this passage Dionysius is speaking of the knowledge by which we know God in the wayfaring state through a created form, by which our intellect is formed to see him. But, as Augustine says,Augustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 7. God evades every form of our intellect, since in the case of any form that our intellect conceives, that form does not attain the character of the divine essence. And thus he himself is impenetrable to our intellect. But in the wayfaring state we know him most perfectly when we know that he is above everything our intellect can conceive of. And in this way we are joined to him as something unknown. But in heaven we will see him through the form that is his essence and will be joined to him as known.  

Reply Obj. 4: *God is light*, as 1 Jn 1:5 says. Now brightness is the impression of light in what is illuminated. And because the divine essence belongs to a mode other than every likeness of it that is impressed on our intellect, he says that the divine darkness obscures all brightness, since the divine essence, which he calls *darkness*, remains unindicated by the impression on our intellect due to its surpassing splendor. And for this reason it follows that it is hidden from all knowledge. And thus if anyone who sees God conceives something in his mind, he conceives not of God but rather one of God’s effects.  

Reply Obj. 5: The splendor of God, though it surpasses every form by which our intellect is now informed, does not surpass the divine essence itself, which will be like a form for our intellect in heaven. And thus, though it is invisible to us now, it will then be visible.  

Reply Obj. 6: Even though there cannot be a proportion of the finite to the infinite, since the remainder of the infinite beyond the finite is indeterminate, there can be a proportionality that is a likeness of proportions between them. For as finite is equated to finite, infinite is equated to infinite. Now for something to be known completely, there must sometimes be a proportion between the knower and the known, since the power of the knower must be adjusted to the knowability of the thing known, equality being a kind of proportion. But sometimes the knowability of the thing exceeds the power of the knower, as when we know God; sometimes the converse is true, as when God knows created things. And in such a case there need not be a proportion between the knower and the known, but instead only a proportionality, namely, such that as the knower is related to the knowing, so also the thing knowable is related to the fact of its being known. And such a proportionality suffices for the infinite to be known by the finite as well as vice versa.  

Or we may say that the original meaning of ‘proportion’ signifies the relation of quantity to quantity with reference to a determinate excess or equalization, but that its meaning was later transferred to signify every relation of any given thing to another. And it is in this latter manner that we say that matter should be proportionate to form. And in this way nothing prevents our intellect, though finite, from being called proportionate to seeing an infinite essence, though not to comprehending it due to its immensity.  

Reply Obj. 7: ‘Likeness’ and ‘distance’ can be taken in two ways. In one way, they are understood with reference to agreement in nature, and in this way God is more distant from the created intellect than a created intelligible object is from the senses. In the other way, they are understood with reference to proportionality, and in this way the reverse is true. For the senses are not proportionate to knowing anything immaterial, whereas the intellect is proportionate to knowing any immaterial thing. And it is this likeness, not the first kind, that is required for knowledge. For it is obvious that when the intellect understands a stone, it is not like it in natural being, as, too, sight apprehends reddish honey and reddish bile, though it does not apprehend that honey is sweet. For the honey’s being reddish in color shares more with bile inasmuch as it is visible than the sweetness of honey does with bile.  

Reply Obj. 8: In the vision by which God will be seen in his essence, the divine essence itself will be like the intellect’s form by which it understands. And it is not necessary for them to become one single thing with respect to being simply, but only to become one so far as this pertains to the act of understanding, as was said above.  

Reply Obj. 9: In this respect we do not maintain what Avicenna said, for in this he is also contradicted by other philosophers, that Avicenna is speaking the knowledge of separated substances as they are known through the habits of the speculative sciences and the likeness to things. For he cites this to show that science is not a substance in us but an accident. Even so, although the divine essence, according to the properties of its nature, is more distant from our intellect than the substance of an angel, it has a greater character of intelligibility, since it is pure act with no admixture of potency, which is not the case in other separated substances. Nor will the knowledge by which we will see God in his essence be in the genus of accident on the part of what will be seen, but rather only as regards the act of the one understanding, which will be neither the substance of the one who understands nor of the one who is understood.  

Reply Obj. 10: A substance separated from matter understands itself and understands other things, and the authority cited can be held as true in either way. For since the essence itself of a separated substance is actually intelligible through itself by the fact that it is separated from matter, it is obvious that when a separated substance understands itself, what understands and what is understood are completely identical. For it does not understand itself through any intention abstracted from itself, as we understand material things. And this seems to be the Philosopher’s understanding in *On the Soul* 3, as is clear from what the Commentator says on the same passage. But to the extent that it understands other things, what is understood in act becomes one with the intellect in act, inasmuch as the form of what is understood becomes the form of the intellect inasmuch as the power of understanding is in act, not that it is the intellect’s very essence, as Avicenna proves in *On Natural Things* 6. For the intellect’s essence remains one under two forms when it understands two forms successively, after the manner in which prime matter remains one under different forms. Hence, too, the Commentator, commenting on *On the Soul* 3,Averroes, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, comment 5. compares the potential intellect to prime matter in this regard. And in this way it does not at all follow that our intellect becomes the divine essence itself when it sees God, but that the essence is compared to it as a perfection and form.  

Reply Obj. 11: These authorities and all those like them should be understood of the knowledge by which we know God in the wayfaring state, on the basis of the argument given before.  

Reply Obj. 12: The ‘infinite’ taken in a privative sense is unknown as such. For it is named from the removal of a completeness, from which comes the knowledge of a thing. And this is how the infinite is reduced to matter subject to privation, as is clear in the *Physics* 3.Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 3, ch. 7, 207b35–208a4; cf. ch. 6. But ‘infinite’ taken in a negative sense is named from the removal of limiting matter, since form is also limited in a certain way through matter. And so the infinite in this sense is maximally knowable, and this is the sense in which God is infinite.  

Reply Obj. 13: AugustineAugustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina*, par. 9 (CSEL 44:296). is speaking of corporeal vision, by which God will never be seen, as is clear from what he says just before: *for no one has ever or ever can see God as these visible things that are named are seen, and he is by nature invisible, just as he is incorruptible*. Now just as by his nature he is most of all a being, so also he is in himself most of all intelligible. But the fact that he is sometimes not understood by us is due to our own deficiency. For this reason the fact that he will be seen by us after not having been seen is due not to a change on his part but rather on ours.  

Reply Obj. 14: In heaven the saints will see God as he is, if this refers to the mode of what is seen, for God will be seen by the saints to have that mode which he has. But if it refers to the mode of the knower, then he will not be seen as he is, since the efficacy of the created intellect is not as great in seeing as the efficacy of the divine essence in being known.  

Reply Obj. 15: There are three kinds of medium found in corporeal and intellectual vision. The first is the medium under which something is seen. And this is what perfects sight for seeing in general, though not determining sight to any specific object, as corporeal light is related to corporeal sight and the light of the agent intellect to the potential intellect. The second is the medium by which something is seen. And this is the visible form by which both kinds of sight are determined to a specific object, as for example, through the form of a stone knowledge the intellect is determined to the understanding of a stone. The third is the medium in which it is seen. And this is that which, when looked into, leads the sight to another thing, as for example, by looking into a mirror sight is led to the things represented in the mirror and by seeing an image it is led to what it is an image of. And in this way, too, the intellect is led to a cause through its knowledge of an effect and vice versa. Therefore, in the vision of heaven there will not be the third medium, namely God’s being seen through the species of other things, as he is now known, and for this reason we are now said to see in a mirror. Nor will there be there the second medium, since the divine essence itself will be what our intellect will see God by, as is clear from what has been said. Instead there will only exist there the first medium, which will elevate our intellect to the ability to be joined to the uncreated essence in the way described. But this knowledge is not said to be mediated by this medium, since it does not fall between the knower and the thing known but is, rather, what gives to the knower the power of knowing.  

Reply Obj. 16: Physical created things are not said to be seen directly, except when whatever can be joined to sight in them is joined to it. But they are not able to be so joined in their essence by reason of their materiality. And so they are then seen directly when their likeness is joined to the intellect. But God is able to be joined to the intellect in his essence. Thus he would not be seen directly if his essence were not joined to the intellect. And this unmediated vision is called ‘seeing his face.’ Furthermore, a physical thing’s likeness is received in the sight according to the same account by which it exists in the thing, though not according to the same mode of being. And so the likeness leads to the thing directly. But no likeness can lead our intellect to God in this way, as is clear from what has been said. And for this reason the case is not similar.  

Article 2  

***Whether they can see God with the eyes of the body***

Obj. 1: To the second question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 3; *In IV Sent*. d. 48, q. 2, a. 1; *Super Matt.* 5; *Super Iob* 19, lec.  It seems that after the resurrection the saints will see God with bodily eyes. For the glorified eye will possess greater power than a non-glorified eye. But blessed Job saw God with his eye: *I had heard of thee by the hearing of the ear, but now my eye sees thee* (Job 42:5). Therefore, all the more will the glorified eye be able to see God in his essence.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, *from my flesh I shall see God*, my savior (Job 19:26). Therefore, it seems that in heaven God will be seen with physical eyes.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, Augustine,Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 22, ch. 29, n. 3. speaking of the sight possessed by glorified eyes, says the following: *those eyes will have a far greater power, not to see more sharply in the way that serpents or eagles are regarded as seeing (for no matter how clear these animals’ power of perception, they can see nothing other than bodies) but to see also incorporeal things*. But any power that can know incorporeal things can be elevated to see God. Therefore, glorified eyes will be able to see God.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, the same difference exists between corporeal things and incorporeal things as vice versa. But an incorporeal eye can see corporeally. Therefore, a corporeal eye can see incorporeal things. And so the same follows as before.
