Obj. 5: Furthermore, commenting on the passage: *it stood still, but I could not discern its appearance* (Job 4:16), Gregory,Gregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143), Bk. 5, par. 34. in his *Moralia* 5, says the following: *man who, if he had willed to keep the precept, would have been destined to become spiritual even in his flesh, by sinning became fleshly even in his mind*. But from the fact that he became fleshly in his mind, as it says in the same passage, he only thinks on things that lead to the spirit through corporeal images. Therefore, too, when he becomes spiritual in his flesh, as promised to the saints after the resurrection, he will also be able to see spiritual things in the flesh. And so the same follows as before.  

Obj. 6: Man can only be made blessed by God. But he will be made blessed not only as regards his soul but also his body. Therefore, he will be able to see God not only in his intellect but also in his flesh.  

Obj. 7: Just as God is present in the intellect through his essence, so also he will be present in the senses, since he will be *all in all*, as it says in 1 Cor 15:28. But he will be seen by the intellect by virtue of the fact that his essence is joined to it, as was said above. Therefore, he will also be able to be seen by the senses.  

On the contrary (1), AmbroseAmbrose, *On the Gospel of Luke*, ch. 1, n. 27 (PL 15:544). says on Luke: *and God is not sought by corporeal eyes, nor circumscribed by sight, nor grasped by touch*. Therefore, God will not in any way be seen by the physical senses.  

Furthermore (2), JeromeJerome, *On Isaiah*, ch. 6 (PL 24:93). says: *the eyes of the flesh are unable to gaze upon the deity, not only of the Father, but neither of the Son nor of the Holy Spirit; but the eyes of the mind can, of which it is said, 'blessed are the clean of heart.'*  

Furthermore (3), JeromeIbid. again says: *incorporeal things are not seen by corporeal eyes*. But God is maximally incorporeal. Therefore, etc.  

Furthermore (4), AugustineAugustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 11, n. 28. says: *no one has ever seen God as he is, either in this life, or in the life of the angels, in the way that these visible things are discerned by corporeal vision*. But the blessed life is called the life of the angels, in which the resurrected will live. Therefore, etc.  

Furthermore (5), man is said to have been made in the image of God because he can perceive God, as Augustine says.See Augustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28), Bk. 3, ch. 20. But man is in the image of God not according to the flesh but according to the mind. Therefore, he will see God in his mind, not his flesh.  

I answer that, something can be sensed by the physical senses in two ways. In one way, in itself. In the other, incidentally. What is sensed in itself is whatever can introduce a passion to the physical senses by itself. Now, something can introduce a passion by itself either to a sense as sense or to this particular sense precisely as this particular sense. When it introduces a passion to the sense by itself in this second way, it is called a proper sensible object, as for example color is with respect to sight and sound with respect to hearing. But because sense as sense uses a bodily organ, something can be received in it only in a corporeal manner, since everything received in something is in it after the mode of the receiver. And thus all sensible objects introduce a passion to the sense as sense insofar as they have magnitude. And thus magnitude and everything consequent upon it, such as motion, rest, number, and such things, are called common sensibles in themselves alone. Something is sensed incidentally, however, that does not introduce a passion to the senses, either as sense or as this particular sense, but instead is joined to things which introduce a passion to the sense as such; for example, Socrates, the son of Diarius, a friend, and other such things. These are known per se in the universal by the intellect, but in the particular they are known in man in the cogitative power, and in other animals in the estimative power. The external senses are said to sense things of this sort, though incidentally, when from what is sensed per se the apprehensive power, to which it belongs to know what it is known as such, apprehends at once and without doubting or discursion, as when we see that someone is alive from the fact that he is speaking. But when the situation is otherwise, the senses are not said to see such an object, even incidentally.  

I say, therefore, that God can in no way be seen by corporeal sight or sensed with any other sense as visible as such, either here or in heaven. For if what befits a sense precisely as a sense is taken away from the sense, it will not be a sense at all. Likewise, if what belongs to sight precisely as sight is taken away from sight, there will be no sight. Since, then, the sense considered as a sense perceives magnitude, and sight, precisely as this kind of sense, perceives color, it is impossible for sight to perceive something that is neither color nor magnitude, unless sense is spoken of equivocally. Since, therefore, sight and sense are going to be the same specifically in the glorified body, it will not be possible for them to see the divine essence as visible as such. But it will see it as visible incidentally, when on the one hand corporeal sight will see God’s great glory in bodies, especially glorified bodies, and most of all the body of Christ, and on the other hand, the intellect will see God so clearly that God will be perceived in things seen corporeally, as life is perceived in the fact of speaking. For even though our intellect will not then see God from creation, it will see him in created things seen corporeally. And this is the manner in which God can be seen corporeally that AugustineAugustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 22, ch. 29, n. 6. posits at the end of the *City of God*, as is clear from his words to one who reads them closely. He says: *it is quite believable that in that state we will see the worldly bodies of the new heavens and the new earth in such a way that we will see with the clearest acuity God present everywhere, governing all bodies, not as we now perceive intellectually the invisible things of God through the things that have been made, but rather in the way that when we look at men we do not have to believe that they are alive but instead see it at once*.  

Reply Obj. 1: This passage of Job is understood as about the spiritual eye, about which the Apostle says: *the eyes of your heart enlightened* (Eph 1:18).  

Reply Obj. 2: This text is not understood to mean that we are going to see God through the eyes of flesh but rather that, being ourselves in the flesh, we will see God.  

Reply Obj. 3: In those words Augustine is speaking by way of inquiry and conditionally, as is clear from what comes before: *and so these eyes will have a different power by far if that incorporeal nature will be seen through them*. And later he adds: *and consequently, the power*, and afterwards makes a determination, as has been said.  

Reply Obj. 4: All knowledge comes through an abstraction from matter. And thus the more a corporeal form is abstracted from matter, the more it is the principle of knowledge. And so it is that a form existing in matter is in no way the principle of knowledge, but a form existing in the senses is in a way the principle of knowledge, as being separated from matter, and a form in our intellect is better still. And thus the spiritual eye, from which the impediment to knowledge is removed, can see a corporeal thing. It does not follow, however, that the corporeal eye, in which the cognoscitive power falls short to the extent that it participates in matter, can know perfectly the incorporeal things that can be known.  

Reply Obj. 5: Even though a mind that has been rendered carnal can only think about things received from the senses, it still thinks about them in an immaterial way. Likewise, what sight apprehends it must always apprehend in a corporeal manner. Hence it cannot know things that cannot be apprehended corporeally.  

Reply Obj. 6: Beatitude is the perfection of man precisely as man. And because man possesses the fact of being man not from the body but rather from the soul, though the body pertains to man’s essence inasmuch as it is perfected by the soul, man’s beatitude consists principally only in an act of the soul. And from the soul beatitude is channeled into the body through a kind of overflowing, as is clear from what was said.Distinction 44, Question 2, Article 4. But there will be a kind of beatitude of our body inasmuch as it will see God in sensible creation, especially in Christ’s body.  

Reply Obj. 7: The intellect can perceive spiritual things, but physical sight cannot. And thus the intellect will be able to know the divine essence joined to it, but the physical sight will not.  

Article 3  

***Whether by seeing God they will comprehend him***

Obj. 1: To the third question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 7. It seems that in heaven the saints, by seeing God, comprehend him: *so run that you may comprehend* (1 Cor 9:24). Therefore, the comprehension of God is the prize for virtue that is awarded to the saints.  

Obj. 2: In *On Seeing God*, AugustineAugustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 9. says: *something is wholly comprehended by seeing it when it is seen in such a way that nothing of it is hidden to the one seeing*. But if God is seen by someone, nothing of him is hidden to the one seeing. For since he is simple, it cannot be the case that something of him is seen and something else is hidden. Therefore, God is comprehended by anyone who sees him.  

Obj. 3: Everything that is seen completely and wholly is comprehended. But the whole of God is seen wholly by those who see his essence, since the whole of God is nothing but his essence. Moreover, any mode of his is his essence. Nor can his essence be seen in any way except as a whole and wholly. Therefore, all who see his essence comprehend him.  

Obj. 4: As it says in *On the Soul* 3,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 430a2–6. the intellect understands itself and other things in the same way. But, as AugustineAugustine, *The Book of Eighty-Three Questions* (CCSL 44a), q. 15. says in the *Book of Eighty-Three Questions*: *everything that understands itself comprehends itself*. Therefore, everything that understands another comprehends it. But anyone who sees the divine essence understands it. Therefore, he comprehends it.  

Obj. 5: Whenever what something is is known, it is comprehended, since then it is known, as it were, in its definition. Now something is comprehended when its boundaries can be seen all around, as Augustine says in *On Seeing God*.Augustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina*, par. 9 (CSEL 44:296). But when something’s essence is seen, what it is is known. Therefore, anyone who sees the essence of God comprehends him.  

Obj. 6: The quantity of an action follows on the quantity of the form, which is the principle of the action. For the quantity of heating follows on the quantity of the heat. But the form that is the principle of knowledge is what a knower knows by. Therefore, the mode of knowledge follows on the mode of what the thing is known by. But what God is known by is nothing other than his essence, as was said above, and this is infinite. Therefore, the vision is also infinite and thus the divine essence can be comprehended, even though it is infinite.  

Obj. 7: If the divine essence cannot be comprehended, this is only because of its excessive brightness. But a knowable excess impedes knowledge inasmuch as it corrupts the cognoscitive potency, which occurs only in the senses but not in the intellect. This is clear from what is said in *On the Soul* 3,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 429a29–429b5. that one who understands the greatest things is not less able to understand the least things but is, rather, more able to understand them, whereas the contrary occurs in the senses. Since, then, the divine essence is only seen by the intellect, as has been said, it seems that the excess of divine brightness does not impede comprehension of the divine essence.  

Obj. 8: The divine essence can be comprehended by an intellect, since God comprehends himself. If, then, it cannot be comprehended by the human intellect, this will be due to a defect of that intellect. But the light of glory takes away every intellectual defect, since through glory every bodily defect is also taken away. Therefore, those who will see God in the light of glory will comprehend him.  

On the contrary (1), in *On Seeing God* AugustineAugustine, *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 8. says: *no one ever comprehends the fullness of God, not only with the eyes of the body but even with the mind itself*.  

Furthermore (2), as AugustineAugustine, *The Book of Eighty-Three Questions* (CCSL 44a), q. 15. says in the *Book of Eighty-Three Questions: what comprehends itself is finite to itself*. Therefore, what is comprehended by something else is also finite to it. But God cannot be finite to any created intellect. Therefore, no created intellect can comprehend God.  

Furthermore (3), DamasceneJohn Damascene, *On the Orthodox Faith*, Bk. 1, ch. 5 (PG 94:799). says in Book 1: *for those who believe divine Scripture there is no doubt that the substance of God is incomprehensible*.  

Furthermore (4), everything that comprehends something is either greater than or equal to it. But the created intellect cannot be greater than God or even equal to him. Therefore, it cannot comprehend him.  

I answer that, to ‘comprehend’ is said as if ‘taking together,’ i.e., taking hold of. And thus something is properly comprehended when it is taken hold of together, i.e., with everything that belongs to it. For this reason everything that is comprehended must be enclosed in the comprehensor. But what is contained is properly enclosed in what contains it. And thus what is comprehended must be contained in the comprehensor. Now just as something is said to be contained in something else physically when it does not exceed the container by its dimensive quantity on any side, like wine in a jar, so also something is said to be contained by something else spiritually when it lies under its power and exceeds it in no respect. And thus something is said to be comprehended by knowledge when what is known lies under the act of the cognoscitive power and does not exceed it.  

Now every excess refers to a quantity. And the knowable is said to exceed the cognoscitive power according to the amount that the knowable is knowable by it. The sensible, however, is known both according to dimensive quantity, because the senses use a bodily organ in knowing by reason of which they know all sensible things that are reduced to dimensive quantity, and also according to quantity of power, as is seen in the proper objects of sense, which are qualities. And thus, too, the senses’ comprehension is impeded both because of an excess of dimensive quantity, as one is impeded from comprehending the whole earth, and also due to an excess of quantity of power, as one is impeded from comprehending the brightness of the sun. For the eye is not as powerful in its ability to know as is the sun’s brightness, which is knowable. Now, what is intelligible is not known by the intellect under the aspect of dimensive quantity except incidentally, namely inasmuch as it is received from the senses. From this it follows that it understands with the continuous and time. And in this regard the intellect is impeded from comprehending the intelligible due to the excess of quantity, as it is impeded from comprehending a line or an infinite number. But speaking *per se*, the intelligible is compared to the intellect under the aspect of quantity of power from the fact that the proper object of the intellect is the what. And thus in things separated from the senses the intellect’s comprehension is only impeded through excess of quantity of power. And this occurs when the intelligible object is more knowable than the intellect knows or can know. Just as one who knows the conclusion that a triangle has three angles equal to two right angles Euclid’s *Elements*, Bk. 1, prop. 32. on the basis of a probable argument, namely because people commonly say this, does not comprehend this fact. And this is not because he sees part of it and does not see another part but rather because the way in which he knows it falls short of the way in which it can be known, for it can be known by demonstration.  

Now the divine essence is knowable through its own truth, and the intellect can know through the intellectual light that is in it. But the truth of the divine essence exceeds any light of a created intellect. Thus it is impossible for it to see God as perfectly as he is seeable. And from this it follows that no created intellect can comprehend God.  

Reply Obj. 1: The Apostle is speaking of the comprehension of glory, i.e., that by which the soul becomes able to hold in itself all that pertains to the perfection of glory. And to this extent it is said to comprehend glory, though it never comprehends the essence intellectually. Or else in this passage arriving at the possession of God is called comprehension, inasmuch as comprehension is said to succeed to the place of hope.  

Reply Obj. 2: Augustine includes two elements in the definition of comprehension. One of these excludes the excess of the knowable above the power of the knower with respect to dimensive quantity. In this regard he says: *when it is seen such that nothing of it is hidden to the one seeing*. The other excludes excess with respect to quantity of power. With regard to this he says: *or whose boundaries can be seen on every side*. For a thing’s boundaries are seen on every side when the one seeing it reaches the end of vision by seeing perfectly.  

Reply Obj. 3: The saints will see the whole divine essence in heaven, but they will not see it completely; not because there will be any mode in God that they do not see or any mode in their intellect that is not turned toward God, but rather because the mode by which they are turned toward knowing God is not equal to the mode by which God can be known. And it is this inequality that is shown when it is said that the divine essence will not be seen wholly. For the adverb ‘wholly’ indicates neither absolutely the mode of the one seeing nor absolutely the mode of the thing seen but instead the mode of the one seeing by way of comparison to the thing seen. For this adverb modifies the seeing itself in accord with which the one seeing is compared to what is seen.  

Reply Obj. 4: When something understands itself, the knower and the known have the same mode. And thus if it understands itself it comprehends itself. But the knower and the known do not have the same mode when the intellect understands something else. Thus the case is not similar.  

Reply Obj. 5: Someone knows what a thing is in the same way that he knows the thing’s essence, since the essence is the thing’s quiddity. And thus only one who comprehends a thing’s essence comprehends what it is. Hence just as the saints will see the divine essence but not comprehend it, so also they will see what God is but not comprehend this. And thus God will not be seen by them in the way that a thing is seen through its definition and whose essence is thus comprehended.  

Reply Obj. 6: Action is attributed not to form alone, which is the principle of action, but to the composite, just as heating is attributed to what is hot, not to heat alone. And thus we must measure an action not only with reference to the form, which is the principle of the action, but also with reference to the form’s subject. But a form that is not subsistent by itself has no other mode from the mode of the subject, since it only has being inasmuch as it is the act of such a subject. And thus the measure of the form is the measure of the composite. But a form that is subsistent by itself has a mode inasmuch as it is a given subsistent thing and another mode to the extent that it is the act of such a subject. And this mode is taken after the measure by which the perfectible attains to being perfected by such a form, and it is on the basis of this mode that the action is measured. Now the divine essence is subsistent by itself. And thus the vision that comes to the created intellect through it is measured not according to the infinite mode that belongs to the essence itself in its own right but rather according to the mode by which the intellect attains to being perfected through it. But it attains to this inasmuch as it is joined to it through the light of glory, which is finite. And thus the vision is finite on the part of the one seeing.  

Reply Obj. 7: The truth of God’s essence, through which he is knowable, is called his brightness, just as the sun is knowable through its brightness. And even though it does not impede comprehension by corruption of the intellect, it does impede it from its very excess, since it cannot be the case that a created intellect attains to knowing the divine essence as perfectly as it can be known.  

Reply Obj. 8: Glory perfects nature and does not destroy it. And thus the light of glory does not take away an imperfection that pertains to the very character of the nature, such as the fact that it is created from nothing. Yet from this very fact the created intellect falls short of the possibility of comprehension. And thus it is unable to arrive at comprehension even through the light of glory.  

Article 4  

Whether some of those who see God in his essence will see him more perfectly than others  

Obj. 1: To the fourth question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 6. It seems some of those who see God in his essence do not see him more perfectly than others. For, as AugustineAugustine, *The Book of Eighty-Three Questions* (CCSL 44a), q. 32. says in the *Book of Eighty-Three Questions*, one cannot understand the same thing more than another. But the divine essence will be seen only intellectually. Therefore, one will not see it more fully than another.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, the more perfectly something is seen, the more express is the likeness of the thing seen in the one who sees it. But there will not be a more express likeness by which God is seen in one person than in another of those who see God in his essence, since the essence is the same in all of them. Therefore, one person will not see better than another.  

Obj. 3: Someone from whom impediments to vision are more removed sees better. But no impediment to vision will remain in anyone. Therefore, all will see equally.  

Obj. 4: All the saints *shall see God as he is* (1 Jn 3:2). But he cannot be seen better than he is. Therefore, one will not see better than another.  

Obj. 5: The best mode of knowing God is that by which he is seen in his essence. But there cannot be anything better than the best. Therefore, of those who see God in his essence, one does not see better than another.  

On the contrary (1), humans are taken up into the lot of the angels. But the higher angels see God more clearly than the lower angels, as is clear from Dionysius in the *Celestial Hierarchy*.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, chs. 5, 8, and 10 (PG 3:195ff.). Therefore, in glory some will see more clearly than others in the case of human beings, too.  

Furthermore (2), man’s beatitude consists essentially in the vision of God. But some will be more blessed than others, as was said above. Therefore, some will see more clearly than others.  

Furthermore (3), the saints will see God in the light of glory, as it is said: *in thy light do we see light* (Ps 35:10). But some will be filled with greater light than others. *For star differs from star in brightness*. *So is it with the resurrection of the dead*, as it is said in 1 Corinthians 15:41–42. But the body’s brightness will indicate the mind’s brightness. Therefore some will see God more clearly than others.  

I answer that, since the principle of action is the form by which the action is carried out, it is on the part of the form that we must take the measure of the action. But the case is different in subsistent forms than in other forms, as was said before. For in other forms there is quantity of strength in the action on the basis of the quantity of what is received of the form in the subject, such as what is received of heat in what is hot. In subsistent forms, however, there is quantity of strength to the extent that the subject is more or less subject to the form.Thomas speaks here of a *quantitas fortitudinis* rather than his more usual expression *quantitas virtualis*. Now a cognoscitive potency is subject to the form by which it knows with reference to its possessing the power of knowing. For what makes sight able to see is what makes it receptive to the likeness of color. Now just as the potential intellect is rendered able to know natural objects of knowledge through the light of the agent intellect, so also through the superadded light of glory it is rendered able to see the divine essence. For this reason the mode by which our intellect lies under the divine essence as an intelligible form is taken according to the quantity of the received light. Consequently, so is the mode of perfection in operation, of which such a form is the principle, namely in the vision of God in his essence. And thus, since some saints are filled with a greater light of glory than others, some will see more clearly than others.  

Reply Obj. 1: One person cannot understand the same thing more than another if the word ‘more’ implies the mode on the part of the thing understood. For a person can only understand a thing by understanding that the thing has the mode that it has. For if someone attributes another mode to the thing, he errs and does not understand it. And this is how Augustine understands it. But if the word ‘more’ indicates the mode on the part of the one understanding, then in this case one can understand better than another inasmuch as his knowledge of one and the same knowable object is keener.  

Reply Obj. 2: Even though one saint does not have in his intellect a more express likeness of God by which he knows him than another saint does, since they all see God in his essence, the intellect of one does lie more perfectly under the form by which it understands than does the intellect of another. And thus one understands more perfectly than another.  

Reply Obj. 3: The intensity according to which something is called greater or less is caused not only by withdrawal from the contrary but also by approach to the terminus, as is evident in the case of intensity of corporeal light. And so also spiritual light is intensified in one of the blessed more than in another through a greater assimilation to God, even though nothing contrary to the light is found in any of them.  

Reply Obj. 4: When it is said that *all the saints will see God as he is*, the word ‘as’ implies the mode on the part of what is seen, not on the part of the one seeing. For everyone will see that God is in that mode in which he is. Even so, the mode of the one seeing will not be equal to the mode of the thing seen but rather at an infinite distance. And thus the mode of one who sees can be less distant than the mode of another.  

Reply Obj. 5: Seeing God in his essence is, according to genus, the most perfect mode of knowing God there is. But within this genus greater and less can still be found, just as fire is the most subtle of corporeal things in its species, though one fire is found to be more subtle than another.  

Article 5  

Whether it is necessary for those who see the divine essence to see everything that God sees  

Obj. 1: To the fifth question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 8; q. 106, a. 1, ad 1; *In II Sent.* d. 11, q. 2, a. 2; *In III Sent.* d. 14, a. 2, qa. 2; *In IV Sent.* d. 45, q. 3, a. 1; *De veritate,* q. 8, a. 4; q. 20, aa. 4 & 5; *SCG* III, chs. 56 & 59. It seems that the saints who see God in his essence see everything that God sees in himself. For, as Isidore says in *On the Highest Good*,Isidore, *On the Highest Good*, Bk. 1, ch. 10, n. 17 (PL 83:556). the angels know all things in the Word of God before they happen. But the saints will be equal to the angels, as is clear from Matthew 22:30. Therefore, the saints will also see everything by seeing God.  

Obj. 2: Gregory says: *for there all behold God with a common clarity, and what is there that they do not know in that place where they know the one who knows all?*Gregory the Great, *Dialogues* (SC 265), Bk. 4, ch. 33. But he is speaking of the blessed who see God in his essence. Therefore, one who sees God in his essence knows all things.  

Obj. 3: As it says in *On the Soul* 3,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 429a29–429b5. by being able to understand the greatest things, the intellect is also more able to understand the least. But God is the greatest intelligible object. Therefore, he increases the intellect’s power for understanding most of all. Therefore, the intellect that sees God understands everything.  

Obj. 4: The intellect is only impeded from understanding something if it is overcome by it. But no created thing overcomes the intellect that sees God. For, as Gregory says in the *Dialogues* 2,Gregory the Great, *Dialogues* (SC 260), Bk. 2, ch. 35. *all creation becomes small to the soul that sees the creator*. Therefore, those who see God in his essence know everything.  

Obj. 5: Every passive potency that is not reduced to act is imperfect. But in the potential intellect of the human soul there is, as it were, a passive potency to know all things. For the potential intellect is how it can become all things, as it says in *On the Soul* 3.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, chs. 4, 5, and 8. If, then, in beatitude it did not understand all things, it would remain imperfect, which is absurd.  

Obj. 6: Whoever sees a mirror sees the things reflected in the mirror. But in the Word of God all things are reflected as in a mirror. For the Word itself is the pattern and likeness of all things. Therefore, the saints who see the Word in his essence see all created things.  

Obj. 7: As it is said: *the desire of the righteous will be granted* (Prov 10:24). But the saints desire to know all things. For all men by nature desire to know,See Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1. and nature is not taken away by glory. Therefore, God will give them knowledge of all things.  

Obj. 8: Ignorance is a kind of penalty belonging to the present life. But every penalty will be taken away from the saints by glory. Therefore, so will all ignorance. And thus they will know all things.  

Obj. 9: The saints’ beatitude is more primarily in the soul than in the body. But the saints’ bodies will be reformed in glory after the likeness to Christ’s body, as is clear from Philippians 3:21. Therefore, their souls will also be perfected after a likeness to Christ’s soul. But Christ’s soul sees all things in the Word, as has been said in Book III, Distinction 14, Question 1, Article 2, quaestiuncula 2. Therefore, all the souls of the saints will also see all things in the Word.  

Obj. 10: Like the senses, the intellect also knows every thing with whose likeness it is informed. But the divine essence indicates any thing in a more express manner than any other likeness to that thing. Therefore, since in that blessed vision the divine essence becomes like a form of our intellect, it seems that the saints who see God see all things.  

Obj. 11: The Commentator says about *On the Soul* 3Averroes, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, comment 36. that if the agent intellect were the form of the potential intellect then we would understand all things. But the divine essence more clearly represents all things than the agent intellect. Therefore, the intellect that sees God in his essence knows all things.  

Obj. 12: Due to the fact that the lower angels do not at this time know all things, they are illuminated about what they do not know by the higher angels. But after the day of judgment an angel will not illuminate another angel. For at that time all preeminence will cease, as a Gloss*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:547. says on 1 Corinthians 15. Therefore, the lower angels will then know all things, and for the same reason all other saints who see God in his essence.  

On the contrary (1), as Dionysius says,Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 8 (PG 3:238). the higher angels purify the lower angels of unknowing. But the lower angels see the divine essence. Therefore, an angel who sees the divine essence is able to not know certain things. But the soul will not see God more perfectly than an angel. Therefore, the souls that see God need not see all things.  

Furthermore (2), only Christ has the Spirit without measure, as it is said (Jn 3:34). But it belongs to Christ, inasmuch as he has the Spirit without measure, to know all things in the Word. This is why it says in the same passage that the Father has given all things into his hand. Therefore, it belongs to no one but Christ to know all things in the Word.  

Furthermore (3), the more perfectly a principle is known, the more of its effects are known through it. But some of those who see God,who is the principle of all things, in his essence, will know God more perfectly than others. Therefore, some will know more things than others. And thus not all will know all things.  

I answer that, God, by seeing his own essence, knows all the things that are or will be or have been. And this is called knowing by the knowledge of vision, since he knows these things as if present after a likeness to corporeal vision. Moreover, by seeing his own essence, he knows all things that he can do, even though he has never done them nor is going to do them. Otherwise, he would not perfectly know his own power. For a power cannot be known unless the objects of the power are known. And this is called knowing by the knowledge of simple intelligence. Now it is impossible for a created intellect, by seeing the divine essence, to know all the things that God can do. For the more perfectly a principle is known, the more things are known in it, as in a single principle of a demonstration someone who has a more perspicacious intelligence sees more conclusions than someone else who has a slower intelligence. Since, then, we take account of the quantity of the divine power on the basis of the things of which it is capable, if an intellect sees in the divine essence all the things that God can do, it would have the same quantity of perfection in its understanding as the quantity of the divine power for producing effects. And thus it would comprehend the divine essence, which is impossible for every created intellect. All those things that God knows with the knowledge of vision, however, can be known by a created intellect; namely, they are known in the Word by the soul of Christ, as was said in Book III, Distinction 14.  

But as regards others who see the divine essence there are two opinions. For some say that all who see God in his essence see all the things that God sees with the knowledge of vision. But this is repugnant to the statements of the saints, who hold that there are some things the angels do not know, though it is obvious according to the faith that they all see God in his essence.  

And thus others say that, apart from Christ, although they see God in his essence, they do not see everything that God sees, due to the fact that they do not comprehend the divine essence. For it is not necessary for one who knows the cause to know all its effects, unless he comprehends the cause, which does not belong to a created intellect. And thus each of those who see God in his essence perceives more things in God’s essence the more clearly he beholds his essence. And so it is that one can instruct another about these things. And so the knowledge of the angels and the holy souls can be increased up till the day of judgment, as can other things pertaining to their accidental reward. But afterward it will not progress further. For then it will be the final state of things and in that state it will be possible for all to know all the things that God knows by knowledge of vision.  

Reply Obj. 1: What Isidore says here, that in the Word, the angels know all things before they come to be, cannot refer to everything that God knows by the knowledge of simple intelligence alone, since these things will never come to be. Rather, it must refer only to the things that God knows by the knowledge of vision. About these he also says that not all the angels know all these things but, perhaps, only some of them do, and even those who know them all do not know them all perfectly. For we can consider many intelligible aspects in a single thing, such as its different properties and relationships to other things. And it is possible for one to perceive more aspects than another by means of the same thing known in common to the two. This is also why Dionysius saysPseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 7, n. 3 (PG 3:218). that the lower angels are taught the knowable aspects of things by the higher. And thus, too, the angels who know all creation need not participate in all the things that can be known in it.  

Reply Obj. 2: These words of Gregory show that in the beatific vision there is a sufficiency for the perception of all things on the part of the divine essence, which is the medium by which there is vision, through which God sees all things. But the fact that not all things are seen is due to a deficiency on the side of the created intellect, which does not comprehend the divine essence.  

Reply Obj. 3: The created intellect does not see the divine essence according to the essence’s proper mode but rather according to its own mode, which is finite. This is why it is not necessary that this vision broaden its efficacy in knowing infinitely to the knowledge of all things.  

Reply Obj. 4: Deficiency of knowledge proceeds not only from the knowable exceeding the intellect but also from the fact that a notion that can be united with the intellect is not so united with it, as vision sometimes does not see a stone from the fact that the appearance of the stone is not united with our vision. But even though the divine essence itself, which is the pattern of all things, is united with the intellect of the one who sees God, it is not united with it as being the pattern of all things but rather to the extent that it is the pattern of some. And one who beholds the divine essence more fully perceives more of these.  

Reply Obj. 5: When a passive potency can be perfected by more than one ordered perfection, if it is perfected by its ultimate perfection it is not called imperfect, even if it lacks some of the preceding dispositions. But all knowledge by which the created intellect is perfected is ordered to the knowledge of God as to an end. This is why someone who sees God in his essence, even if he knew nothing else, would have a perfected intellect. Nor is it rendered more perfect by knowing anything else together with God, except as leading to fuller vision of him. Thus Augustine saysAugustine, *Confessions* (CCSL 27), Bk. 5, ch. 4. in his *Confessions* 5: *unhappy is that man who knows everything*, i.e., all creatures, *without knowing you, but blessed is he who knows you even if he knows them not. Indeed one is not more blessed for knowing both you and them but rather is blessed on your account alone*.  

Reply Obj. 6: That ‘mirror’ is possessed of a will and so just as it shows itself to whom it wills, so also it shows in itself what it wills. And this is not similar to a material mirror, which does not have the power to be seen or not to be seen. Hence we must say that in a material mirror both things and the mirror itself are seen under their proper form, even though the mirror is seen through the received form while a stone is seen through its own form being reflected in another thing. And thus the first is known through the same aspect as the other. But in the uncreated Mirror a thing is seen through the form of the Mirror itself, as an effect is seen by way of likeness to the cause and vice versa. And thus it is not necessary for anyone who sees the eternal Mirror to see everything that is reflected in the Mirror. For it is not necessary for one who sees the cause to see every one of its effects, unless he comprehends the cause.  

Reply Obj. 7: The desire of the saints by which they desire to know all things will be fulfilled by seeing God alone, just as their desire by which they wish to have all good things will be fulfilled by possessing God. For just as God is sufficient for the affections in the fact that he possesses perfect goodness and all good things are in a certain way possessed by possessing him, so also seeing him suffices for the intellect: *Lord, show us the Father, and we shall be satisfied* (Jn 14:8).  

Reply Obj. 8: ‘Ignorance’ taken in the proper sense means privation. And in this way it is a punishment. For in this way ignorance is not knowing things that ought to be known or that it is necessary to know. But the saints will lack knowledge of none of these things in heaven. Sometimes, however, ‘ignorance’ is taken in a common sense for all unknowing. Thus DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 7 (PG 3:206). says that the angels are purged of unknowing. But ignorance in this way is not a penalty but rather a kind of lack. In this way, too, it is not necessary for every such deficiency to be taken away by glory. For by the same reasoning it could also be said that it is a lack in Linus that he has not reached the glory of Peter.  

Reply Obj. 9: Our body will be conformed to Christ’s body in glory by way of likeness, not equality. For it will be brilliant like Christ’s body, though not equally. Likewise, too, our soul will possess glory after likeness to Christ’s soul, but not in equal measure. And thus our soul will also have knowledge like Christ’s soul, but not as much, i.e., it will not know all things as does Christ’s soul.  

Reply Obj. 10: Even though the divine essence is the pattern of all things that can be known, it will not be joined to any created intellect as being the pattern of all things. And thus the argument does not follow.  

Reply Obj. 11: The agent intellect is the form proportionate to the potential intellect, as also the potency of matter is proportionate to the potency of the natural agent, in such a way that everything that is in the passive potency of matter or the potential intellect is in the active potency of the agent intellect or the natural agent. And thus if the agent intellect were the form of the potential intellect, then the potential intellect would have to know all the things to which the power of the agent intellect extends. But the divine essence is not proportionate to our intellect in this way. And thus the case is not similar.  

Reply Obj. 12: Nothing prevents our saying that after the day of judgment, when the glory of men and angels will be thoroughly consummated, all the blessed will know everything that God knows by the knowledge of vision, though in such a way that not everyone sees everything in the divine essence. Rather, the soul of Christ will see everything there fully, as it now sees it, whereas others will see there more or fewer things according to the degree to which they know God. And so the soul of Christ will illuminate all other souls concerning the things that it sees more than others in the Word. Hence it says in Rev 21:23 that the brightness of God illuminates the city of the blessed and the Lamb is its lamp. Likewise, too, the higher angels will illuminate the lower, not by a new illumination as if the knowledge of the lower would be increased by this, but rather by a kind of continuation of the illumination, just as if we understood the sun to illuminate the air while being at rest. And thus it is said: *those who turn many to righteousness shall shine like the stars for ever and ever* (Dan 12:3). But it is said that preeminence of order will cease as regards the things now carried out concerning us through their ordained ministries, as is clear from a Gloss on the same passage.  

Article 6  

Whether any created being can arrive at seeing God in his essence from purely natural endowments  

Obj. 1: To the sixth question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 4. It seems that a created intellect can see God in his essence from its own proper natural endowments. For, according to the opinion of many, the angels were created in a state of natural endowments alone. But the angels at the beginning of their creation saw things in the Word, as Augustine says in the *Literal Meaning of Genesis*.Augustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28), Bk. 2, ch. 8. And this is called their morning knowledge. Therefore, a created intellect can see God in his essence from its own proper natural endowments.  

Obj. 2: The good and wicked angels have nothing in common except natural endowments. But the wicked angels see God in his essence, as is seen from what we have in Job 1:6: *the sons of God came to present themselves before the Lord, and Satan also came among them*. But it is the angels that enjoy the most intimate contemplation who are called those who stand before the throne, according to Gregory.Gregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143), Bk. 2, par. 3. Therefore, the angels are able to see God in his essence from their own proper natural endowments.  

Obj. 3: One who can see what is less intelligible can see also what is more intelligible, as the Commentator says about *On the Soul* 3.Averroes, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, comment 7. But the divine essence is more intelligible than these material objects that are only intelligible if we render them so, whereas God, since he is altogether free from matter, is intelligible by himself. Since, then, we see material creation from our own proper natural endowments, it seems that all the more can we see God in his essence from our proper natural endowments. And this seems especially true of the angels.  

Obj. 4: As it says in *On the Soul* 3: *the intellect is to the intelligible as sight is to the visible*.See Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 429a18: “Thought must be related to what is thinkable, as sense is to what is sensible.” Now sight is only impeded from vision in one of four ways. In one way, it is impeded due to the corruption of the sight, as when there is a mark on the pupil. This cannot be said of the angelic intellect even in the state of natural endowments, since they are clean and clearest mirrors, as Dionysius says.Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 4, n. 22 (PG 3:723). In another way, it is impeded due to the absence of the visible object. This, too, cannot be said of the case in question, since God is present to every intellect because he fills all things. In a third way, from the fact that the visible object is not actually visible, as when colors are in darkness. For they only become actually visible through light. This, too, cannot be said in the case in question, since God is visible by himself because he is intelligible light, as is clear (1 Jn 1:5). In the fourth way, sight is impeded through the excellence of the object, which corrupts the senses. This, too, has no place in the case in question. For the excellences of intelligible things do not corrupt the intellect in the way that the excellences of sensible objects corrupt the senses, as it says in *On the Soul* 3.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 429a29–429b5. Therefore, there is nothing to impede a created intellect from being able to see God in his essence from its own proper natural endowments.  

Obj. 5: Each thing is known through its likeness. But an angel by its own nature is the express likeness of God himself, as Dionysius says,Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 4, n. 22 (PG 3:723). asserting that an angel is *the image of God, the manifestation of that hidden light*, i.e., the divine light. Therefore, an angel can see God in his essence through its own proper nature.  

Obj. 6: It says in the *Book of Causes**Book of Causes*, prop. 8. that every intelligence understands what is above it inasmuch as it is caused by it. But every created intellect is in its own nature caused by God. Therefore, it can see God in his essence by its own natural endowments.  

Obj. 7: It pertains to the immensity of the divine liberality to communicate to a created being everything of which it is capable. But a created being is capable of receiving the gift of seeing God in his essence by its own natural endowments. For it can attain to seeing God in his essence through the light of glory, and this light is like something superadded to nature in such a way that it could be implanted in nature by God. Therefore, it has been conferred on a created being that from its own proper natural endowments it attains to seeing God in his essence.  

Obj. 8: The Commentator says at the beginning of the *Metaphysics*Averroes, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 2, last comment. that if there were an intelligible object that no intellect understood then it would be intelligible in vain. But it is absurd to say that the divine essence is intelligible in vain. Therefore, there is an intellect that can see it by its own nature.  

On the contrary (1), it is said: *the grace of God is life eternal* (Rom 6:23). But eternal life is seeing God in his essence, as is clear: *this is eternal life, that they know thee the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom thou hast sent* (Jn 17:3). Therefore, it is only by grace that anyone can arrive at seeing God in his essence.  

Furthermore (2), what is given to charity as a reward cannot be arrived at by natural endowments, since even charity itself is above nature. But the vision of God in his essence is given to charity as a reward, as is clear from Scripture: *he who loves me will be loved by my Father, and I will love him and manifest myself to him* (Jn 14:21). Therefore, the created intellect cannot arrive at the vision of God in his essence from its own proper natural endowments.  

Furthermore (3), nature together with grace is stronger than nature alone. But the saints when they were in limbo, even though they had grace, did not see God in his essence. Hence it is said in their person: *what manner of joy shall be to me, who sit in darkness and see not the light of heaven?* (Tob 5:12) Therefore, much less can anyone attain to this from purely natural endowments.  

Furthermore (4), the order of causes follows the order of effects. For a more noble effect has a more noble cause. But among all the things that can be bestowed on a mere creation, seeing God in his essence is the greatest. Therefore, the cause of this cannot be nature but rather God alone, who is the highest cause of all things.  

I answer that, for the intellect to see God in his essence, the divine essence must be united to our intellect in a certain way as an intelligible form. But a form is only joined to what is perfectible when the perfectible has the dispositions by which it is rendered capable of receiving such a form, as the body is only united to the soul when in the body there are fitting dispositions for the reception of the soul. Hence for the intellect to be united to the divine essence in the aforementioned mode there must be in it something after the manner of a disposition preparing it for the aforementioned union. And this is the light by which the intellect is perfected for the vision of God in the divine essence.  

But this disposition cannot be natural to a created intellect. For the final disposition for a form, for the same reason, is received both in what is perfectible and the form itself in such a way that if one is natural, the other must also be natural. For what can be perfected attains to the form through this final disposition to the form. But the form that is the divine essence exceeds every natural capability and capacity. For a potency and its proper act are always reckoned in the same genus. Hence potency and act divide each genus of being, as is clear in the *Physics* 3.Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 3, ch. 1, 200b25–31. And thus the capability or potency of a created thing only extends to a form belonging to its own genus. And so the divine essence, which is outside every genus, exceeds the natural capability of any given created intellect. And thus the final disposition for the union of the intellect with such an essence exceeds every capability of nature. Hence it cannot be natural but is, rather, above nature. And this disposition is the light of glory, of which it is said: *in thy light do we see light* (Ps 35:10). The created intellect can, however, arrive at the knowledge of God in accord with its natural capability through the created forms implanted in it or acquired by it. But this knowledge is not the vision of him in his essence, as is clear from what was said, nor through this knowledge is it known what he is, but only that he is and what he is not.  

Reply Obj. 1: The angels in their first condition did not see the Word in his essence, though they did see him through a created likeness, which is their very nature, since it is a likeness to God. And this is the case whether we posit that they were created in grace or not, as long as we do not posit that they were created in glory. Hence, too, evening knowledge, by which they knew themselves in their proper nature, in them preceded morning knowledge, by which they know things in the Word, as is clear from the fact that the first day has evening without morning.  

Reply Obj. 2: The devil is not said to have stood before the Lord as if seeing him in his essence. Rather, this is said because the devil was seen by God and God’s providence extended to him just as to the good angels, after the manner in which we say that those things are at hand to us that lie subject to our providence. Hence ‘standing before’ is taken in an equivocal sense.  

Reply Obj. 3: It is possible for something to be more intelligible in itself while being less intelligible to some intellect, as is manifestly true in the case of our own intellect. For the capability of our intellect is determined to sensible forms that are rendered actually understood through the agent intellect, by virtue of the fact that the phantasms are related to our intellect as sensible things are to the senses, as it says in *On the Soul* 3.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 7. And thus our intellect must naturally be gently led through sensible forms to whatever it understands, as Dionysius also says.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 1 (PG 3:119). And because separated substances, which are most intelligible in themselves, surpass the genus of sensible forms, our intellect is found to be too weak to know them. It is for this reason that it says in the *Metaphysics* 2Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 2, ch. 1, 993b9–11. that our intellect is to what is by nature most manifest as the eye of the night owl is to the sun. As for the natural capability of the angelic intellect, even though it is not determined to sensible forms, it is still determined to created forms of its own genus. And thus just as our intellect falls short of the knowledge of separated substances, so also and much more does the angelic intellect fall short of the vision of God in his essence with reference to its natural capability. The Commentator’s statement, however, is understood as referring to created intelligible objects.  

Reply Obj. 4: The created intellect is not impeded from the natural ability to see God in his essence out of a deficiency in the divine essence, as if it were not actually intelligible in its own right, but instead on the part of the created intellect itself. First, on account of the deficiency of the created power of understanding itself, whose natural capability is determined to created being. This deficiency, though, differs in angels and in man, if we consider them with respect to their natural endowments. For in the intellect of an angel a deficiency is not found with reference to its own generic character but rather by comparison to an intellect outside its genus, i.e., the divine intellect. For it possesses every perfection due to the genus of intellect, in which only the created intellect is included, since the uncreated intellect is outside any genus. But the human intellect suffers deficiency even with reference to its generic character, since in it there is intelligible light that is obscured by shadow. Hence Isaac says that reason arises in the shadow of intelligence.J. T. Muckle, "Isaac Israeli Liber de definicionibus," *Archives d'histoire doctrinale et littéraire du Moyen Age* 11 (1937‒38): 313. Second, due to the absence of the intelligible object. For even though the divine essence is present to each and every thing, it is not present to every intellect as an intelligible form, for this only happens when there is in the intellect a sufficient disposition for union to the divine essence as to an intelligible form. Third, due to the excess of the brightness of the divine essence beyond the form by which a created intellect naturally understands. And so the created intellect consequently falls short of seeing God in his essence, even though the immensity of the divine essence does not corrupt but rather strengthens the intellect.  

Reply Obj. 5: A created intellect can arrive at knowledge of God through a created likeness from its natural endowments. But this knowledge is not the vision of God in his essence, as was proved above.  

Reply Obj. 6: The effect retains a likeness to its cause. And from this it follows that an intelligence naturally knows its cause from the likeness to it that it possesses. But this is not seeing God in his essence, as has been said.  

Reply Obj. 7: Not everything that is superadded to a created being can pertain to the constitution of its very nature. For what pertains as proper to a higher nature cannot be communicated to a lower nature in such a way that it would naturally possess it, unless the lower nature is transferred to the higher nature, just as it cannot be communicated to what is transparent that it should shine brightly in the absence of a bright body, unless it itself is made into a bright body, even though light is indeed communicated to it by a bright body. But to be determined to an uncreated intelligible form is a property of the uncreated intellect. And thus the light through which the intellect is united to the uncreated intelligible form can be communicated to a created intellect, but that the created intellect should naturally possess this very light cannot be communicated to it, unless it is transferred to the nature of the uncreated intellect, which is impossible.  

Reply Obj. 8: Even if no intellect understood the divine essence, either by nature or by grace, the divine essence would not be intelligible in vain, both because it understands itself and because it does not exist for the sake of anything else. But for something to be in vain occurs only in the case of things that exist for the sake of something else to which they do not attain.  

Article 7  

***Whether anyone can see God in his essence in the wayfaring state***

Obj. 1: To the seventh question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 11. It seems that God can be seen in his essence in the wayfaring state. For it is said: *I saw the Lord sitting upon a throne high and elevated: and all the earth was filled with his majesty; and the Seraphim cried out* (Is 6:1). But one whose majesty fills all the earth is one who is essentially God. Therefore, Isaiah saw God in his essence while in the wayfaring state.  

Obj. 2: Furthermore, to see God face to face is to see God in his essence, as is clear from 1 Corinthians 13:12, for this is the name the Apostle gives to the blessed vision. But Jacob saw God face to face while he was in the wayfaring state. This is why he says: *I have seen God face to face, and yet my life is preserved* (Gen 32:30). Therefore, someone in the wayfaring state can see God in his essence.  

Obj. 3: Furthermore, *I had heard of thee by the hearing of the ear, but now my eye sees thee* (Job 42:5). But sight concerns things that are present, whereas hearing concerns those that are absent. Therefore, after Job had known God as if absent through hearing, while still in this life he saw God as if present in his essence.  

Obj. 4: Furthermore, it is said of Moses: *plainly and not by riddles does he see the Lord* (Num 12:8). But every vision of God is either by a riddle or in his essence, as is clear (1 Cor 13:12). Therefore, Moses saw God in his essence while he was in the wayfaring state.  

Obj. 5: Furthermore, on the passage: *a man . . . was caught up to the third heaven* (2 Cor 12:2), a GlossFrom Augustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28.1), Bk. 12, par. 28. says that God showed the Apostle the life in which God will be seen forever. And thus the Apostle was caught up to the vision of God in himself, not in any figure. And below it is said that in the third order of angels are thrones, cherubim, and seraphim, who contemplate God more closely. It is after a likeness to them that the Apostle is said to have seen God. From all this it is plainly shown that he saw God in his essence. And so the same follows as before.  

Obj. 6: Furthermore, according to AugustineAugustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28.1), Bk. 12, par. 31. in the *Literal Meaning of Genesis* 12, things that are in the soul by their essence are seen with intellectual vision. But intellectual vision pertains to the intelligible objects themselves, not by way of any likenesses but in their essences, as he says. Therefore, since God is in the soul of a wayfarer by his essence, it seems that the wayfarer sees him in his essence.  

Obj. 7: Furthermore, the affections are more corrupted by sin than the intellect. For sin is in the will, as Augustine says.Augustine, *Retractations*, bk. 1, ch. 15. But the affections can love God immediately in the wayfaring state. Therefore, the intellect can also see God immediately.  

Obj. 8: Everything that the intellect turns itself to without being turned to anything else is seen directly by the intellect. But the intellect of a wayfarer can be turned to God without being turned to something else. Therefore, a wayfarer’s intellect can see God immediately.  

Obj. 9: Furthermore, the means by which we judge of other things must be more known to us than those other things are. But, according to Augustine in *On the True Religion*,Augustine, *On the True Religion*, ch. 30. we judge of all things on the basis of uncreated truth, taking counsel from it in all matters even in the wayfaring state. Therefore, we see this truth. But the uncreated truth is the divine essence. Therefore, in the wayfaring state we can see God in his essence.  

Obj. 10: Furthermore, *that on account of which something is what it is, is even more so*.Aristotle, *Posterior Analytics*, Bk. 2, ch. 15, 72a29. But everything that we know we know through the divine light, which is the divine essence itself. For, as it says in *On Spirit and Soul*,Pseudo-Augustine, *On Spirit and Soul*, ch. 12 (PL 40:787). our mind cannot proceed to the act of knowledge unless it has the divine light poured into it. Therefore, we see God himself in his essence more than we see anything else.  

Obj. 11: According to Augustine,This may be gathered from a number of works of Augustine, but the statement as Thomas cites it is expressly found in the aforementioned *On Spirit and Soul*, ch. 11 (PL 40:786). our mind is informed by God without the interposition of any created thing. But in every mediated vision something else is interposed. Therefore, in this life our mind sees God directly.  

Obj. 12: Furthermore, by any speculative science we know about the subject what it is. But God is the subject in a speculative science, or at least in part of a science, as in theology and metaphysics, which also concerns God, as is clear in the beginning of the *Metaphysics*.See Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 2. Therefore, through a speculative science we know of God what he is. And this is to see him in his essence. Therefore, since we can have all the speculative sciences in the wayfaring state, we are able to see God in his essence in the wayfaring state.  

Obj. 13: Furthermore, nothing is impeded except by its contrary. But the corruption of the body is not contrary to the disposition of the intellect, since they do not occur in the same thing. Therefore, the disposition of the intellect is not impeded by the corruption of the body. But the soul in an incorruptible body will see God in his essence. Therefore, when it is joined to a corruptible body it is also able to see God in his essence.  

On the contrary (1), it is said in Exodus 33:20: *man shall not see me and live*, upon which a GlossFrom Gregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143A), Bk. 18, par. 54. says: *for as long as one is wholly in this life, God can be seen through certain images, but through the very species of his nature he cannot be seen*.  

Furthermore (2), the Gloss upon John 1:18, *no one has ever seen God*, says: *no mere man living in this body sees God as he is*.  

Furthermore (3), commenting on 2 Corinthians 5:6–7: *while we are at home in the body we are away from the Lord, for we walk by faith, not by sight*, a Gloss says: *it cannot happen that a man still leading this mortal life come to drink of that most serene light of unchangeable truth with every cloud of corporeal phantasies struck down and borne away*.  

Furthermore (4), it belongs to Christ alone to be both wayfarer and comprehensor. But whoever sees God in his essence is a comprehensor. Therefore, no one other than Christ, while still a wayfarer, can see God in his essence.  

Furthermore (5), if the cause is present, so is the effect. But the reason why the soul is blessed is the divine vision, as is clear from what has been said. If, then, someone sees God in his essence in the wayfaring state, he will experience beatitude in the wayfaring state, which is absurd.  

I answer that, since beatitude is the ultimate end of the rational creature, the wayfaring state in any rational creature is said to be that state in which it falls short of beatitude. Now, the beatitude of every rational creature consists in the vision of God in his essence, as is clear from what has been said. But that most delightful activity which beatitude consists in must belong to a potency disposed and perfected with regard to the most worthy object, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10.See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7. And thus for the rational creature to be blessed through the vision of God in his essence it must be capable of this vision through a disposition inherent in it, which we say is the light of glory. Now in this way no mere creature in the wayfaring state can see God in his essence. For by all the dispositions he has in the wayfaring state, man is capable of nothing more than understanding whatever he understands through species abstracted from the senses. For the phantasms are to our intellect as sensible things are to our senses, as is clear from the Philosopher in *On the Soul* 3.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, ch. 4, 429a13–17; ch. 7, 431a15–18. Even the angelic intellect in the wayfaring state by all natural dispositions, or even those of grace, is not capable of anything more than understanding everything it understands through the created forms to which the natural capability of its intellect is determined, as is clear from what has been said. But God in his essence cannot be seen either through a form abstracted from the senses or through any created form, as is clear from what has been said. Hence it is clear that no intellect of a mere creature in the wayfaring state is capable of the vision of God in his essence through any disposition inherent in it.  

But it sometimes happens by divine power that a thing is miraculously led to something of which it is nowise capable through any dispositions inherent in it, as that a virgin should give birth or that fire should burn in water. Likewise, too, it can happen miraculously that by divine power a created intellect having only a disposition of the wayfaring state be elevated to the vision of God in his essence. Even so, one cannot be called blessed simply from such a vision but instead only in a qualified sense, namely inasmuch as he shares in the act of the blessed. For a body in the state of gravity can also participate miraculously in the act of agility or impassibility, as we read of Habakuk (Dan 14:35) and of the three young men preserved in the fiery furnace (Dan 3:93), though their bodies were not glorified. But things that come about miraculously are said to be possible not with reference to the nature in which they come about but only with reference to the power of the one who brings them about. For we do not say, properly speaking, that a blind man can regain sight but that God can grant him sight.  

And thus it should be said that no mere created being in the wayfaring state can see God in his essence, but that God can bring it about that a created thing sees him in his essence while still remaining in the wayfaring state. From what has been said it is also clear that no man can know the essence of God in the wayfaring state, nor even the angels, since it exceeds all sensible forms, through which our intellect knows in the wayfaring state and exceeds, too, our mind itself, from whose likeness we do know something of intellectual substances.  

Reply Obj. 1: According to Dionysius,Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 4, n. 3 (PG 3:179). when Scripture says that a given saint, such as Isaiah or any other, has seen God, this should not be understood as meaning that he saw the essence of God, which is hidden to all wayfarers, but rather that certain forms, either corporeal or imaginary, were shown to them through which they were gently led to the knowledge of God. And this occurred by the ministry of angels, by whose illumination a prophet’s intellect was strengthened to reach some knowledge of divine things from forms that appear. And because an angel principally intended not to lead him to knowledge of itself but to knowledge of God, the angel was not said to appear in visions of this sort but rather God, on behalf of whose person the angel appeared and by whose power it had the efficacy to illuminate the mind. And this is called *theophany*, i.e., the apparition of God, in Scripture. Sometimes, however, the angel is also said to be the one who appears, on account of the ministry that it carried out. Hence Gregory says: *the angel who is described as having appeared to Moses is recounted now as an angel, now as the Lord. It is mentioned as an angel because it served in an outward way by speaking, as the Lord because he who presides within granted the efficacy to the one speaking*.Gregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143), Preface, par. 3. And this is also Augustine’s conclusion in *On the Trinity* 2.Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 2, ch. 13 et seq.  

Reply Obj. 2: That vision of Jacob’s was imaginary or corporeal. Even so, he is said to have seen *face to face* because the figure in which God appeared to him was seen by him face to face. For this reason a Gloss on the same passage says: *not because God can be seen but because he saw the form in which God spoke to him*. Yet in the fact that it says *face to face* a certain eminence of vision is noted. For, as Rabbi Moses says, distinguishing levels of prophecy, the level of prophecy is more eminent when something appears to the prophet in a waking vision than if it appears to him while sleeping. And if there is a revelation in either case the more eminent level is when the one who speaks appears in a figure that is seen rather than when his speaking is only heard without anything being seen. Furthermore, the level is more eminent if the one who speaks appears in the figure of an angel than in the figure of a man. And the apparition to Jacob had this eminence. For it was not when he was sleeping but when he was awake. Nor did he only hear the voice of the one speaking with him but also saw the figure, though it was that of a man. For this reason it is said that a man appeared to him whom he understood to be an angel. And it is to designate this eminence that it says that he saw the Lord face to face, referring to the angel as the Lord for the reason already mentioned.  

According to Gregory’s Gloss on the same passage, it cannot be said that this should be understood of a spiritual vision, by which the minds of the saints, purged of vices, contemplate God even in the wayfaring state by the aid of the divine light poured into them. Now, even though the divine nature is not seen as it is in such a vision, since the light received is not a likeness that sufficiently represents the divine essence, as said above, a more perfect knowledge of God is had, since the light is a more express likeness to him than the light of nature or of a sensible creature, from which man is led to knowledge of God by natural knowing. For if he had not seen it, namely the uncreated truth, in any way, he would not perceive that he was unable to see it, as it says in the same Gloss. And because we know someone by his face, we call knowledge of God *his face*.  

Reply Obj. 3: Job’s statement can be understood as describing an imaginary or an intellectual vision. If it is understood as an imaginary vision, then it indicates Job’s advancement in the level of prophecy, since first a revelation was only made to him by hearing the one speaking, whereas later it was by seeing the speaker. If, on the other hand, it is understood as an intellectual vision, then the advancement of his knowledge of faith in the understanding of divine things is shown, of the sort that the saints possess through the contemplation we mentioned before, which is called vision. For just as by seeing things we know them by receiving from the things themselves, so also in contemplation the saints receive light from God himself, through which they contemplate divine matters in a higher manner than others. But we know things that are heard by assenting to those who received the species from the things themselves when they relate to us what they have seen. And likewise through faith we adhere to the statements of the apostles and prophets, who were enlightened by divine light. And in this way we need not posit that he saw God in his essence.  

Reply Obj. 4: Scripture attributes two kinds of eminence in vision to Moses. One where it is said that *the Lord used to speak to Moses face to face, as a man speaks to his friend* (Ex 33:11). It cannot, however, be assumed from these words that he saw God face to face, as is clear from the fact that he asked, *show me thy glory* (Ex 33:18), because it was not at that time granted to him. That is why it was said to him: *you cannot see my face* (Ex 33:20). And thus a Gloss*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 113:289. on the same passage says: *Scripture is speaking according to people’s opinion, for they thought that Moses spoke with God face to face, since he would appear and speak to him through a subject creature, i.e., an angel and the cloud*. But this speaking can be understood to have been imaginary or corporeal. This is why Augustine says in *On the Trinity* 2: *the speech that occurred vocally had the tenor of a friend speaking to a friend*.Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 2, ch. 16. It can also be understood as an intellectual vision, to the extent that he gazed upon the intelligible objects of divine things, after the manner of which it is said: *let me hear what God the Lord will speak* (Ps 84 [85]:8). And the privileged status of his revelation is shown in both ways, inasmuch as God revealed his secrets to him in a way more familiar than the rest, as to a friend, according to what is said: *I have called you friends, for all that I have heard from my Father I have made known to you* (Jn 15:15). And thus it says: *there has not arisen a prophet since in Israel like Moses, whom the Lord knew face to face* (Deut 34:10).  

The other eminence of Moses’ vision is shown when the Lord says to Aaron and Miriam about him: *with him I speak mouth to mouth, clearly, and not in dark speech; and he beholds the form of the Lord*, or *the Lord’s glory*, according to the Septuagint (Num 12:8). This passage shows that his petition was fulfilled when he had asked: *show me thy face* (Ex 33:18). Thus he saw God in his essence before his death, as Augustine says (*On Genesis* 12 and *On Seeing God*).See Augustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28.1), Bk. 12, ch. 27; *Epistle 147 to Paulina* (CSEL 44), par. 13. And so Scripture says significantly: *I shall speak to him face to face*, which had not been said to him in Exodus. For the vision of God in his essence does not occur by the mediation of any higher spirit, but rather God himself shows himself immediately. And this is what it means to speak mouth to mouth. For we should understand not the mouth of the body but the mouth of the mind, as Augustine says.Ibid. But the vision of contemplation belonging to the wayfaring state occurs by the mediation of the angels’ illumination, as is clear from Dionysius.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 4, n. 3 (PG 3:179). And thus it cannot be said in this regard that he speaks mouth to mouth but instead that he speaks as if through intermediaries. Still, the vision by which he saw God in his essence prior to death did not render him blessed simply. Thus that vision did not remain in him permanently but only for a time, though that span of time is not specified in Scripture. For it was not from a disposition in the intellect but only from the divine power as if miraculously, as has been said.  

Now when a created nature is miraculously elevated to something above nature, it cannot at the same time be in the contrary act, as when Peter’s body miraculously possessed the act of the gift of agility by walking on the water, the act of gravity, which is to be borne downward, was not in it at the same time. Likewise, too, when the intellect of a wayfarer is miraculously elevated to the vision of God in his essence, it cannot at the same time be in act of the vision of the wayfaring state, by which the soul naturally perceives sensible objects. And thus in that state the soul of the one seeing must be altogether drawn out of the act of the senses. Thus Augustine says Augustine, *On the Literal Meaning of Genesis* (CSEL 28.1), Bk. 12, par. 27. that no one can see God in his essence, unless in a certain way he dies to this life, whether altogether departing from the body or turned away from and removed from sensible, carnal things. And he says the same in *On Seeing God*. In this way it is not contrary to what is said: *man shall not see me and live* (Ex 33:20).
