Hence GregoryGregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143A), Bk. 18, par. 89. says in a Gloss on the same passage: *the brightness of the eternal God can be seen by some living in the flesh though growing with inestimable virtue, though man shall not see him and live, for one who sees that wisdom, which is God, utterly dies to this world*.  

Reply Obj. 5: When he was taken up at that time Paul saw God in his essence in the same way as was said of Moses, as Augustine says in *On Seeing God* and *Literal Meaning of Genesis* 12,In the same chapters cited just above. so that in this manner the teacher of the gentiles and the master of the Jews would obtain the same privilege. Though some say that this sort of vision was a middle between the vision of the wayfaring state and the vision of heaven, meaning that he did not see God in his essence but instead by a most eminent contemplation in the wayfaring state, though the words of the saints give the contrary impression. Even so, in this regard it can be said that it was a middle between these two types of knowledge, since in the fact that he saw God in his essence it was above the knowledge of the wayfaring state, while in the fact that Paul did not simply experience beatitude from that vision, it was below the knowledge of heaven.  

Reply Obj. 6: Intellectual vision does not pertain to things that are in the soul in their essence in every way, but things that are in it as intelligible forms are in the intellect. Now God is not in the soul of a wayfarer in this way in his essence but instead by a likeness. And what Augustine says, that intellectual vision is of those things whose likenesses are nothing other than themselves, should be referred to the object of vision, not to the medium by which the vision is had. For it is obvious that the medium by which a stone is seen is the species of it in the soul, which is not the stone’s very essence. Rather, by the stone’s likeness one arrives at knowing the stone’s very essence. For the object of the intellect is the ‘what,’ that is, the essence or quiddity of the thing. But this does not occur in imaginary or corporeal vision because the object of the imagination or the senses is not the thing’s very essence but rather the accidents that are on the outside, such as color, shape, and such things. Hence, too, in the intellectual vision of a wayfarer it is not necessary for the divine essence to be itself the medium by which the vision is had, but for a sort of knowledge of the divine essence to be arrived at through a likeness to it, not one by which it is known what God is, but one by which it is known what he is not.  

Or else we may say that Augustine, in the *Literal Meaning of Genesis* 12 (from which the words are taken in the Gloss on 2 Cor 12), cites this to show the difference between intellectual vision and other kinds of vision. For intellectual vision has the advantage over the others of being able to know the things belonging to the intellect in their essence, whereas the senses or imagination cannot do this. But it does not follow that because of this the intellect only understands through its essence and not through any likenesses.  

Reply Obj. 7: In all potencies ordered to one another it is necessary that where the act of a prior potency ends, the act of a secondary potency must begin. For this reason, since the will presupposes the intellect, the will is borne toward what the intellect ends in. Now even though the intellect knows God in the wayfaring state only through his effects, its activity does terminate in God himself according to however little knowledge it receives concerning him. And thus to be borne up to God the affections do not need to return to these means. Rather, they can be borne immediately to God, to whom the intellect has come.  

Reply Obj. 8: Even when something is seen through the likeness of another thing, it can be the case that someone seeing the thing through a medium thinks about the thing directly without his knowledge being turned to any other thing. For it is not turned to the medium as a given thing but as the image of the thing known through it. But the motion of the intellect to an image precisely as image and to what is imaged is the same, though the intellect’s motion to an image as a given thing and to what it is an image of are different. And thus when, through the likeness of a created thing that the intellect has within itself it is turned to the created thing not as a certain thing but only as the likeness of a thing, then it thinks about God directly, even though it does not see God directly.  

Reply Obj. 9: As a Gloss says on the passage *truths are decayed*, (Ps 11:2), from the one uncreated truth many truths are reflected in our souls, just as from a single face many likenesses are reflected in a broken mirror or in many mirrors. And this truth reflected in our intellect first and principally consists in the first self-evident principles. And thus when we judge all things by them, our judgment is in accord with the first truth, which the truth of these principles is a likeness of.  

Reply Obj. 10: Something can be known from another in two ways. In one way, as when from the knowledge of it one arrives at the knowledge of the other. And in this way what something is known by is more known, as principles are more known than conclusions. In the other way, as perfecting the knower for knowing, either like a form, as the species of a stone in the eye perfects the eye for seeing, or as an effective principle of this form, as it is said that the eye sees by the sun. And in this way what something is known by need not be more known, or rather it is even possible for it not to be known. For it is known by a certain bending of the intellect away from the knowable object to what was the principle of the knowledge. It is in this way that we know all things in God. And thus the argument does not follow.  

Reply Obj. 11: Augustine’s statement should be understood with regard to the fact that no created being is interposed between our mind and God by whose mediation our mind proceeds into being from God or by whose mediation it is informed by grace or by glory as by an efficient means. Even so, nothing prevents our mind from being informed by God by the mediation of a created form, such as the habit of grace or charity. And it is in this way that the mind of a wayfarer is informed by a created species for the vision of God.  

Reply Obj. 12: Avempace posited that man could reach an understanding of the essences of separated substances through the speculative sciences that we come to learn in this life. But the Commentator, commenting on *On the Soul* 3,Averroes, *On the Soul*, Bk. 3, comment 36. disproves this position from the fact that no principles have yet been discovered by which we could reach this understanding, which does not seem probable if such an understanding could be reached through the principles of the speculative sciences, especially since all of human knowledge is ordered to this. For it would not be probable for the human species to fall short of its natural perfection completely. And thus it should be said that his position is impossible. The reason for this is that the speculative sciences proceed from self-evident principles received from the senses, as it says in the *Posterior Analytics* 2.Aristotle, *Posterior Analytics*, Bk. 2, ch. 19. And thus we cannot arrive by them at the essences of things that exceed all sensible forms. For this reason there is no speculative science that we now acquire by which we know what God is or what an angel is, except under certain likenesses, knowing of them rather what they are not than what they are. And for this reason the Philosopher says at the beginning of the *Metaphysics*Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 2, 982b29. that man does not possess science about God, namely because we are unable to have it completely.  

Reply Obj. 13: The body can only be corruptible when the soul does not have full dominion over the body. For if it did have full dominion it would confer its incorruption on it. And thus in the state of corruption the soul must know in accord with the requirements of the body, namely by receiving from the senses. But God cannot be seen in his essence by such a mode of knowledge, as is clear from what has been said, and to this extent the state of corruption is incompatible with the vision of God in his essence.  

Question 3  

***The delight of the blessed***

***Next, we inquire about delight; and here five questions arise:***

first, what is delight;  

- second, what is the cause of delight;
- third, on the comparison of delight to sadness;
- fourth, on the goodness or evil of delight and sadness;
- fifth, on the comparison of spiritual delight to corporeal delight.
- Article 1

***What is delight***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the first question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 1; q. 35, a. 1. It seems that delightThe word *delectatio* could easily be translated as “pleasure” when referring to physical and sensible pleasures. We have translated it “delight” throughout the ensuing question in order to preserve the relationship between physical pleasures, which humans share with animals, and spiritual delights, which humans experience in common with angels. is not a passion. For every passion consists in motion, as it says in the *Book of Six Principles*Gilbertus Porretanus, *Liber sex principiorum*.  and the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 2. But delight is neither a motion nor any kind of generation, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12. Therefore, delight is not a passion.  

Obj. 2: An activity is not a passion but rather an action, since it belongs to one in act precisely as being in act. But delight is a kind of perfection of an activity, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4. Therefore, delight is not a passion.  

Obj. 3: In every passion what experiences it is drawn into the limits of what is acting, for the agent assimilates the patient to itself. But the cause of delight is fittingness, and thus whatever is delighted remains within its own limits. Therefore, delight is not a passion.  

On the contrary (1), joy and delight belong to the same genus. But joy is a passion. For it is included as one of the four principal passions, as is clear from the *City of God* 9.Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 9, 3. Therefore, delight is a passion.  

Furthermore (2), the object of delight is the good. But the good is the proper object of the appetite. Therefore, delight pertains to the appetite. But other things that pertain to the appetite are passions, such as love, anger, and so forth. Therefore, delight, too, is a passion.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that delight cannot be in the intellective part of the soul or in other spiritual substances.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 4; q. 35, a. 1; *In I Sent*. d. 45, a. 1. For, as it says in the *Physics* 7,Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 7, ch. 3, 247a1–20. every passion is in the sensitive part of the soul. But delight is a passion, as has been proved. Therefore, it cannot be in the intellective part or in any spiritual substance.  

Obj. 2: Avicenna says in *On Natural Things* 6Avicenna, *On Natural Things*, Bk. 6, pt. 2, ch. 3. that the subject of delight belongs to a corporeal species. But the intellective part does not use any corporeal organ. Therefore, there cannot be any delight in it.  

Obj. 3: What belongs to the intellective part is not common to both us and brute animals. But delight also exists in brute animals. Therefore, delight does not belong to the intellective part.  

On the contrary (1), it is said: *there is joy before the angels of God over one sinner who repents* (Lk 15:10).  

Furthermore (2), the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14, 1154b26. that God rejoices always by a single, simple activity. Therefore, there can be delight in other spiritual substances for the same reason.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that delight is in time.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 2; *De veritate*, q. 8, a. 14, ad 12; *Super Eth*. 10, lec. 5. For delight follows on our activity, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b23. But our activity is in time. Therefore, delight, too, is in time.  

Obj. 2: Everything in which we can understand a before and an after is in time. But we can understand a before and an after in delight, since someone can delight continuously for a long period of time. Therefore, it seems that delight is in time.  

Obj. 3: Delight is categorized alongside other passions. But the other passions of the soul are in time, such as anger and the like. Therefore, so is delight.  

On the contrary (1), the PhilosopherAristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174a17. says in the *Ethics* 10: *no one would understand delight with reference to a time*.  

Furthermore (2), delight follows apprehension of what is fitting. But vision and other apprehensions are not in time but in an instant. Therefore, neither is delight measured by time.  

Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that delight and joy are absolutely identical, as well as exultation, rejoicing, and joyfulness.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 3; q. 35, a. 2; *In III Sent*. d. 26, q. 1, a. 3; d. 27, q. 1, a. 2, ad 3; d. 49, q. 3, a. 1, qa. 4; *De veritate*, q. 26, a. 4, ad 5; *SCG* I, ch. 90. For passions and habits are distinguished on the basis of objects. But joy and delight and the others mentioned have the same object, namely, the fitting good. Therefore, they are the same.  

Obj. 2: From the same cause proceeds the same effect. For the same is always such as to do the same. But we can posit nothing but one and the same cause for delight and joy, namely, being joined to what is fitting. Therefore, they are the same.  

Obj. 3: One thing has one opposite. But delight and joy have a single opposite, which is sadness. Therefore, delight and joy are altogether a single thing.  

On the contrary (1), in *On Natural Things* 6, AvicennaAvicenna, *On Natural Things*, Bk. 6, pt. 4, ch. 5. says that joy is a species of delight. Therefore, they are not altogether identical.  

Furthermore (2), if joy and delight were altogether identical, then wherever there is delight there would be joy. But this is false. For, properly speaking, we say that there is delight in the senses, but not joy. Therefore, delight and joy are not completely the same.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, when anything attains to its proper perfection it obtains stability and vigor, as do bodies when they arrive at the limits of their natural motion, since they are kept in their natural place. But since motions are distinguished on the basis of limits, we must make a different judgment about arriving at a limit of completion according to different kinds of motion.  

Now something is moved to its perfection in two ways. In one way, through a natural inclination, as a stone is moved downward. And this motion does not first require knowledge in what is moved but in another who is the principle causing this inclination. This is natural motion. In the other way, through an inclination whose principle is the knowledge of whatever is moved. And this inclination is called appetite, either animal or intellectual. Therefore, just as in the termination of natural motion there is a kind of natural invigoration of what is moved, so also in the pursuit of the perfection that the animal appetite tends toward, there is a sort of rest or invigoration of it, presupposing knowledge of the perfection now possessed, just as the appetite presupposed knowledge of the perfection not yet possessed. And such vigor or rest of the appetite is called delight. Hence, too, the Commentator says on the *Ethics* 10 that delight is a kind of grand blossoming of nature.  

But there are two kinds of appetite, just as there are two kinds of knowledge: that of the sensitive part and that of the intellective part. The appetite of the sensitive part is a power in a corporeal organ and is the immediate principle of physical motion. This is why everything that occurs in the sensitive appetite is connected to some sort of physical transmutation, which does not occur in things in the intellective appetite, unless ‘passion’ is taken in a broad and improper sense, as was said in Book III, Distinction 15, Question 11, Article 1, Quaestiuncula 2. For this reason, delight in the sensitive appetite is a passion, whereas delight in the intellective appetite is not, unless ‘passion’ is taken in a broad sense.  

Reply Obj. 1: It is not a problem for a motion to terminate in another motion incidentally, although this is impossible *per se*, as is proved in the *Physics* 5.Aristotle, *Physics*, Bk. 5, ch. 2, 225b17–33. Therefore delight, precisely as such, always consists in a termination of motion. But even so, because in the termination of the motion of the appetite a motion arises, in which animal passion consists, it happens that passion is accidentally in delight, but it is in sensitive delight *per se*.  

Reply Obj. 2: Even though an activity precisely as an activity is not a passion, it can still have a passion connected to it, either as preceding it or as following it. It may precede, as sensing has the reception of sensible species connected to it, and understanding has the reception of intelligible species connected to it, to the extent that all sensing is a kind of undergoing. It may follow, however, as some sort of physical change follows on the activity of the sensitive appetite, in the dilation or constriction of the heart and other such things. And in this way, even though delight is about activity, it still consists in a kind of passion to the extent that the delight is sensitive.  

Reply Obj. 3: Someone who is delighted is within the bounds of his perfection, which is what his delight springs from. Now these limits are foreign in the way that perfection and the perfectible differ, but they are proper to the extent that perfection is connatural to the perfectible.  

We concede the other two objections with regard to sensitive delight.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that delight is found in God, angels, men, and brute animals in different ways, according to the different ways they relate to their own perfections, from attainment of which their delight arises.  

Brute animals cannot attain to a higher perfection than material perfection. This is why they obtain it by a material motion, i.e., one carried out through a bodily organ. And so in them the delight that arises is a material passion.  

In the case of angels, since they are altogether separated from matter, there can be no material perfection but only immaterial perfection. This is why they also obtain it in an immaterial manner and the delight that follows in them is immaterial. Hence Dionysius says at the end of the *Celestial Hierarchy*: *the heavenly governances*, i.e., angels, *in no way whatsoever receive what belongs to the delight of passion for us*.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 15, n. 9 (PG 3:339).  

But men are composed of a spiritual and material nature. And thus as regards the lower part, namely the sensible appetite, in which men and brute animals agree, there is material delight, whereas in the higher appetite, in which men and angels agree, they have immaterial delight, such as when they rejoice about spiritual matters, like acts of contemplation and other divine gifts. Hence Dionysius says in the same place that: *holy men have often also been made to participate in the angelic delight through the deifying superadditions of divine illuminations*.Ibid.  

Now in all the cases mentioned, perfection is something different from what is perfected. For this reason it is possible for there to be more than one perfection in a single thing. And thus both in brute animals as well as in men and angels there are multiple delights that come to them. But God has no perfection that is not identical to himself, since he is pure act. And thus he has only the one, unique delight, not one that comes from without but one that is essential. Hence the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14, 1154b26. that God always rejoices with one, simple delight.  

Reply Obj. 1: There is no passion in the intellective appetite, only in the sensitive appetite. For what undergoes something is moved and led by another. But the intellective appetite, since it has free mastery of itself, is not said to be led, whereas the sensitive appetite is led. This is why passion is found in it alone, while the delight that is in the intellective appetite is not a passion but a kind of perfection of activity, as has been said.  

Reply Obj. 2: Avicenna is speaking of sensitive delight, which is completed through a physical organ, as in the case of other things pertaining to the sensitive part.  

Reply Obj. 3: Things that are common to us and to brute animals under the same account do not pertain to the intellective part. But something is common to us and to brute animals under different accounts when in us it pertains to the intellect, such as knowledge, and other such things. Some things extend from God down to brute animals by a certain commonality of analogy, such as knowledge. Others, such as life, extend from God down to plants. Finally, others, such as being, extend even down to stones, as is clear from DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 4 (PG 3:694). in *On the Divine Names*. And in this respect spiritual delight has something in common with delight in brute animals.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that there are two ways in which something can be said to exist in time: *per se* and incidentally. Now because ‘before’ and ‘after’ pertain to the very notion of time, and whatever applies to something according to its specific character is in it *per se*, those things in which something required for their specific completion is awaited in the future are essentially in time. And this is the way that motion is in time. For a species of motion is not completed in the middle but only if it reaches the term. Likewise, too, rest is in time by virtue of the fact that to be at rest is to be in the same manner now as before. And so it pertains to the notion of rest that something be awaited in the future. On the other hand, something is said to be in time incidentally when nothing required for the completion of its species is awaited in the future, such as being a man, which is completed in a single instant. But it is connected with motion, which is in time essentially, inasmuch as the being of man is changeable.  

Delight, however, awaits nothing in the future for the completion of its species and character of it. For, even in an instant, it is true to say that a man delights. Hence it is only in time incidentally, namely, to the extent that the perfection that delight arises from is itself connected to motion. And thus if there is a perfection that has no contact with motion, such delight will be in time neither *per se* nor incidentally. And this is the spiritual delight in the angels. In brute animals, however, delight exists in time incidentally inasmuch as motion is directly joined to their perfections. But in us delight is not in time as regards the intellective part even incidentally, unless very indirectly, namely, inasmuch as the activity of our intellect is joined to phantasms. Still, it is true that no delight is in time, speaking *per se*.  

Reply Obj. 1: Delight follows directly on the soul’s activities, which are to apprehend and to will. But these activities are only in time incidentally. For to see, to understand, and to will all occur in a moment.  

Reply Obj. 2: The fact that someone is said to delight for a longer time than another is only inasmuch as delight is in time incidentally, just as a man is said to see or to live for a longer time.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even other passions of the soul, as regards what is on the part of the soul, are only in time accidentally, namely, inasmuch as motion on the part of the body is joined to them. Delight, however, has more the character of not being in time than other passions. For other passions consist in an appetite to the extent that it tends to something, such as anger to vengeance and hate to another’s harm. But delight consists in an appetite to the extent that its motion is terminated in its appropriate appetible object. But every term of motion is not in time but in an instant.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said that delight, joy, rejoicing, and all such things have in common that they are caused by an appetite’s being united to its perfection. Now a perfection can be joined to something in two ways: in one way, really; in the other way, according to the understanding of it. Now from the fact that a perfection is really joined to something, delight arises, whereas from its being joined in apprehension, joy and all the rest arise. For this reason, we have joy over the hope of future things and the memory of past ones. But we only delight in present things. Thus it is also the case that because the external senses only apprehend present things, we are not said to rejoice in them but to delight. The internal senses, however, apprehend both present and absent things. This is why we are said both to delight and to rejoice in the interior senses. The internal senses are said to delight over the fact that they are joined to their perfection, as over its very activity, over which the internal senses also rejoice inasmuch as the perfection is apprehended. But in the activity of the external senses, which is only joined to it by apprehension, one can rejoice but not delight, just as an internal sense or the intellect does not delight in eating sweets but can rejoice in the fact inasmuch as it apprehends it as fitting to the external senses.  

Now rejoicing, exultation, and joyfulness express certain effects of joy. Rejoicing expresses the interior effect, to the extent that the affections themselves are expanded as if taking on strength and being perfected from being united to the object of appetite. Hence, too, the passion of joy is perfected with a swelling of the heart. For we say ‘rejoicing’ as if saying ‘widening.’There is a play on words in Latin, *laetitia* and *latitia*, which is impossible to convey in English. But exultation expresses a further effect of joy breaking out into external expression as a sign of internal joy. This is why it is called ‘ex-ultation,’ from the fact that interior joy bursts out externally, which happens from the greatness of that interior joy. This is why Cassiodorus says on Psalm 9 that we speak of exultation in great joys.Cassiodorus says something like this in various places. Here is the closest in Psalm 9: “Exsultare est enim animi et corporis maiore motu iucundissima libertate gaudere” (*Exposition of the Psalms* [CCSL 97], Ps 9, l. 57). Finally, joyfulness implies a further effect of joy breaking out into external expression, which not only points to interior joy but also stirs others to rejoice.  

Reply Obj. 1: Joy and delight, though they have a single reality as their object, do not have that reality as their object under the same notion. For the good really acquired produces delight, but it produces joy as grasped in apprehension. Rejoicing, exultation, and joyfulness, however, are the same as joy, except that they express certain effects of joy, as has been said.  

Reply Obj. 2: The cause is the same for delight and joy, though not in the same order. It is first and *per se* the cause of delight, but it is secondarily the cause of joy. For something that is apprehended never produces joy except inasmuch as it is judged as possessed or able to be possessed in reality to produce delight. And thus being united with a perfection first and *per se* produces delight, but inasmuch as it is apprehended as producing delight it produces joy even when it does not actually cause delight. This is how delight is prior to joy in nature.  

Reply Obj. 3: Sadness is directly opposed to joy, whereas the opposite of delight is pain, according as delight is in the external senses, especially touch. But according as it is internal, it has no other opposite than sadness, though it could have another opposite if a name were proposed for it.  

Article 2  

***What is the cause of delight***

Obj. 1: To the second question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 1, ad 1; q. 32, a. 1. It seems that the cause of delight is not only the unimpeded connatural activity of a habit. For Avicenna says in *On Natural Things* that delight arises from the perception of the fitting acquired. But not only activity is fitting, but also many other things. Therefore, not only activity is the cause of delight.  

Obj. 2: Contraries do not have the same cause. But activity is sometimes the cause of sadness, even proper and connatural activity. Hence acedia, which is a kind of sadness, also arises from spiritual works. Therefore, activity is not the proper cause of delight.  

Obj. 3: If something is the cause of something else, the continuation of the former is the cause of the continuation of the latter. But the continuation of activity is not the cause of the continuation of delight. In fact, continuation of activity frequently leads to tedium and activities are more delightful when they are novel. Therefore, activity is not the cause of delight.  

Obj. 4: Rest is opposed to activity. But rest causes delight. Therefore, activity is not the proper cause of delight.  

Obj. 5: Those who are old give greater attention to activity than the young. But there is less delight in the old than in the young, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 8.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 8, ch. 3, 1156a21–31. Therefore, activity is not the cause of delight.  

Obj. 6: If activity were the cause of delight, then people in a given relation to activity would be related in a similar way to delight. But some who have similar activity do not similarly delight. For the melancholy delight less than the sanguine. Therefore, it seems that activity is not as such the cause of delight.  

On the contrary (1), the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12, 1153a15. that delight is the connatural activity of a habit. And this can only be understood causally. Therefore, activity is the cause of delight.  

Furthermore (2), a given thing is distinguished from others according as its cause is distinguished from others. But delights are distinguished from each other based on the distinctions between activities, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1175a22–27. Therefore, activity is the cause of delight.  

Furthermore (3), a habit is known only through an activity or through something caused by an activity. But there is a habit known from delight, as it says in the *Ethics* 2: *we must take as a sign of habit the pleasure that arises in acts*.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 2, ch. 3, 1104b4–6. Since, then, delight is not itself an activity, it seems that it is caused by an activity.  

I answer that, just as the soul is the form of the body, so also in things that are common to soul and body, what is on the part of the soul is formal whereas what is on the part of the body is material, as in anger the desire for vengeance is formal while the heating of blood in the heart is material.  

Since, then, delight insofar as it is a passion is among the things common to soul and body, we can seek two causes for it: a formal cause on the part of the soul and a material cause on the part of the body. But delight, to the extent that it is on the part of the soul, has a character that is sort of common to all in whom delight is found, namely, God, angels, men, and brute animals; while to the extent that it is on the part of the body, it abides only in the sensitive part of the soul. Consequently, the formal cause of delight is the cause of all delight, but not its material cause. It is also clear that with reference to form it is something actual, with respect to matter it is something potential, and with respect to the dispositions of matter it is something that has an aptitude or adeptness to act. And thus on the basis of the material cause we know how someone is disposed to delight, while on the basis of its formal cause it is apparent how someone actually delights. The formal cause, then, of every passion or activity is its object. Now the formal object of delight is the fitting good that one is united with. For from the fact that delight is in the appetitive part, its object must be the good, while from the fact that the appetite is already terminated in its object, it must be fitting and attained.  

Now the good that is the proper object of delight is activity, especially for the following three reasons.  

First, since delight consists in the resting of the appetite and the motion of the appetite is to the good, the delight of the rest itself must be in the ultimate of goods. Now the ultimate and most perfect in each thing is its activity. Hence every inherent form is compared to activity in a certain way as potency to act, and for this reason the form is called first act, such as science, and activity is called second act, such as considering, as is clear in *On the Soul* 2.Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 2, ch. 1, 412a6–11.  

Second, since delight is in an appetite and every passion or activity of an appetite requires apprehension as a prerequisite, the attained good that causes delight must be apprehended. But a disposition already inhering is not apprehended by us like that while it is in the process of becoming, as Avicenna says in *On Natural Things* 6.Avicenna, *On Natural Things*, Bk. 6, pt. 3, ch. 3. For example, those suffering from a continuous fever feel less of the fever’s heat than others suffering from fever, even though the fever is more intense in their case. For the heat is already diffused to the members as if part of their constitution. And this occurs because our sensing and understanding come from the intellect and senses being changed by their objects, whereas nothing is changed by what is already at rest in it. And so since our being and our living and all our proper acts are in us as resting in us, while activity alone is in us as in the process of becoming, it is in activity itself that we perceive both our being and our living. This is why we take delight in our activity as well as in living and sensing and other such things, in which we receive a certain indication of our being and our living, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle says this in a variety of ways in *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, chs. 4–5.  

Third, because activity proceeds from the goods that are in us and in a certain way contains them in itself as an effect contains its cause. By activity we are also united to external goods. Hence every good in which we can take delight gives rest to our appetite by the mediation of activity; and so activity is the essential cause of delight. Not every activity, however, is the cause of delight, but rather one that proceeds from a habit and is not impeded. For the attained good only gives delight inasmuch as it is fitting.  

Now an activity is rendered fitting to us and connatural, as it were, through a habit. For a habit inclines to an act after the manner of nature, as Tully says of virtue.See Cicero, *De inventione* (Teubner, ed. E. Stroebel), Bk. 2, ch. 53, par. 159: “virtus est animi habitus naturae modo atque rationi consentaneus.” And thus it says in the *Ethics* 2Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 2, ch. 9. that when a work brings about delight this is a sign of an inherent habit. But even though an activity becomes connatural to us on the basis of a habit, it can still be unfitting to us for some accidental reason, that is, inasmuch as we have certain impediments in our progress toward such an activity. Hence the activity that causes delight must be unimpeded. And this is what the PhilosopherAristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12, 1153a15. says in the *Ethics* 7: that delight consists in the unimpeded activity of a connatural habit. In this way, then, the proper formal cause of delight is the unimpeded activity of a connatural habit, while the material cause of delight should be taken on the part of its subject. Now the subject of delight and of all the soul’s passions is the animal spirit, which is the proximate instrument of the soul in activities that are carried out through the body.  

Now for the spirit to be ready for delight two things are required: due quantity and due quality.  

Due quantity is that there be an abundance of spirits, for two reasons. First, because a spirit that abounds in quantity abounds also in power. Second, since expansion of the heart and of spirits is required for the passion of delight, such expansion cannot be completed when there are few spirits. For nature constricts them and holds them back at their source. But when there are many spirits, nature can hold part back at their source and release the excess for the expansion. And thus the convalescent, in whom there are few spirits, are more prone to sadness than to delight.  

But due quality is found in three things. First, that the spirit have a temperate constitution. And thus the sick do not tend to delight. Second, that the spirit be clear, not cloudy. And thus the melancholic, in whom there are dark and earthy spirits, are prone to sadness. Third, that its substance be at a midpoint between denseness and thinness. For if it is too thick, as in the case of the elderly, or too thin, as in the case of the lean, it is not apt for delight because then it is not capable of easy expansion. These are the reasons why wine gives joy, since it generates many spirits that are clear, temperate, and midway between thickness and thinness. Therefore, the greater the disposition on the part of material causes to joy or sadness, the less is required on the part of the formal cause, and vice versa. And thus there are some who rejoice or are saddened at a slight occurrence while others only experience joy or sorrow from a great one.  

Reply Obj. 1: Even though there are many things besides activity that are fitting, delight only arises from all of them by the mediation of activity. For we are united to external fitting things by an activity, while internal ones are apprehended in activities.  

Reply Obj. 2: Not every activity is a cause of delight, but rather the unimpeded activity of a connatural habit. And thus when we are constrained to activities contrary to the habits that are in us or in which we suffer impediments, by reason of which we experience fatigue and exertion, we do not delight in such activities but instead are saddened.  

Reply Obj. 3: There are three reasons why we do not delight in an activity as it proceeds as we do at its beginning.  

The Philosopher assigns the first reason in the *Ethics* 10,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1175a29–1175b2. namely, that since activity is the cause of delight, the more attentively one works, the greater the delight that arises from the activity. But man is more attentive to an activity at its beginning than as it progresses. For everything that is new draws more of our focus to itself. And thus as an activity progresses, when a person turns to an activity more negligently, he does not feel as much delight in the work.  

He gives the second reason in the *Ethics* 7,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14. which reason was also mentioned above. For delight in us must follow the mode of our nature. Therefore, since our nature is changeable there will not always be the same object of delight for us, since we will have already been changed from our original disposition. And so after a little while there what was previously fitting for us will no longer be fitting. And thus what was previously an object of delight becomes for us less delightful or even sorrowful. And this is also the reason why the continuity of a single thing in us leads to fatigue, while changing back and forth delights. In God, however, who is always in the same state, there is always the same delightful activity, and he is not delighted by any alternation. And the argument is similar regarding the angels and blessed men.  

The third reason can be gleaned from the words of Avicenna in *On Natural Things* 5.Avicenna, *On Natural Things*, Bk. 6, pt. 3, ch. 3. For since perception of the fitting is required for delight, the less something is felt, the less it is an object of delight. Now things are felt more when they are new, since their disposition afterwards becomes like a quality inherent in those who feel it. For example, craftsmen who are used to hearing the sound of hammers are not affected by these sounds in the same way as others are. And thus at the beginning of an activity a person takes more delight than later as it progresses continuously, even though the activity is, perhaps, more intense as it progresses.  

Reply Obj. 4: Even if an activity is delightful in itself, nevertheless, inasmuch as a motion is attached to it that leads to fatigue, it is in a certain way painful and saddening. This is why rest is also incidentally delightful, inasmuch as it takes away the cause of saddening, namely motion.  

Reply Obj. 5: With regard to the material cause of delight the elderly are less apt to delight than the young. And thus they are also not as affected by a greater formal cause of delight as the young are by a lesser cause, speaking of delight as a passion, i.e., something overflowing into the sensible appetite and more sensible to us. So too, sulfur can be lit by a meager fire, since it is apt for this, while wood that is less apt for this is not lit by a larger fire. Even so, spiritual delight, which is not a passion, is not less in the elderly than in the young, but perhaps greater.  

Reply Obj. 6: Those who carry out a similar activity sometimes do not take delight in it in the same way. This can happen both on the part of the material cause, because they do not have spirits similarly disposed to the activity, and on the part of the formal cause, since the same activity is fitting to one through a habit inherent in him while it is unfitting to another who lacks the habit. In fact, it may even be contrary to him, if he possesses the contrary habit. And for this reason, giving, which is delightful to one who is liberal, is a cause for sadness to one who is avaricious.  

Article 3  

***On the relationship of delight to sadness***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the third question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 35, a. 3; q. 31, a. 8, ad 2; *ST* III, q. 84, a. 9, ad 2. It seems that sadness is not contrary to delight. For passions are distinguished according to their causes. But the cause of sadness is motion and exertion, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7.Aristotle does not say exactly this, but something like it, at *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14, 1154a22ff. This is why the young, who are in motion, desire delights as a sort of medicine against sadness. Now the cause of delight is activity. But motion and activity are not contraries, since they are sometimes joined together. Therefore, neither is delight contrary to sadness.  

Obj. 2: When two things are contrary, one is not the matter of the other. But delight can be the matter of sadness. For, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 9,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 9, ch. 4, 1166b5–24. one who is depraved is after a little while saddened by what had delighted him. Likewise, too, sadness is the matter of delight, as a penitent is enjoined that he should always sorrow and rejoice at this sorrow. Therefore, delight and sadness are not contraries.  

Obj. 3: Delight and joy are not altogether identical, as is clear from what has been said. But sadness is opposed to joy. Therefore, it is not opposed to delight.  

On the contrary (1), medicines come about by way of contraries, as it says in the *Ethics* 2.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 2, ch. 3, 1104b15–16. But delights are medicines against sadnesses, as it says in the *Ethics* 7.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14, 1154a27–28. Therefore, delight and sadness are contraries.  

Furthermore (2), good and evil are contraries. But delight is opposed to sadness as good is to evil, as it says in the *Ethics* 7.Ibid. Therefore, they are contraries.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that there is some sadness contrary to the delight that comes from contemplation.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 35, a. 5; *ST* III, q. 46, a. 7, ad 4; *In III Sent*. d. 15, q. 2, a. 3, qa. 2, ad 3; d. 26, q. 1, a. 5, ad 5; *De veritate*, q. 26, a. 3, ad 6, and a. 9, ad 8; *Comp. theo*. 165. For fatigue is a cause of sadness. But fatigue occurs in contemplation. Therefore, sadness occurs in contemplation. Therefore, there is a sadness that is contrary to the delight that comes from contemplation.  

Obj. 2: Just as it happens that we may contemplate something as fitting, so also it happens that we may contemplate something as harmful. But the contemplation of what is fitting causes delight. Therefore, the contemplation of what is harmful causes sadness. And thus the same follows as before.  

Obj. 3: Every action that is contrary to an inherent habit is a cause of sadness, just as to one who has a habit of injustice the activity of what is just is saddening. But it does happen that someone who has the habit of a science proceeds to act against the character of the inherent habit, as when a grammarian speaks ungrammatically and a logician syllogizes improperly. Therefore, in contemplation there can be sadness. And thus the same follows as before.  

On the contrary (1), the Philosopher says in the *Topics* 1Aristotle, *Topics*, Bk. 1, ch. 15: “the pleasure of drinking has a contrary in the pain of thirst, whereas the pleasure of seeing that the diagonal is incommensurate with the side has none” (106a37–38). that there is not sadness contrary to the delight that arises from contemplation.  

Furthermore (2), contemplation proceeds from notions in the soul. But the notions of things, even of contraries, in the soul are not contraries, as it says in the *Metaphysics* 7.Cf. Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 7, ch. 7, 1032b1–5. Therefore, neither can anything be contrary to the delight that is caused by contemplation.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that sadness has a greater effect than delight.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 35, a. 6; *ST* II-II, q. 138, a. 1; *In III Sent*. d. 27, q. 1, a. 3, ad 3. For, as Augustine says in the *Book of Eighty-Three Questions*: *there is no one who does not rather flee pain than desire pleasure*.Augustine, *Book of Eighty-Three Questions* (CCSL 44A), qu. 36. Therefore, man is more affected by sadness than by delight.  

Obj. 2: Just as sadness by its intensity moves a person to flight, so also delight moves someone to desire. But not everyone desires delights. In fact some, such as the temperate, avoid them. But everyone flees sorrows, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics*.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 13. Therefore, delight has less effect than sadness.  

Obj. 3: According to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5. all sadness impedes man in his activity. But not every delight helps, but only proper delight. Therefore, sadness has a more intense effect than delight.  

On the contrary (1), good is more efficacious in acting than evil. For evil only acts by the power of the good, as Dionysius says.Pseudo-Dionysius, *On the Divine Names*, ch. 4, n. 32 (PG 3:731). But the good is the cause of delight and evil the cause of sadness. Therefore, delight has a greater effect than sadness.  

Furthermore (2), as it says in the *Ethics* 7,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14. delight drives out not only sadness that is contrary to it but also any other sadness as well. Therefore, delight is stronger than sadness.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, the formal character of every passion is drawn from its object. Therefore both actions and passions are distinguished on the basis of their objects. Now the object of delight is the good, while the object of sadness is evil. Hence sadness must be formally contrary to delight.  

Reply Obj. 1: Even though motion is not in itself contrary to activity, we do find in motion some basis for its causing sadness, which is contrary to activity insofar as it is the cause of delight. For activity is the cause of delight to the extent that it is fitting to the one acting. But in motion, in which there is progress from one thing to another, there must be something unfitting to what is moved, since the things between which the motion exists are contraries. And thus by reason of the contrariety that it has toward what is movable, motion induces weariness and sadness. For this reason unnatural and violent motions especially cause weariness. This is how, then, motion causes sadness by reason of contrariety, whereas activity causes delight by reason of fittingness. And therefore delight and sadness must be opposed on the basis of the opposition between fittingness and contrariety.  

Reply Obj. 2: Sadness is not the matter for delight, nor is delight the matter for sadness, speaking *per se*. Rather, this occurs only incidentally, inasmuch as a delight happens to be evil, which is the proper matter of sadness, or a sadness happens to be good, which is the proper matter of delight. And it can occur in these contraries that one is incidentally the matter of the other because of the commonality of their objects, which are good and evil, one of which can be connected to the other under different aspects. For what is good in one aspect can be evil in another aspect. It happens, too, that contraries of this sort may consist in an act of the soul, which reflects on its act inasmuch as it knows that it knows and wills itself to will. In this way, too, sadness can be the matter for joy and even joy itself, though it cannot occur in natural things that something be the matter of itself or even that contraries would be the matter for one another.  

Reply Obj. 3: If this force were given to the words, so that delight was distinguished from joy as suffering from sadness, then sadness would correspond to joy as its opposite and suffering would correspond to delight. But because suffering is properly understood as referring to the senses, especially the sense of touch, while delight, properly speaking, also extends to the internal powers, we say that delight is opposed not only by suffering but also by sadness.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that apprehension can be called the cause of delight in two ways. In one way, on the part of the very thing apprehended, as when we apprehend a good that befits us and so we are delighted. And in this way apprehension is the cause of all delight. Hence nothing prevents something being contrary to the delight that is caused like this from apprehension, whether sensible or intelligible; otherwise, sadness would not be contrary to any delight. In the other way, on the part of the apprehension itself, as when someone delights not in what is apprehended but in the very apprehension by which he apprehends it. And in this case, speaking *per se*, there cannot be a sadness contrary to the delight that is caused from any apprehension. For this type of sadness would also have to be caused by an apprehension, since contraries concern the same thing. But apprehension, speaking *per se*, can in no way produce sadness. For only what is contrary can produce sadness, but no apprehension as such is contrary or repugnant to the person who apprehends. In fact, it is that person’s perfection. For the species by which apprehension occurs are perfections of the one apprehending. For this reason, no apprehension, either sensible or intelligible, speaking *per se*, causes sadness but only delight, sometimes more and sometimes less, according as the apprehension is more perfect and more fitting to the one who takes delight. This is why the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 10Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b14ff. that the most perfect—and thereby the most delightful—vision belongs to the faculty of sight that is best disposed to the most beautiful of the objects of vision.  

Now, it does happen accidentally that sadness is caused by apprehension in two ways. In one way, on the part of the one apprehending, namely, a person who apprehends through a physical organ, which can undergo weariness and corruption due to a change in the organ. And this is how an apprehension of the sensitive part accidentally causes sorrow, whereas intellective apprehension only does by an extremely remote accident, namely, inasmuch as the intellect’s consideration is joined to the activity of the sensitive powers, from which weariness can occur in our intellect’s consideration, which would not be the case if we were able to understand without phantasms. In the other way, on the part of the effect of apprehension, inasmuch as by having devoted time to apprehension we incur a kind of loss, for example, if we are thereby impeded from something more useful or drawn into sin by the occasion. But then, properly speaking, sadness is not caused by apprehension but by consideration of the effect of apprehension. And thus, properly speaking, there is no sadness contrary to the delight that comes from intellectual contemplation.  

The reply to the first and second objection is clear from what has been said.  

Reply Obj. 3: An activity contrary to a habit of knowledge sometimes proceeds from one knowingly. And then such an activity is fitting to the habit, since by a habit of knowledge we know not only what is right but also what is erroneous. Thus even knowing that something is erroneous pertains to the perfection of knowledge. If, however, a consideration contrary to knowledge proceeded from someone unknowingly, as for example when someone has a false view that he thinks is true, such contemplation will still be delightful because it is apprehended as fitting, even though it is not fitting, just as to a just man an unjust activity would be delightful if he thought that it were just.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that both delight and sadness result from some apprehended cause. Therefore, they can also be considered in two ways: on the part of the cause that delights or saddens, or on the part of the apprehension that delight or sadness follows. Now, the cause of delight is the fitting good attained, while the cause of sadness is the repugnant evil endured. But a good can be found that is completely fitting, having nothing disagreeable from any vantage point, whereas there cannot be found any evil that is so disagreeable as to be fitting in no regard whatsoever. For there is no thing that is purely evil so that it is not good in any respect. And thus the cause of delight is more perfectly related to giving delight than the cause of sadness is to saddening, generally speaking. But one cannot lay down one rule for all specific cases on account of the different causal orders in evilness and goodness. Consequently, some cause that brings delight can surpass one that causes sadness and vice versa. But on the part of apprehension, generally speaking, sadness surpasses delight in its genus by the fact that like things are less felt than contrary things. Therefore since fittingness causes delight while repugnance causes sadness, sadness prevails over delight as regards apprehension, though in particular cases a given delight can take precedence over a given sadness as regards apprehension. It should be said, then, that if we speak properly delight has a stronger effect than sadness, but sadness is felt more keenly than delight, as also we feel the love of a thing absent more keenly than the love of a thing present, as Augustine says in *On the Trinity* 10.See Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 10, ch. 12.  

Reply Obj. 1: We flee sadness more than we desire delight because we feel it more keenly.  

Reply Obj. 2: All sadness, speaking *per se*, is evil, as will be said below,In the following article, sub-question 2. though accidentally it can be good. But not every delight is good, speaking *per se*. Thus both the good and the wicked flee every sadness, but they do not all desire all delights.  

Reply Obj. 3: There are more ways in which an activity can be impeded than in which it can be perfected. For it is only perfected when all the causes come together, whereas an activity is impeded if any of the things necessary for the activity is impeded. And thus all sadness impedes an activity, inasmuch as it is contrary to the one carrying out the activity, but not all delight assists the work. In fact, extraneous delight impedes it inasmuch as it draws the intention away to something other than the attention necessary for perfect action. But proper delight even helps the one working, lending him a certain strength, inasmuch as it is fitting and leads his attention to focus on the delightful activity.  

Article 4  

***On the goodness or evil of delight and sadness***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fourth question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 34, a. 2; *Super Eth*. 7, lec. 11; *Super Eth*. 10, lec. 4 & 8. It seems that every delight is good. For anything that makes other things more worthy of choice when it is united with them is good *per se*. But delight united with any goods whatsoever renders them more worthy of choice. Therefore, delight as such is good. And so every delight will be good.  

Obj. 2: Only the good is desired. But anything is desirable from the fact that it is delightful. Therefore, delight, speaking *per se*, is good. And so the same follows as before.  

Obj. 3: All being precisely as being is good. Therefore, that, too, which is the ultimate perfection of being is universally good. But delight is the ultimate perfection of being. For it perfects activity, which is second act, as is clear from the Philosopher.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b23. Therefore, all delight is good.  

On the contrary (4), what the virtuous man flees is evil. But a person who is temperate flees delight. Therefore, delight is evil.  

Furthermore (5), what impedes the judgment of reason is evil. But delight corrupts the estimation of prudence, as it says in the *Ethics* 6.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 6, ch. 5, 1140b12–20. Therefore, delight is evil.  

Furthermore (6), anything whose desire makes someone evil is evil *per se*. But the desire for delight renders some people evil, as in the case of the intemperate, who pursue delight as an end, and flatterers, who only ever say sweet things to delight others. Therefore, delight is evil.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that not all sadness is evil.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 39, a. 1; *In III Sent*. d. 15, q. 2, a. 2, qa. 1, ad 3. For nothing salvific is evil. But there is a sadness that is salvific: *godly grief produces a repentance that leads to salvation* (2 Cor 7:10). Therefore, there is a good sadness.  

Obj. 2: Nothing praiseworthy is evil. But some sadnesses are praiseworthy, such as mercy, which is sadness at another’s evil, and penitence, which is sadness over past sins. Therefore, not all sadness is evil.  

Obj. 3: As delight is to good, sadness is to evil. But not all delight is good. Therefore, not every sadness is an evil.  

On the contrary (1), everything that impedes right activity is evil. But all sadness impedes activity, as is clear in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1175b16–23. Therefore, all sadness is evil.  

Furthermore (2), anything that, when added to something, makes it less good, is evil in itself. But sadness joined to any good makes it less worthy of choice. This is why it is said: *not with sadness or of necessity: for God loves a cheerful giver* (2 Cor 9:7). And the same argument can be made when sadness is joined to the act of any virtue. Therefore, sadness is evil in itself.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that some delight is the greatest good.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 34, a. 3; *Super Eth*. 7, lec. 11–14; *Super Eth*. 10, lec. 2. For what is the last end is the best. But delight is the last end. For, as it says in the *Ethics* 7: *it is the architectonic end*.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12, 1152b25. But the architectonic ends are more noble and desirable, as it says in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1, 1094a15. Note that here “architect” means, for Aristotle, either the contemplative philosopher or the political philosopher. Therefore, delight is the greatest good.  

Obj. 2: It is said in *On the Heavens* 2: *every thing is for the sake of its activity*.Aristotle, *On the Heavens*, Bk. 2, ch. 3, 286a7–8. But activity is for the sake of delight, since delight is the perfection of activity, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b23. Therefore, delight is the last end.  

Obj. 3: What is desired only for its own sake and never for the sake of something else is the last end. But that is what delight is. For it is foolish to ask why someone wishes to have delight. Therefore, delight is the last end.  

Obj. 4: What all people desire and with whose contrary there is nothing that can be chosen seems to be the greatest good and the last end. But all desire delight: children and beasts, wise men and fools. And no good would be capable of being chosen if it were sad. Nor would it be possible to persevere continuously in a good if it were sad. Therefore, delight is the greatest good.  

On the contrary (5), man’s last end and his greatest good is his beatitude. But beatitude is not delight but rather an activity, as is clear from what was said above. Therefore, delight is not the greatest good.  

Furthermore (6), according to Plato, delight is a sensible generation into some nature. But no generation is the greatest good, since generation is a movement toward perfection. Therefore, no delight can be the greatest good.  

Furthermore (7), everything that is chosen is chosen in its order to the greatest good. But, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 10,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 3, 1174a4–9. we would give our attention to many things even if they brought no delight, for example, seeing, remembering, knowing, and the possession of virtues. Therefore, delight is not the greatest good.  

Furthermore (8), everything that is ordered by nature to something else is not the last end. But nature directs delight to activity. Hence nature has attached delight to the activities of the nutritive and generative part, which are ordered to the preservation of the individual and the species, so that animals will be more inclined to these works. Therefore, delight is not the greatest good or the last end.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, delight can be considered in two ways: as delight or as this particular delight. Delight precisely as delight names a good. But inasmuch as it is a particular given delight, it can have the aspect of evil on the part of what specifies it, just as every action as regards the nature of action is good, but as regards a connected defect a given action can be evil. Likewise, too, inasmuch as something of defect is joined to a given delight, to this extent something of evil is joined to it. But defect is joined to delight in the manner in which it is also joined to activity, since delight is caused from activity. And in this way a deficient delight comes from a deficient activity. And thus, as it says in the *Ethics* 10,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5. as activities differ from one another in goodness and evilness, so do delights, such that in this regard a delight is called good when it proceeds from a good activity and evil when it proceeds from an evil activity.  

Reply Obj. 1: The delight that makes goods more worthy of choice is a thing’s proper delight, as when a just man takes delight in a just activity. But if the delights of what is intemperate were joined to just activities, they would not render them more delightful. Hence it does not follow that all delights are good.  

Reply Obj. 2: It is not only good that is desired but also apparent good. Now every delight from the fact that it is delight is good; no matter how evil it becomes from some circumstance, it has that whereby it can appear good and consequently why it can be desired. And this is how it is desirable to someone who does not possess right judgment. But to someone who has right judgment certain delights are not desirable, as one who is prudent and temperate does not desire the delights of what is intemperate.  

Reply Obj. 3: This argument does not prove that delight is good, except precisely as delight.  

But because by the arguments to the contrary some people wished to show that every delight is evil, we must also respond to them.  

Reply Obj. 4: A virtuous person does not flee every delight and, in fact, embraces proper delight, which is taking delight in the works of the virtues. He does, however, flee inordinate delights. And we concede that the latter are evil.  

Reply Obj. 5: Not every delight impedes the judgment of reason, but rather immoderate and extraneous delight, as physical delight disturbs reason’s judgment if it is intense. But spiritual delight strengthens the judgment of reason, since a person works more attentively on the things in which he takes delight, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1175a29ff. And so it also does not follow that every delight is evil.  

Reply Obj. 6: The delights that make a man wicked should not be called delights truly and simply, since they are not fitting to a man as man but rather as the good of man is corrupt in him in a certain way, just as what seems sweet to someone with a corrupt sense of taste should not be called sweet simply.See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1176a4ff. Yet even granting the supposition that they are true delights, they differ specifically from delights that are good.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that something can be called good in two ways: either as worthy of choice in itself, or as ordered to what is worthy of choice, as bitter potions are not good or desirable by themselves but only inasmuch as they are beneficial to health. Therefore no sadness, speaking *per se*, is good as being desirable in itself. For this reason, too, Augustine in the *Confessions*Augustine, *Confessions* (CCSL 27), Bk. 10, ch. 28. judges them (tribulations and sorrows) to be tolerated, not loved. The reason for this is that every sadness proceeds from something unfitting, but the possession of something unfitting cannot be desirable *per se*. But there are some sorrows that are good or desirable inasmuch as they are useful for something, whether to drive out perverse delights or some other such thing.  

And in this way the reply to the first objection is clear. For that sadness is said to be godly and salvific which is ordered to salvation. Thus it does not follow that it is good except as a kind of medicine.  

Reply Obj. 2: Sadness can only be praiseworthy on the supposition of an existing evil. For supposing that there is misery, mercy is praiseworthy. Likewise, in the case of pre-existing sin, penitence is worthy of choice. However, where there is the perfection of goods, there is no place either for mercy or penitence. Hence it does not follow that sadness is good simply, but only in a qualified sense.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though all sadness should be fled from in itself, it does not follow that all delight is in itself good and to be desired. For in order that something be good, many factors must come together and if any of them is lacking this brings in the character of evil. And thus, everything that possesses something of evil is to be fled from, though not everything that has something of the good is to be desired simply. And so all sadness, inasmuch as it possesses something of defect, is to be fled from. But not every delight is to be desired, even though it has something of the good in it.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that there have been three opinions on delight. One was that of the Epicureans, who posited that delight as such is the greatest good, thereby also being of the opinion that all delight is good. Another was the opinion of the Stoics and Platonists, who held that all delight is evil. The third was the opinion of Aristotle and the Peripatetics, who posited that some delights are good and others evil and that nothing prevents a delight from being the greatest good in the genus of human goods. For delight follows on activity. Therefore, since there is an activity that is the greatest good in the genus of human goods, which is happiness, the delight following on it will also be the greatest good. And these two should not be considered as two goods but rather as a single good. For just as from a perfection and the perfectible a single perfect thing results, so also from delight and activity comes about a single perfect activity, which is happiness, since delight is the perfection of activity, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 4, 1174b23.  

Now which of these should be chosen for the sake of the other, i.e., whether delight should be chosen for the sake of activity or vice versa, the Philosopher left unresolved in that place. But even so, it seems that we should say according to the truth of the matter, that delight is ordered to activity as to an end, as all secondary perfections exist for nothing else but the perfecting of their perfectible objects. But delight is a perfection superadded to activity, as comeliness is to youth. Hence delight is ordered to activity. In this way, then, properly speaking, no delight is the greatest good, but it is something belonging to the greatest good, namely happiness. For happiness includes delight, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7, 1177a23–26. And this is how both arguments should be answered.  

Reply Obj. 1: Delight is said to be the architect’s end, not because it it is the architectonic (i.e., civil) last end, except perhaps as something belonging to the last end, as has been said. Rather, it is called the architect’s end as a kind of rule by which the architect—i.e., the civil architect—judges everything. Hence the Philosopher adds in this passage: *by regarding which we call this good and this evil*.*Nicomachean Ethics*, bk. 7, ch. 11. For from the fact that one delights in good things he is known to be good, and one who delights in evils is known to be evil.  

Reply Obj. 2: Delight is not the perfection of an activity by which the activity has its species but rather one that is superadded to it after the manner of a secondary perfection, as health is related to man, and not as the soul is related to man. Now there is this difference between primary and secondary perfections: the perfectible is ordered to primary perfection as to an end, as matter is to form, but conversely secondary perfection is ordered to the perfectible, namely, so that the perfectible may have perfect being through it. And this is the way that delight is ordered to activity.  

Reply Obj. 3: Concerning things ordered to an end we usually ask on what account those that are not united to the end are done, whereas no such question arises in the case of those that are united to the end, since it is at once apparent that they are desirable. And because delight is united with perfect activity, which is the end, it is not usual to ask for what reason someone wishes to have delight. And besides, delight indicates the resting of the appetite in something. Therefore, to ask why someone wishes to have delight is the same as asking why he wishes his appetite to be at rest, which is the same as if we asked why someone wishes to obtain his will’s end. Now even though the end is an activity, ‘activity’ does not by its name imply the notion of end or of resting in the end. And this is why we ask, “what are you working for?” rather than “what are you taking delight for?”  

Reply Obj. 4: The end of any thing is its most perfect activity. Thus all desire delight, by which activity is perfected, just as they desire the perfection of their proper activity. And just as error can occur in the desire for an activity, since some focus on extraneous activities while neglecting proper activities, as do all the wicked who pursue the activities proper to beasts while omitting those belonging to man, so also some pursue extraneous delights in place of proper ones.  

To the arguments on the other side of the question:  

Reply Obj. 5: Happiness is not just any activity, but a perfect activity, which must be delightful. And this is how delight is something belonging to the greatest good.  

Reply Obj. 6: This definition that Plato gives is problematic, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 7 and 10.See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 12; Bk. 10, chs. 3–4. For delight consists not in generation but rather in having been generated, since a thing can have its proper activity, which is the cause of delight, when it is already perfect, but not when it is imperfect and in the process of becoming.  

The other two arguments conclude that activity is better than delight, which is true. But perfect activity is better than either, and delight is part of perfect activity.  

Article 5  

***On the comparison of spiritual delight to physical delight***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fifth question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 5; *Super Eth*. I, lec. 13; *Super Eth*. X, lec. 10; *Super Metaphys*. 12, lec. 8; *Super Ps*. 18. It seems that physical delights are more powerful than spiritual ones. For the delightful precisely as delightful is desirable. Therefore, what the appetite is more inclined to is more delightful. But it is inclined to physical delights in more cases and more frequently than to spiritual ones. Therefore, physical delights are more powerful than spiritual ones.  

Obj. 2: The thing itself has greater efficacy than the likeness of a thing. But physical delights, such as touch and taste, come from contact with things themselves, whereas spiritual delights, such as there are in contemplation, come from the likenesses of things, by which we understand things. Therefore, physical delights are more powerful than spiritual ones.  

Obj. 3: As Avicenna says in *On Natural Things* 5, the activity of present useful causes is more efficacious than the activity of future useful causes. Likewise, the activity of useful causes is more efficacious for delight than the activity of useful causes is for uprightness. Likewise, the activity of what is by opinion is more efficacious than what is by reason. But physical activities proceed from present goods that are useful for delight according to an opinion of the good, whereas spiritual ones proceed especially from future upright goods that are in accord with reason. Therefore, physical delight is more powerful than spiritual delight.  

Obj. 4: Physical delights sometimes distract reason by their efficacy and cause a change in the body. And thus they need to be held in check, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7: *the strong desires for venereal pleasures manifestly effect a change in the body and even produce insanity in some*.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 3, 1147a14–17. Spiritual delights, however, do not seem to do this. Hence there is no need to hold them in check. Therefore, physical delights are more powerful than spiritual ones.  

On the contrary (1), the PhilosopherAristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7, 1177a23–25. says in the *Ethics* 10 that: *the most delightful activity is the one that corresponds to wisdom. For philosophy seems to have wondrous delights in purity and steadfastness*. And this is also what is said from the mouth of wisdom: *the remembrance of me is sweeter than honey, and my inheritance sweeter than the honeycomb* (Sir 24:20).  

Furthermore (2), the good is the cause of delight. But spiritual good has preeminence over physical good. Therefore so too does spiritual delight have preeminence over corporeal delight.  

Furthermore (3), what is whiter has less mixture of black. But physical delight has sadness mixed with it, which is contrary to it, whereas spiritual delight has no contrary sadness that can mix with it. Therefore, spiritual delight is more powerful than physical delight.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that among physical delights what has to do with touch is not the most powerful.Parallels: *ST* I-II, q. 31, a. 6; *ST* II-II, q. 141, a. 4; *De malo*, q. 14, a. 4, ad 1. For spiritual delight is more powerful than physical delight. Therefore, the closer a physical delight is to spiritual delight, the more powerful it is. But the delight of sight and hearing is nearer to spiritual delight than the delight of touch. Therefore, it is more powerful.  

Obj. 2: The cause of delight is the perception of the fitting. But among the other senses sight is more perceptive and capable of knowledge, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Metaphysics* 1.Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1, 980a23–27. Therefore, the delight of sight is also the most powerful among physical delights.  

Obj. 3: The delights of touch are especially in the acts of the nutritive and generative parts. But these acts are also common to plants, in which no delight is found. Therefore, touch has a minimum of delight.  

On the contrary (1), temperance concerns the greatest physical delights and sadnesses. But it concerns the delights of touch, as it says in the *Ethics* 3.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 3, ch. 10, 1118a17–1118b8. Therefore, the delights of touch are more powerful than other physical delights.  

Furthermore (2), pain is opposed to physical delight. But pain pertains especially to the sense of touch, since it comes from the division of what is continuous. Therefore, among physical delights, too, the delight of touch is more powerful.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that among spiritual delights, delight in works of the active life is more powerful than delight in the works of the contemplative life. For delight pertains to the affections. But the active life depends on rectitude of the will, whereas the contemplative life consists more in perfection of the intellect.
