Therefore, delight in works of the active life is more powerful than delight in works of the contemplative life.  

Obj. 2: The cause of delight is the activity of a connatural habit. But the habit of the moral virtues, which give perfection in the active life, moves more after the manner of nature than the habit of the intellectual virtues. For one who has knowledge can consider something false without difficulty, but a just man cannot without difficult do unjust works, as it says in the *Ethics* 5.See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 5, ch. 9, 1137a5–25. Therefore, the delight of the moral virtues, which pertain to the active life, is more powerful than the delight of the intellectual virtues, which pertain to the contemplative life.  

Obj. 3: Fittingness is an aspect of delight. But delight shares more with moral virtue than with intellectual virtue: we posit both moral virtue and malice in relation to delight and sadness, as it says in the *Ethics* 2 and 7.The references here are to the entire books, which make this point repeatedly. Therefore, the delight of the active life is more powerful than the delight of the contemplative life.  

On the contrary (1), the activities of the contemplative life have no admixture of bitterness or tedium. Hence: *companionship with her*, namely wisdom, *has no bitterness, and life with her has no pain* (Wis 8:16). But the activities of the active life are sprinkled with many bitternesses. Therefore, the delights of the contemplative life are more powerful.  

Furthermore (2), the more delightful an activity is, the more it can be continued. But the activity of contemplation can be continued most of all, as it says in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7, 1177a21–23. Therefore, the activity of contemplation is most delightful.  

Quaestiuncula 4  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that there is equal delight in all the blessed. For, as a GlossCf. *Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:548–49. says on the passage *star differs from star in glory* (1 Cor 15:41), there will be equal joy in unequal brightness.  

Obj. 2: There will be perfect charity in the blessed. And so each will perfectly fulfill the precept: *you shall love your neighbor as yourself* (Matt 22:39). Therefore, each will only rejoice at the good of another as at his own good. And so it seems that all will possess equal joy.  

Obj. 3: Joy is only lessened by some impediment. But there will be no impediment in any of the blessed. Since, then, there is one reason for everyone’s act of rejoicing, namely the vision of God, it seems that all will rejoice equally.  

On the contrary (1), men are taken up on the basis of difference of merits to the different orders of angels, as Gregory says.See Gregory the Great, *Homilies on the Gospels* (CCSL 141), Bk. 2, homily 34. But there is not equal joy in all the angels. Hence DionysiusPseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 7 (PG 3:206). says that the supreme hierarchy alone has been made worthy to share the divine refreshment in household unity, ‘refection’ being understood as delight. Therefore, not all the blessed will rejoice equally.  

Furthermore (2), the vision of God in the blessed will be the cause of delight. But some saints will see God more clearly, as said above. Therefore, they will not all take delight equally.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, just as something is said to be more known in two ways, either simply or in relation to us, so also one delight can be judged as more powerful than another either simply or in relation to us.  

Speaking simply, then, spiritual delight has an incomparable preeminence over physical delight for six reasons. First, because spiritual perfection, which causes spiritual delight, incomparably surpasses physical perfection, which causes physical delight.  

Second, because, being better, it is more fitting to the one who takes delight. For each person is most of all what is most perfect in him, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 10.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 7, 1178a5–8. But in us spiritual nature is the most perfect, in respect of which spiritual things are fitting to us, as in each the form is more powerful than the matter. And what befits us according to spiritual nature is more fitting to us. And for this reason, as far as it itself is concerned, it is such as to give greater delight.  

Third, because spiritual perfection is naturally more delighted in than physical perfection. And this is clear from experience. For men sustain many physical harms in order to avoid infamy or to possess victory or eminence, which are intelligible perfections, not physical ones. But the more something is loved, the more it is delighted in when possessed. For this reason the Philosopher says in the *Parts of Animals* 1Aristotle, *Parts of Animals*, Bk. 1, ch. 5. that when the lover loves something, he is drawn to what he comprehends of it, even though sometimes what he comprehends is little in comparison to the many things he comprehends of other things. And in this way the little knowledge that we have of heavenly spiritual substances is most delightful to us.  

Fourth, since union is the cause of delight, the more intensely something is united with us, the more delight it gives. But spiritual, intellective things are united with us more intimately than physical things apprehended through the senses. For the senses are joined through apprehension to physical things as it were only on the surface. But the intellect penetrates to the depth of a thing’s quiddity. This is why intelligible delight is more powerful than sensible delight.  

Fifth, since activity is the cause of delight by the fact that it proceeds from someone doing it perfectly, as when someone is perfected by a habit, the more perfection is found in the doer regarding the activity, the more delightful his activity is. But motion is the act of the imperfect. For this reason activities mixed with motion fall short of the perfection of delight to the extent that they are connected with motion. For from motion they give rise to weariness. And thus since motion is joined to physical activities, whereas spiritual activities are removed from motion, spiritual delights will be more pure than physical delights and, consequently, have greater intensity.  

Sixth, because spiritual delights are longer-lasting and more stable than physical delights, as proceeding from incorruptible and immovable causes.  

On the other hand, speaking of delight in relation to us, physical delight is more perceptible to us for four reasons. First, because we perceive sensible, physical things more than spiritual things by the fact that we must arrive at intelligible things from sensible ones. Now before there can be delight, perception of what is fitting is required. And so it is that more are inclined to physical delights than to spiritual ones, since more know sensible things than intelligible ones.  

Second, because of the disposition of our affections, which are used to physical delights. For this reason, just as a diseased sense of taste does not perceive pure sweetness, so also our affections, being polluted by physical delights, are not able to perceive those delights which are most pure, namely spiritual ones. And thus virtuous men, who have purified their affections from physical delights, enjoy spiritual delights more. This is also why the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 10Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 5, 1176a15–19. that the delight which seems more powerful to one who is virtuous is the most powerful of all.  

Third, because physical delights are closer to physical motive powers. And so from physical delights and pains an immediate change happens in the body. But this is not the case with spiritual delights and sadnesses, unless they are so strong that they overflow into the sensible appetite. Now bodily changes are most keenly perceived. This is also why physical delight is more perceived than spiritual delight.  

Fourth, because, as Augustine says in the *Confessions* 8,Augustine, *Confessions* (CCSL 27), Bk. 8, ch. 3. joy or delight follows more where greater trouble preceded. Now in physical matters we are continuously troubled by continuous motions, all of which come with a certain exertion. But in spiritual matters we find no such troubling. For this reason physical delights appear greater, as more closely affording a remedy against physical troubles. Therefore, according to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 7,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 14, 1154a26–28. physical delights are desired as a kind of medicine against physical troubles. And for this reason the young, in whom nature continuously labors from the growth of the body and other motions, are more drawn to physical delights. Likewise, too, the melancholic, whose bodies always feel the bite of their agitated constitution, have an intense appetite for pleasure, as if always in need of medicine, as the Philosopher says in the same place.Ibid., 1154b11–14.  

Reply Obj. 1: This argument proceeds from a consideration of delights in relation to us.  

Reply Obj. 2: Through the spiritual we abstract we reach the inner depths more than through the real union offered to the senses, since touch and taste only know the accidents, whereas the intellect knows the quiddity itself of the thing.  

Reply Obj. 3: Avicenna is speaking in this passage about the comparison of delights in relation to us. This is why he says the opposite in his *Metaphysics*, when speaking about the comparison of delights in themselves. There he says that physical delights do not even deserve the name ‘delight’ by comparison to spiritual ones. Or else it may be said that Avicenna is speaking in this passage about the delights that consist in passion, which come about through the motion of the bodily spirit of an animal. For he is of the opinion that sensible delights of this sort come less from spiritual things than physical ones.  

Reply Obj. 4: Because physical delights cause a change in the body more than spiritual ones, they naturally impede reason, as do other bodily changes. But spiritual delights, though they seize the mind for themselves, in no respect impede the use of reason. For this reason they are given the name ‘sober.’ Thus we can find over-abundance in corporeal delights, according to the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 7,Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 10, ch. 14, 1154a10–17. since they are not worthy of choice to man in themselves but only accidentally, namely, as being medicines staving off corporeal sadnesses. And thus they should be desired with the preservation of a certain measure, which reason affixes to them. And if they go beyond this measure, they are deemed harmful. But spiritual delights are desired in themselves, as connatural to man, not as medicines, since they have no contrary sadnesses. And thus reason affixes no measure to spiritual delights. Rather, the greater they are the more worthy they are of being chosen.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that the delights of the sense of touch are compared to the delights of the other senses in two ways.  

In one way, as regards intensity. And in this way, the delights of touch are more intense than others for four reasons. First, because the object of touch is really joined to us, whereas the objects of sight, hearing, and smell are joined to us through something of theirs, although none of the senses can enter into the interiority of a thing. But a physical thing is more efficacious in giving delight physically than the species of the thing or something caused by the thing.  

Second, because the objects of touch are more connatural to us due to the fact that an animal is constituted by hot, cold, moist, and dry. And thus objects of touch bring greater delight to us than other sensible objects, just as they also bring greater harm. For when they are excessive, they corrupt not only one sense but the whole animal.  

Third, since the sense of touch is like the foundation of the senses, by reason of which, when its organ is immobilized all the senses are immobilized, as it says in *On Sleeping and Waking*.Aristotle, *On Sleep*, ch. 1, 454b9–455a2. What occurs in touch, both in the case of delight and in the case of harm, overflows into the other senses. And for this reason the delight of touch is the more intense among physical delights.  

Fourth, because the delights of touch concern what is necessary for the preservation of the individual and the species, as in food, drink, and reproduction. And for this reason the greatest delight is attached to them, in order that animals not neglect them. And so it is that temperance concerns these delights most of all, as it says in the *Ethics* 3.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 3, ch. 10, 1118a17–1118b8.  

In the second way, the delights of touch are compared to other delights as regards nobility. And in this way they are more material and more bestial and, consequently, less noble. For the other senses receive their objects in a more spiritual manner than touch. This is also why other animals only delight in the other senses as ordered to touch, as it says in the *Ethics* 3,Ibid., 1118a17–22. In various places Aristotle notes how human sense-knowledge is connected with delight in knowledge as such. See, e.g., *Sense and Sensibilia*, ch. 1, 437a4–16; *Metaphysics*, Bk. 1, ch. 1, 980a20-b25. but man delights in the others even as ordered to knowledge alone.  

Reply Obj. 1: From the fact that the delights of the other senses are closer to spiritual delights than the delights of touch, we can gather that they are more noble, but not that they are more intense, since they do not transcend the mode of physical delights.  

Reply Obj. 2: Even though sight is more perceptive than the other senses, its perception is according to a lesser joining to the thing than in the sense of touch. And thus the delight that pertains to the sensible object of delight is more through touch than through vision. Nonetheless, delight that pertains to the knowledge of sight is greater than the delight that pertains to the knowledge of touch. But other animals do not delight in sensing in this way, but only men in order to the knowledge that they acquire from the senses. Hence such delight falls in a certain way in the genus of spiritual delights.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though those things that the delights of touch especially pertain to are found in plants, the plants do not perceive them. And so plants’ lack of delight does not stem from a lack of delightful objects, but from the lack of perception.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that the delights in the works of the speculative life have preeminence over the delights in the works of the active life, for four reasons.  

First, because they are more noble, as coming from more noble things. For the acts of the active life follow on the turning of the soul’s lower part to the things below it, like the passions, which the moral virtues correspond to, and the uses of external goods, in which some virtues, such as justice and liberality, consist. But the acts of the contemplative life exist insofar as the soul through the higher reason is turned to eternal things, which are above it.  

Second, because they are more fitting. For just as reason in us is more excellent than the senses, so also what is more fitting to reason is more fitting to us than what befits the senses. Likewise, the speculative intellect takes precedence over the practical. For this reason the perfections of the speculative intellect are more fitting to man.  

Third, because they are more pure. For the works of the active life concern the passions of the sensitive part and physical motions. And so they have an admixture of weariness and materiality, which is not the case in spiritual delights.  

Fourth, because spiritual delights are more perfect. For man requires many others for the works of the active life. But in the works of the contemplative life each person is more sufficient to himself. And so the delight of the contemplative life does not suffer from deficiency in the way that the delight of the active life does due to there being something lacking of the things required for the activity’s perfection.  

Reply Obj. 1: The affections are related equally to both the active and the contemplative life. For just as someone may be willing to carry out works of the active life, someone may also be willing to carry out works of the contemplative life. And so in the affections there is delight in common in both cases. But the cause of the delight of the contemplative life is in the intellect as regards the higher part, while the cause of the delight of the active life is in the lower part of the soul. Now the affections are not less affected by what belongs to the higher part of reason than what belongs to the lower part, but rather more affected.  

Reply Obj. 2: A habit inclines to act in two ways. In one way, to the act’s being carried out. And in this way to incline to act only belongs to a habit in the appetitive part. For the appetite that has the good and end as its object moves itself and the cognitive power to act. For the fact that one carries out some action, if it is not from a necessity of nature, arises from the desire for the act or end. In the other way, as regards the mode of acting, namely, that what is done be done rightly. And every habit inclines to act in this way. Therefore, a contemplative habit such as wisdom or understanding does not incline a person to use the act of contemplation, but rather to use it rightly when he uses it. But for one to use it comes from the inclination of a habit not in the lower appetite but of one in the higher, such as charity. For this reason charity also pertains to the contemplative life, as inclining contemplative habits to their acts. But the habits of the active life, such as the moral virtues, are in the appetitive part, as fortitude and temperance are in the irascible and concupiscible. And so these habits incline both to the doing of an act and to its being done rightly. And as far as this goes they are compared to nature, which is the principle of motion. Nevertheless, what a speculative habit inclines to is more connatural to us than what an active habit inclines to.  

Reply Obj. 3: The cause of delight is fittingness, not the fittingness of the delight but rather of the cause of the delight. But delights that correspond to moral virtue are physical delights or are mixed in with physical affairs, which are the matter of the moral virtues. But other more noble delights, namely spiritual ones, apply to the contemplative life, not that delight is the matter of contemplation but rather that it is a consequence of contemplation.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 4  

To the fourth question, it should be said that the intensity of delight can come from two factors: from a cause of delight that is stronger and from a disposition in the one who takes delight that has greater capacity for delight. Now the proximate cause of delight is activity, whereas the remote cause is the object of the activity, as in the delight of the blessed which we are now discussing, the proximate cause is the vision of God, but the primary cause is God. This primary cause, then, is one and common to all the blessed. Therefore in this regard their delight is equal. The vision and disposition for delight, however, are not equal, since one person sees more clearly than another, as is clear from what was said above. Also, one person is more capable of delight than another on account of a more perfect habit, which is the light of glory. And thus some delight more than others, though in a qualified sense all delight equally, materially speaking, i.e., inasmuch as they rejoice over an equal thing, namely God. And this is how the Gloss that was cited should be understood, that there will be equal joy in unequal clarity.’Clarity’ here could also be translated ‘glory.’  

And so the reply to the first objection is clear. Or else we may say to it that all the blessed are said to have equal joy inasmuch as each arrives at the height of joy according to his own mode. But the mode of one will be more ample than that of another. And in this regard the joy of one will be greater than another’s joy, such that the parity implies an equality not of quantity but of proportion.They are all equal inasmuch as the fullness or actualization of one’s capacity stands to that capacity as the fullness of another’s stands to that other’s.  

Reply Obj. 2: Even though in the kingdom of heaven the lesser rejoices not only at his own good but also at the good of one higher than him, he does not rejoice in it as much as his superior does. For God will be the reason for joy in all. And thus the more a person rejoices in God, the more he will rejoice in anything else. Hence the higher will rejoice more at the good of the lower than even the lower himself.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though there is no impediment to delight in any of the blessed, there are in them other causes of intensity. For something is intensified not only by moving away from the contrary but also by approaching the terminus.  

Question 4  

***The dowries of the blessed***

***Next, the dowries must be inquired about; and here five questions arise:***

first, whether any dowries should be assigned to the blessed;  

- second, whether a dowry differs from beatitude;
- third, whether there are dowries in Christ;
- fourth, whether they are in the angels;
- fifth, on the number of the dowries.
- Article 1

***Whether any dowries should be assigned to the blessed***

Obj. 1: To the first question, we proceed thus. It seems that we should not posit any dowries in blessed men. For a dowry is given by law to the bridegroom for undertaking the burdens of marriage. But the saints bear not the figure of the bridegroom but rather the figure of the bride, inasmuch as they are members of the Church. Therefore, dowries are not given to them.  

Obj. 2: Dowries are given by law not by the father of the bridegroom but by the father of the bride. But all the gifts of beatitude are given to the blessed by the Father of the Bridegroom, namely Christ: *every good endowment and every perfect gift is from above, coming down from the Father of lights* (Jas 1:17). Therefore, the sort of gifts given to the blessed should not be called dowries.  

Obj. 3: Dowries are given in marriage for the easier bearing of the burdens of marriage. But there are no burdens in spiritual marriage, especially in the state of the Church triumphant. Therefore, we should assign no dowries there.  

Obj. 4: Dowries are only given for the sake of marriage. But spiritual marriage is contracted with Christ through faith during the state of the Church militant. Therefore, for the same reason if any dowries apply to the blessed, they will also apply to the holy ones in the wayfaring state. But they do not apply to the latter. Therefore, neither do they apply to the blessed.  

Obj. 5: Dowries have to do with external goods, which are called goods of fortune. But the rewards of the blessed will pertain to internal goods. Therefore, dowries should not be counted among them.  

On the contrary (1), it is said: *this is a great sacrament: but I speak in Christ and in the Church* (Eph 5:32). From this we hold that spiritual marriage is signified by fleshly marriage. But in fleshly marriage the bride endowed with a dowry is led into the bridegroom’s home. Therefore, since the saints are led into the home of Christ when they are beatified, it seems that they are endowed with certain dowries.  

Furthermore (2), in physical marriage the dowries are assigned for the solace of the marriage. But spiritual marriage is more delightful than corporeal marriage. Therefore, it should be assigned dowries most of all.  

Furthermore (3), the bride’s adornments pertain to the dowry. But the saints are led adorned into glory, as it is said: *he has clothed me with the garments of salvation . .* . *as a bride adorns herself with her jewels* (Is 61:10). Therefore, the saints in heaven are in possession of dowries.  

I answer that, without a doubt, when the blessed are transferred to glory they are divinely granted certain gifts for their adornment. And these adornments are given the name of ‘dowries’ by the masters. Hence a kind of definition of ‘dowry’ is given, in the sense in which we are now speaking: *a dowry is a perpetual adornment of the soul and body, sufficient for life, continually perduring in eternal beatitude*. And this description is taken after the likeness of a corporeal dowry, by which a bride is adorned and provision is made to the bridegroom whereby he can sufficiently nurture his bride and children. Even so, the dowry is preserved for the bride in a way that cannot be lost such that it reverts to her if the marriage is dissolved. But on the meaning of the name different people are of different opinions.  

For some say that ‘dowry’ is not taken from any likeness to corporeal marriage, but rather according to the manner of speaking by which we call every perfection or adornment of any man a dowry, as someone is said to be ‘endowed’ with knowledge who abounds in knowledge. And this is the way in which Ovid used the term ‘endowment’ when he said: *and be pleasing by whatever endowment you are able*.Ovid, *Ars amatoria* I.596. But this does not seem altogether fitting. For whenever a term is applied to signify something principally, it is not usually transferred to other things except according to a likeness. Hence since the first established meaning of the term ‘dowry’ pertains to fleshly marriage, it seems that in any other instance we will find some likeness to the principal signified object.  

And thus others say that we find a likeness in this regard: that a ‘dowry’ properly refers to a gift that is given in corporeal marriage on the part of the bridegroom to the bride when the bride is led into the bridegroom’s home, pertaining to the bride’s adornment. This is clear from what Shechem said to Jacob and his sons: *ask of me ever so much as marriage present and gift* (Gen 34:12), and from Exodus 22:16: *if a man seduces a virgin . . . and lies with her, he shall give the marriage present for her, and make her his wife*. This is why the adornment furnished to the saints by Christ when they are led into the house of glory is called a dowry as well.  

But this is manifestly contrary to what the jurists say, to whom it pertains to treat of these matters. For they say that a dowry is properly a kind of giving on the woman’s side to those who are on the man’s side, given because of the burden of marriage that the man sustains. But what the bridegroom gives the bride is called a brideprice. And the dowry in 1 Kings 9:16 is understood in this way, where it is said that Pharaoh, the king of Egypt, captured Gezer and gave it as dowry to his daughter, the wife of Solomon.  

Nor do the authorities cited contradict this. For even though dowries are usually assigned by the parent of the girl, it is sometimes the case that the bridegroom or the bridegroom’s father assigns dowries in place of the girl’s father. This happens in two ways. Either it is from great affection for the bride, as was the case with Shechem, whose father wished to bestow the dowry he should have received because of his son’s strong love for the girl. Or it occurs as a punishment to the bridegroom so that he bestows a dowry from his own wealth on a virgin he has corrupted, which the girl’s father would have had to assign. And this is the case of which Moses is speaking in the authority cited.  

And thus, according to others, it should be said that in fleshly marriage a dowry is properly said to be what is given by those on the woman’s side to those on the man’s side for sustaining the burdens of marriage, as has been said. But then the difficulty remains of how this signification can be applied to the case in question, since the adornments in beatitude are given to the spiritual bride by the Father of the Bridegroom. This will be made clear by replying to the arguments.  

Reply Obj. 1: Even though in fleshly marriage dowries are allotted to the bridegroom for his use, their ownership and dominion pertain to the bride, as is clear from the fact that if the marriage is dissolved the dowry remains legally the bride’s. And so also in spiritual marriage the adornments themselves that are given to the spiritual bride, namely the Church in her members, belong to the Bridegroom himself inasmuch as they yield glory and honor to him, but to the bride inasmuch as she is adorned by them.  

Reply Obj. 2: The Father of the Bridegroom, namely of Christ, is the person of the Father alone, but the Father of the bride is the entire Trinity. Now effects in creation pertain to the entire Trinity. Hence dowries of this sort in spiritual marriage, properly speaking, are given rather by the Father of the bride than by the Father of the Bridegroom. But even though this conferral is done by all the persons of the Trinity, it can be appropriated to each person in some way: to the person of the Father as giving, since in him is authority and paternity with respect to creation is appropriated to him as well, as was said in Book I, Distinction 18, Question 1, Article 5, so that in this way the Father of the Bridegroom and of the bride may be the same; to the Son inasmuch as they are given for him and through him; and finally to the Holy Spirit inasmuch as they are given in him and according to him. For love is the reason for all giving.  

Reply Obj. 3: What is brought about by a dowry, namely, support for marriage, applies to a dowry *per se*. But what is removed by a dowry, namely the burden of marriage, which is relieved by way of a dowry, applies to it incidentally, just as it applies to grace *per se* to make a person just, but it is incidental that it makes a just person out of a wicked one. Therefore, even though in spiritual marriage there are no burdens, there is in it the utmost joyfulness and dowries are conferred on the bride to bring this joyfulness to perfection, i.e., so that through them she may be united with the Bridegroom with the utmost delight.  

Reply Obj. 4: Dowries were not customarily bestowed on the bride when espoused but when she was led into the bridegroom’s home, such that she held the bridegroom face to face. But as long as we are in this life *we are away from the Lord* (2 Cor 5:6). And thus it is not the gifts that are conferred on the saints in this life that are called dowries but instead those that are conferred on them when they are transferred into glory, in which they enjoy the Bridegroom face to face.  

Reply Obj. 5: In spiritual marriage interior beauty is required, as it says in Psalm 45 (44): 13: *all glorious is the princess within*. But in corporeal marriage external ornament is required. And for this reason the kind of dowries that are bestowed in corporeal marriage do not need to be bestowed in spiritual marriage.  

Article 2  

***Whether a dowry differs from beatitude***

Obj. 1: To the second question, we proceed thus.Parallel: below, a. 5, qa. 1, ad 5. It seems that a dowry is the same as beatitude. For as is clear from the definition above, it is *an adornment of soul and body, continually perduring in eternal beatitude*. But beatitude of the soul is a kind of adornment of it. Therefore, beatitude is a dowry.  

Obj. 2: Whatever imbues the bride’s union with the bridegroom with delight is called a dowry. But beatitude does this in spiritual marriage. Therefore, beatitude is a dowry.  

Obj. 3: Vision, according to Augustine,Augustine, *Explanation of the Psalms*, Ps. 90, serm. 2, par. 13: “tota merces nostra uisio est.” is the whole substance of beatitude. But vision is included as one of the dowries. Therefore, beatitude is a dowry.  

Obj. 4: Enjoyment makes a person blessed. But enjoyment is a dowry. Therefore, a dowry makes a person blessed, and thus it is beatitude.  

Obj. 5: According to Boethius in the *Consolation of Philosophy* 3,Boethius, *Consolation of Philosophy* (CCSL 94), Bk. 3, prose 2. beatitude is the state perfected by the gathering together of all goods. But the state of the blessed is perfected by the dowries. Therefore, dowries are parts of beatitude.  

On the contrary (1), a dowry is given without merits. But beatitude is not given but rendered. Therefore, beatitude is not a dowry.  

Furthermore (2), beatitude is only one, whereas the dowries are more than one. Therefore, beatitude is not a dowry.  

Furthermore (3), beatitude is in a person according to what is most powerful in him, as it says in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 4, 1095a14–20. But a dowry is also applied to the body. Therefore, dowry and beatitude are not the same.  

I answer that, concerning this there are two opinions. For some say that beatitude and dowry are really the same though they differ logically. For a dowry regards the spiritual marriage between Christ and the soul, whereas beatitude does not. But, as it seems, this cannot be the case, since beatitude consists in activity, while a dowry is not an activity but rather a quality or a kind of disposition. And thus we should say, according to others, that beatitude and dowry really differ such that beatitude refers to the perfect activity itself by which the blessed soul is joined to God but dowries refer to habits, dispositions, or whatever other qualities that are ordered to this perfect activity. In this way the dowries are ordered to beatitude rather than being in beatitude as parts of it.  

Reply Obj. 1: Beatitude, properly speaking, is not an adornment of the soul but is something that comes from the adornment of the soul, since beatitude is a kind of activity. Adornment, on the other hand, refers to a loveliness of the blessed person himself.  

Reply Obj. 2: Beatitude is not ordered to the union but rather is itself the union of the soul with Christ, which is by an activity. But dowries are gifts disposing to this sort of union.  

Reply Obj. 3: Vision can be taken in two ways. In one way, actually, i.e., for the act of vision itself. And in this way it is not a dowry but beatitude itself. In the other way, it can be taken as habitual, i.e., for the habit by which such an activity is elicited, i.e., for that very brightness of glory by which the soul is divinely enlightened to see God. And in this way it is a dowry and the principle of beatitude, though not beatitude itself.  

And the same thing should be said to the fourth objection concerning enjoyment.  

Reply Obj. 5: Beatitude gathers together all goods, not as parts of the essence of beatitude but as in a way ordered to beatitude, as said above.  

Article 3  

***Whether Christ has dowries***

Obj. 1: To the third question, we proceed thus. It seems that it belongs to Christ to have dowries. For the saints will be conformed to Christ through glory. Thus it is said: *who will change our lowly body to be like his glorious body* (Phil 3:21). Therefore Christ, too, has dowries.  

Obj. 2: Dowries are assigned in spiritual marriage after a likeness to corporeal marriage. But in Christ there is found a kind of spiritual marriage that is unique to him, namely that of two natures in one person, to the extent that the human nature in him is said to be espoused by the Word, as is clear from a Gloss on the passage: *he hath set his tabernacle in the sun, as a bridegroom leaving his chamber* (Ps 19:4), as well as: *behold the tabernacle of God with men* (Rev 21:3). Therefore, it belongs to Christ to have dowries.The word “tabernacle” is read here in the sense of a bridal chamber.  

Obj. 3: As Augustine says in *On Christian Doctrine*,Augustine, *On Christian Doctrine*, Bk. 3, ch. 31. in accord with the rule of Ticonius, Christ refers to himself not only as Bridegroom but also as bride on account of the unity of the mystical body between head and members, as is clear: *as a bridegroom decks himself with a garland, and as a bride adorns herself with her jewels* (Is 61:10). Since, then, brides are due dowries, we must, it seems, hold that there are dowries in Christ.  

Obj. 4: A dowry is due to all the members of the Church, since the Church is the bride. But Christ is a member of the Church, as is clear: *now you are the body of Christ and members of a member* (1 Cor 12:27), where a Gloss*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:542. explains *of Christ*. Therefore, dowries are due to Christ.  

Obj. 5: Christ has perfect vision, enjoyment, and delight. But these are included as dowries. Therefore, Christ had dowries.  

On the contrary (1), there is a distinction of persons between bridegroom and bride. But in Christ there is nothing personally distinct from the Son of God, who is the Bridegroom, as is clear: *he who has the bride is the bridegroom* (Jn 3:29). Therefore, since the dowries are assigned to the bride or for the bride, it seems that it does not belong to Christ to have dowries.  

Furthermore (2), the same person does not give dowries and receive them. But Christ is the one who gives spiritual dowries. Therefore, it does not belong to Christ to have dowries.  

I answer that, concerning this difficulty there are two opinions.  

For some say that in Christ there is a threefold union: one that is called consensual, by which he is united to God through a connection of love; another conferring dignity, by which the human nature is united to the divine; third, that by which Christ is united to the Church. They say, then, that it belongs to Christ to have dowries under the account of dowry according to the first two unions, though with regard to the third a dowry most excellently befits him, though not under the account of dowry, since in such a union Christ is like the Bridegroom and the Church the bride. And a dowry is given to a bride as regards property and dominion, though it is given to the bridegroom as regards use. But this does not seem fitting. For in that union by which Christ is joined to the Father through the consent of love, even as God, there is not said to be a marriage, since there is not any subjection there, which there must be in the bride with respect to the bridegroom. Neither can the notion of dowry be found properly in the union of the human nature to the divine in the unity of the person or even through a conformity of will, for three reasons. First, because a conformity of nature between bridegroom and bride is required in a marriage in which dowries are given, and this is lacking in the joining of the human nature to the divine. Second, because there is required in this case a distinction of persons, but the human nature is not personally distinct from the Word. Third, because a dowry is given when the bride is newly led into the bridegroom’s home, and this seems to pertain to the bride, who goes from not being joined to being joined. But the human nature, which is assumed by the Word into unity of person, was never something not perfectly joined.  

Hence we must say, with others, that either the aspect of dowry does not at all befit Christ or that it does not befit him properly as in the other saints, though what the dowries indicate befit him in a most excellent manner.  

Reply Obj. 1: This conforming should be understood in accord with what a dowry is, not in accord with the account of a dowry that is in Christ. For it is not necessary for what we are conformed to Christ in to exist in us and in Christ in the same way.  

Reply Obj. 2: The human nature is not properly said to be a bride in the union by which it is joined to the Word, since there is no distinction of persons preserved in that case, which is required between bridegroom and bride. But that the human nature is sometimes said to be espoused to the extent that it is joined to the Word is inasmuch as it possesses the act of the bride, i.e., because it is inseparably joined, and because in this joining the human nature is lower than the Word and is ruled by the Word as a bride is ruled by her bridegroom.  

Reply Obj. 3: The fact that Christ is sometimes called a bride is not because he is truly a bride but inasmuch as he assumes the person of his bride, namely the Church, who is spiritually joined to him. Hence nothing prevents that after this manner of speaking he could be said to have dowries, not that he himself has them, but because the Church has them.  

Reply Obj. 4: The name ‘Church’ is taken in two ways. Sometimes it names only the body which is joined to Christ as to its head. And in this way the Church alone has the character of the bride and Christ is not a member of the Church but is the head of the Church influencing all the Church’s members. In the other way, ‘Church’ is taken as naming the head and the members joined to it. And in this way Christ is called a member of the Church, inasmuch as he has an office distinct from all others, namely to give an influx of life to the others, although he is not very properly called a member, since ‘member’ implies a kind of partiality. In Christ, however, the spiritual good is not parted out but is whole and integral. Hence he himself is the Church’s whole good, nor is he together with others any greater than he by himself. But speaking in this way of the Church, the Church names not only the bride but also the bridegroom and bride, as through the spiritual union the two become one. Hence, granted that Christ is in a way called a member of the Church, he can in no way be called a member of the bride. And so the notion of dowry does not apply to him.  

Reply Obj. 5: This argument commits the fallacy of accident. For they do not befit Christ under the notion of dowry.  

Article 4  

***Whether the angels have dowries***

Obj. 1: To the fourth question, we proceed thus. It seems that the angels have dowries. For on the passage: *my dove, my perfect one, is only one* (Song 6:8), a Gloss says: *there is one Church in men and angels*. But the Church is the bride, and so it befits the Church’s members to have dowries. Therefore, the angels have dowries.  

Obj. 2: On the passage: *be like men who are waiting for their master to come home from the marriage feast* (Lk 12:36), a Gloss*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:298. says: *the Lord has gone to a wedding when after the Resurrection the new man has joined the multitude of angels to himself*. Therefore, the multitude of angels is the bride of Christ. And so dowries are due to the angels.  

Obj. 3: Spiritual marriage consists in a spiritual union. But the spiritual union between the angels and God is not less than the spiritual union between blessed humans and God. Therefore, since the dowries we are now discussing are assigned by reason of spiritual marriage, it seems that dowries apply to the angels.  

Obj. 4: Spiritual marriage requires a spiritual bridegroom and a spiritual bride. But in nature the angels are more like Christ, inasmuch as he is the supreme spirit, than men are. Therefore, a spiritual marriage of the angels to Christ is even more possible than that of men to Christ.  

Obj. 5: Greater agreement is required between head and members than between bridegroom and bride. But the conformity between Christ and the angels is sufficient for Christ to be called the head of the angels. Therefore, for the same reason this comformity is sufficient for him to be called the Bridegroom with respect to them.  

On the contrary (1), in his commentary *On the Song of Songs*,Origen, *On the Song of Songs*, hom. 1, PG 13:38. Origen distinguishes four persons: the bridegroom and bride, the young women, and the companions of the bridegroom. And he says that the angels are the companions of the bridegroom. Since, then, dowries are only due to the bride, it seems that dowries do not befit the angels.  

Furthermore (2), Christ espoused the Church to himself by his Incarnation and Passion. Hence this is prefigured when it is said: *you are a bridegroom of blood to me* (Ex 4:25). But by his Passion and Incarnation Christ was not joined to the angels in a way other than he had been previously. Therefore, the angels do not belong to the Church insofar as the Church is called bride. Therefore, dowries do not befit the angels.  

I answer that, there is no doubt that the things belonging to the soul’s dowries befit angels as well as men. But they do not befit them as well as men under the notion of dowry, due to the fact that the notion of bride does not apply as properly to angels as it does to human beings. For a conformity of nature is required between bridegroom and bride such that they be of the same species. Now human beings have this commonality with Christ, inasmuch as he assumed a human nature, through which he became like all men in the nature of the human species. However, he is not like angels according to unity of species, either in his divine nature or in his human nature. And thus the notion of dowry does not befit angels as properly as it does men. Nevertheless, with things that are said metaphorically, since a likeness in every respect is not required, one cannot conclude from some dissimilarity that one thing is not predicated of another thing metaphorically. And the argument given cannot prove that dowries do not befit the angels simply speaking, but only that dowries do not befit them as properly as they do men, due to the dissimilarity mentioned.  

Reply Obj. 1: Even though angels belong to the unity of the Church, they are not members of the Church insofar as the Church is called bride by conformity of nature. And so it does not befit them properly to have dowries.  

Reply Obj. 2: This espousal is taken in a broad sense for union, which does not possess a conformity of nature in species. And in this way nothing prevents us from positing dowries in the angels, if we take ‘dowries’ in a broad sense.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though in spiritual marriage there is only a spiritual union, those who are joined together in the full account of marriage must have a common natural species. And for this reason espousal does not properly pertain to the angels.  

Reply Obj. 4: The conformity by which the angels are like Christ as God is not such as suffices for the full account of marriage, since it is not a conformity through sharing in species, but rather an infinite distance still remains.  

Reply Obj. 5: Nor is Christ properly called the head of the angels according to that account of ‘head’ requiring conformity of nature. Yet one should know that, even granted that the head and the other members are parts of an individual of a single species, still if each part is considered by itself, it does not belong to the same species as another part. For a hand is a different species of part than a head. Hence, speaking of the members in themselves, no other agreement than one of proportion is required among them, such that one receives from another and one is subservient to another. And then the agreement between God and the angels is more sufficient for the account of head than for the account of bridegroom.  

Article 5  

***On the number of the dowries***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fifth question, we proceed thus.Parallels: *ST* I, q. 12, a. 7, ad 1; *In III Sent*. d. 1, q. 1, a. 1; *Super Heb*. 1, lec. 6. It seems unfitting to hold that there are three dowries of the soul: vision, love, and enjoyment. For the soul is joined to God according to the mind, in which the image of the Trinity exists on the basis of memory, intelligence, and will. But love pertains to the will and vision to the intelligence. Therefore, something should be posited that corresponds to the memory, since enjoyment pertains not to the memory but rather to the will.  

Obj. 2: The dowries of beatitude are said to correspond to the virtues of the wayfaring state by which we are joined to God, which are faith, hope, and charity, whose object is God himself. But love corresponds to charity and vision to faith. Therefore, something would have to be posited that would pertain to hope, since enjoyment pertains instead to charity.  

Obj. 3: We are only said to enjoy God through love and vision. For we are said to enjoy the things we love for their own sake, as is clear from Augustine in *On Christian Doctrine* 1.Augustine, *On Christian Doctrine*, Bk. 1, ch. 4. Therefore, enjoyment should not be posited as a different dowry from love.  

Obj. 4: Comprehension is required for the perfection of beatitude: *run that you may comprehend* (1 Cor 9:24). Therefore, a fourth dowry would still have to be posited.  

Obj. 5: AnselmNot Anselm, but Eadmer, in his book *De similitudinibus*, ch. 48, PL 159:627. says that the following pertain to the soul’s beatitude: wisdom, friendship, harmony, power, honor, security, and joy. And so it seems that the aforementioned dowries are unfittingly assigned.  

Obj. 6: Augustine says at the end of the *City of God* that: *in that beatitude God will be seen without end, will be loved without tedium, will be praised without weariness*.Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 22, ch. 30. Therefore, praise should be counted with the previously assigned dowries.  

Obj. 7: Boethius includes five things pertaining to beatitude in the *Consolation of Philosophy* 3: *sufficiency*, which riches promise; *joyfulness*, which desire promises; *celebrity*, which fame promises; *security*, which power promises; *veneration*, which dignity promises.Boethius, *Consolation of Philosophy* (CCSL 94), Bk. 3, prose 2. And so it seems that these should be set down as dowries rather than the ones named.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that we should not consider there to be dowries in the body in addition to the dowries of the soul. For the dowries are ordered to spiritual union, in which beatitude consists, as has been said. But the things in the body do not incline to this kind of union. For the activities of the mind, through which the soul is joined to God, are not carried out by physical organs. Therefore, we should not posit any dowries of the body.  

Obj. 2: Dowry and beatitude are due to man insofar as he is in the image of God. But man is not in the image of God according to his body but according to his soul, as is clear from Augustine in *On the Trinity*.Augustine, *On the Trinity* (CCSL 50), Bk. 12, chs. 6–7. Therefore, dowries are not due to the body.  

Obj. 3: The lower powers of the sensitive and vegetative soul are more perfect than the body itself. But no dowries are assigned that correspond to the lower powers of the soul. Therefore, no dowries should be assigned that correspond to the body.  

On the contrary (1), dowries are due to the blessed insofar as they are conformed to Christ. But men are conformed to Christ not only according to the soul but also according to the body. Therefore, dowries are due also to the body.  

Furthermore (2), this is also clear from the definition of ‘dowry’ given above, which runs: *a dowry is an adornment of soul and body*.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that the dowries of the body are unfittingly counted as four: subtlety, agility, clarity, and impassibility. For AnselmEadmer, *De similitudinibus*, ch. 48, PL 159:627. seems to count seven, saying that there will be seven beatitudes of the body: beauty, swiftness, fortitude, freedom, health, pleasure, and longevity.  

Obj. 2: The perfection of a living body involves not only motion but also the senses. But one dowry is included that pertains to motion, namely agility. Therefore, there should be another that would pertain to the senses.  

Obj. 3: The bodies of the saints will be delightful according to all the senses. Therefore, just as clarity, which pertains to sight, is considered a dowry, so also other dowries pertaining to the other senses would have to be included.  

Obj. 4: Just as impassibility befits the glorified body, so also it befits the glorified soul. Therefore, there is no need to include it as a dowry of the body since it is not included as a dowry of the soul.  

Obj. 5: Just as subtlety is a dowry of the body, so also is transparency, as is clear from Gregory in the *Moralia*Gregory the Great, *Moralia in Iob* (CCSL 143A), Bk. 18, par. 48. on the passage: *gold or glass cannot equal it* (Job 28:17). Therefore, transparency should be included among the dowries of the body.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, everyone holds in common that there are three dowries of the soul, but the position is held in different ways. For some say that the three dowries of the soul are vision, love, and enjoyment. Others say that they are vision, comprehension, and enjoyment. Still others say that they are vision, love, and comprehension. Nevertheless, all these ways of assigning them are reduced to the same thing and their number is assigned in the same way. For it has been said that a dowry is something inhering in the soul by which it is ordered to the activity that beatitude consists in. In this activity two things are required: the substance of the activity itself, which is vision, and the perfection of the activity, which is delight. For beatitude must be a perfect activity.  

Now a vision is delightful in two ways. In one way, on the part of the object, inasmuch as what is seen is delightful. In the other way, on the part of the vision, inasmuch as seeing is itself delightful, as we delight in knowing evils, though evils do not delight us. And because this activity in which ultimate beatitude consists should be most perfect, it is required that this vision be delightful in both ways.  

Now for the vision itself to be delightful, it must be made connatural to the one seeing through some habit. But for it to be delightful on the part of the visible object, two things are required: that the visible object be fitting and that it be united with the one seeing. In this way, then, for the delightfulness of vision on the side of the vision itself a habit is required that elicits the vision, and this is one dowry, which all call vision. But on the part of the visible object two things are required: fittingness through affect, and in this regard some include love as a dowry and some enjoyment to the extent that enjoyment pertains to the affections, for what we take utmost delight in we consider most fitting. A union with the visible object is also required, and so some people set this down as comprehension, which is nothing other than to possess God as present and hold him in himself. But, according to others, this is enjoyment, not the enjoyment of hope as in the wayfaring state, but the enjoyment of the very thing at last as it is in heaven.The expressions *fruitio spei* and *fruitio rei* constitute a pun. And so the three dowries correspond to the three theological virtues: vision to faith, hope to comprehension (or enjoyment in one way of taking it), and love to charity—or, according to another way of taking it, enjoyment, for perfect enjoyment of the sort that will be had in heaven includes love and comprehension in itself, and thus some take it for one and others for the other.  

Some, however, attribute these three dowries to the three powers of the soul: *vision* to the rational, *love* to the concupiscible, and *enjoyment* to the irascible, inasmuch as such enjoyment is acquired through a kind of victory. But this is not said properly, since the irascible and concupiscible powers are not in the intellective part but the sensitive part, as was said in Book III, Distinction 26, Question 1, Article 2, while the dowries of the soul are held to be in the mind itself.  

Reply Obj. 1: Memory and intelligence have only a single activity. For either intelligence itself is an activity of memory, or if intelligence is said to be a kind of potency, then memory does not result in activity except by the mediation of intelligence, since it belongs to memory to retain knowledge. For this reason, only one single habit corresponds to memory and intelligence, namely knowledge. And thus a single dowry alone, namely vision, corresponds to both.  

Reply Obj. 2: Enjoyment corresponds to hope inasmuch as it includes comprehension, which takes the place of hope. For what is hoped for is not yet had. And thus in a certain way hope afflicts a person because of the distance from what is loved. And for this reason it does not remain in heaven but rather comprehension takes its place.  

Reply Obj. 3: The enjoyment that includes comprehension is distinguished from vision and love, though in a different way than love is distinguished from vision. For love and vision designate different habits, one of which pertains to the intellect and the other to the affections. But comprehension or the enjoyment that is taken for comprehension, does not imply a different habit from these two but rather implies the removal of the impediments to the mind being able to be united to God face to face. And this occurs through the fact that the very possession of glory frees the soul from every defect, just as it makes it sufficient for knowing without phantasms and for mastering the body, and other such things, that exclude the impediments by which it happens that we now are on pilgrimage far from the Lord.  

The reply to the fourth objection is clear from what has been said.  

Reply Obj. 5: Dowries are properly the immediate principles of the activity that perfect beatitude consists in, through which the soul is united with Christ. However, what AnselmEadmer. enumerates are not of this sort but are, rather, things that accompany or result from beatitude in many different ways, not only in relation to the Bridegroom, to whom, of the things enumerated, wisdom alone pertains. But these things are also in relation to others, whether they be peers, to whom friendship pertains as to the union of affections, and harmony as to consent in things to be done; or whether they be inferiors, to whom power pertains to the extent that the lower are disposed by the higher, and honor in accord with what is shown to the higher by the lower. And they also may be in relation to oneself, to whom security pertains as regards removal of evil and joy as regards attainment of the good.  

Reply Obj. 6: The *praise* that Augustine counts as the third of the things there will be in heaven is not a disposition to beatitude but rather a consequence of beatitude. For from the fact that the soul is united to God, which beatitude consists in, it follows that it breaks forth into praise, which is why praise does not have the character of a dowry.  

Reply Obj. 7: The five things Boethius enumerates are certain conditions of beatitude, not dispositions to the act of beatitude, because beatitude by reason of its perfection possesses alone and uniquely by itself whatever men seek after in various things, as is clear from the Philosopher in the *Ethics* 1 and 10.See Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, esp. chs. 5, 7; Bk. 10, chs. 7–8. And accordingly Boethius shows that these five things exist in true beatitude, since they are what all men seek after in temporal happiness. They pertain either to being free from evil, such as security, or ot to the attainment of the good or of the fitting, as joyfulness, or of the perfect, such as sufficiency; or they are for the manifestation of the good, such as celebrity, inasmuch as the good of one lies within the knowledge of many, and veneration, inasmuch as a sign of this knowledge or good is shown. For veneration consists in showing honor as a testimony of virtue. Hence it is clear that these five elements should not be called dowries but rather certain conditions of beatitude.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that man is a partaker in beatitude to the extent that he exists in the image of God. Now, the image of God consists first and principally in the mind, but through a kind of derivation a representation of the image is found also in man’s body, insofar as the body must be proportioned to the soul, as is also clear from what the Master says in Sentences II, Distinction 16. For this reason, beatitude or glory is first and principally in the mind, but through a kind of overflowing it also spills over into the body so that man’s beatitude can be spoken of with reference to the body, which perfectly follows the command of the soul joined to God. Hence just as the blessed soul’s dispositions to the perfect activity by which it is joined to God are called the soul’s dowries, so also the glorified body’s dispositions by which it is made perfectly subject to the soul are called dowries of the body.  

Reply Obj. 1: The glorified body’s perfection also in a certain way helps the soul more freely and perfectly cling to God, inasmuch as it is not drawn away from clinging to God by any physical impediments, and also inasmuch as the soul possesses being more perfectly when joined to the body than it does when separate from the body. And so its activity is also more perfect, as said above.  

The reply to the second objection is clear from what has been said.  

Reply Obj. 3: As Augustine says in *On the Trinity*,Augustine, *On the Trinity* (CCSL 50), Bk. 12, ch. 1. to the external man pertains not only the body but also the body’s senses. For this reason all the lower powers that are connected with organs are reckoned together with the body and receive dowries also together with it.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that the soul is the form of the body and its mover. Hence since the body’s dowries are ordered to the body’s being perfectly subject to the soul, this will be so that it is subject to it perfectly both as form and as mover. Now three things are caused in matter by form: substantial being, powers and secondary (accidental) forms, and the preservation of both.  

Therefore, inasmuch as the glorified body is perfectly subject to the soul in the substantial being it has from it, there is the dowry of subtlety. For the glorious body is called subtle, as was said above (Distinction 44), from the fact that it is informed perfectly by the soul.  

And inasmuch as it is perfectly subject to it as its form according to the accidental forms that are in the body from the soul, there is the dowry of clarity.  

In turn, inasmuch as the glorious body is perfectly preserved by the soul, there is the dowry of impassibility. For it is called impassible from the fact that by the soul’s power it is preserved immune from all harm.  

But inasmuch as the body is perfectly subject to the soul as its mover, there is the dowry of agility. For the body is called agile insofar as it is responsive and ready for all the acts of the soul.  

Now some correlate the four dowries of the body with the four cardinal virtues, because corporeal goods are the matter of the cardinal virtues. Thus they say that clarity corresponds to prudence on account of knowledge. Next, justice, which is perpetual and immortal, corresponds to impassibility. Next, agility, from which it comes about that nothing can resist the body, corresponds to fortitude, for which reason agility is indicated where it is said: *it is raised in power* (1 Cor 15:43). Finally, subtlety, which attenuates the body, corresponds to temperance. And in this way the four dowries of the body correspond to the four cardinal virtues just as the three dowries of the soul correspond to the three theological virtues, as has been said.  

Reply Obj. 1: The seven things listed by Anselm are contained under these four. For beauty is contained under clarity; swiftness, freedom, and fortitude, under agility; health and longevity under impassibility; and pleasure can be reduced to subtlety, since from the fact that the body is perfectly informed by the soul it is disposed for delight.  

Reply Obj. 2: Agility pertains not only to motion but also to the senses and all other activities of the soul, such that the glorious body is thus called agile insofar as it is perfectly suited to all the activities carried out by the body.  

Reply Obj. 3: According to the Philosopher in *On Generation and Corruption* 2,Cf. *On Generation and Corruption*, Bk. 2, ch. 2, 329b7–15. visible qualities are more noble than other sensible qualities in the fact that lower bodies agree with higher bodies in visibility, and not in other sensible qualities. Hence light or clarity, which is a quality proper to heavenly bodies, is the more noble among other corporeal qualities and a better likness for expressing intelligible clarity. And thus from the overflowing of glory from the soul to the body, clarity is included as a dowry in the glorious body rather than any other sensible quality.  

Reply Obj. 4: The soul is not naturally passible, except incidentally, inasmuch as the body suffers. Or by divine justice it is passible due to fault, which is far from the souls of the blessed on account of the love of God. And so the fact that impassibility is included as a dowry of the body does not necessitate that it be included as a dowry of the soul.  

Reply Obj. 5: The clear and the transparent are reduced to the same genus by the fact that light is the act of the transparent. And thus transparency is not listed as a dowry distinct from clarity. Now of the dowries listed, both of soul and of body, special mention has been made: of enjoyment in Book I, Distinction 1; of love in Book III, in the treatise on charity; of vision above, in this same Distinction; and of the four dowries of the body above in Distinction 44.  

Question 5  

***The crown of distinction***

Next, the “aureole” or crown of special distinction must be inquired about; and here five questions arise:  

first, what an aureole is;  

- second, how it differs from a fruit;
- third, on what basis a crown of special distinction is due;
- fourth, to whom it is due;
- fifth, on the number of the special distinctions.
- Article 1
What an aureole isThe term *aurea*, which refers to the essential reward of heaven, will be translated “golden crown.” The term *aureola*, which is a diminutive of *aurea*, has been translated in previous sections as “special crown of distinction,” but will here be rendered simply as “aureole,” for the sake of brevity.  

Obj. 1: To the first question, we proceed thus. It seems that an aureole is not different reward from the essential reward, which is called a golden crown. For the essential reward is beatitude itself. But beatitude, according to Boethius, is the *state perfected by the gathering together of all goods*.Boethius, *Consolation of Philosophy* (CCSL 94), Bk. 3, prose 2. Therefore, the essential reward includes every good possessed in heaven. And so the aureole is included in the golden crown.  

Obj. 2: More and less do not make for different species. But those who keep the counsels and precepts are rewarded more than those who keep only the precepts. But their reward seems to differ in nothing except that one is greater than another. Since, then, ‘aureole’ refers to a reward that is due to works of perfection, it seems that an aureole does not indicate something distinct from the golden crown.  

Obj. 3: Reward corresponds to merit. But the root of all merit is charity. Since, then, the golden crown corresponds to charity, it seems that in heaven there will not be a reward distinct from the golden crown.  

Obj. 4: Blessed men are taken up into the orders of the angels, as Gregory says.Gregory the Great, *Homilies on the Gospels* (CCSL 141), Bk. 2, homily 34. But among the angels, although some gifts are given to some of them in a surpassing way, nevertheless with them nothing is possessed individually. For all gifts are in all of them, though not equally, since some possess more sublimely than others what they all have, as Gregory also says.Ibid. Therefore in the blessed, too, there will not be any reward that is not common to all. Therefore, an aureole is not a reward distinct from the golden crown.  

Obj. 5: A more excellent reward is owed to more excellent merit. If, then, a golden crown is due to works of precept, but an aureole is due to works of counsel, then an aureole will be more perfect than the golden crown. And then it should not be designated by a diminutive.*Aureola*, the reward for special distinction, is literally a “little crown” as compared with the *aurea*, “golden crown” that represents the reward of attaining heaven. And so it seems that an aureole is not a reward distinct from a golden crown.  

On the contrary (1), on the passage: *he shall make another little golden crown* (Ex 25:25), a Gloss says: *to this crown pertains the new song, which the virgins alone sing together before the Lamb*. From this it seems that an aureole is a kind of crown rendered not to all but to some in particular. A golden crown, however, is rendered to all the blessed. Therefore, an aureole is something other than the golden crown.  

Furthermore (2), a crown is due to a struggle followed by victory: *he is not crowned, unless he competes according to the rules* (2 Tim 2:5). Therefore where the contest has a special character, there should be a special crown. But in some works the contest does have a special character. Therefore, they should have a crown before the others, and this we call an aureole.  

Furthermore (3), the Church Militant descends from the Triumphant, as is clear: *I saw the holy city, the new Jerusalem, coming down out of heaven from God* (Rev 21:2). But in the Church Militant special rewards are rendered to those who perform special works, as a crown to the victors and a prize to the runners. Therefore, it should be likewise in the Church Triumphant.  

I answer that, man’s essential reward, which is his beatitude, consists in the perfect union of the soul with God inasmuch as a person perfectly enjoys him as perfectly seen and loved. This reward is metaphorically called a crown or a golden crown, both from the perspective of merit, which occurs with a kind of struggle, for *the life of man upon earth is a warfare* (Job 7:1), and also from the perspective of the reward, through which man is made in a certain way a partaker in divinity and, consequently, in royal power: *thou hast made them a kingdom and priests to our God* (Rev 5:10). Now a crown is the proper sign of royal dignity and for the same reason the reward that is added to the essential reward has the character of a crown. A crown also signifies a certain perfection by reason of its circular shape and on this basis, too, belongs to the perfection of the blessed.  

But because nothing can be superadded that is not less than that to which it is added, the superadded reward is given the name of aureole.This word, *aureola*, is the diminutive of *aurea*. Now there are two ways that something may be added to the essential reward, which is called the golden crown. In one way, from the condition of the nature that is rewarded, as beyond beatitude, the body’s glory is joined to the soul. Thus even the glory of the body itself is sometimes given the name of aureole. This is why commenting on Exodus 25:25: *thou shalt make another little golden crown* (Ex 25:25), a GlossFrom Bede, *On the Tabernacle* (CCSL 119A), Bk. 1, ch. 6. says that *at the end an aureole is placed on top of it, since in Scripture it is said that a more sublime glory is kept in store for them in the reception of their bodies*. But this is not the aureole that we are now discussing.  

In the other way, from the character of a meritorious work, which has the character of merit from two sources, from which it also has the character of goodness. One is from the root of charity, which is referred to the last end. And so the essential reward, namely attaining the end, is due to it, and this is the golden crown. The other is that by its genus the act has a certain praiseworthiness from due circumstances, from the eliciting habit, and from the proximate end. And in this way an accidental reward is due to it, which is called an aureole. And this is the way in which we mean aureole in the present discussion.  

And so it should be said that ‘aureole’ indicates something that is superadded to a golden crown, i.e., a certain joy in one’s own works having the character of a surpassing victory, which is a different joy from rejoicing at being united with God, which is the joy called the golden crown. Yet some say that the common reward itself, the golden crown, takes the name ‘aureole’ insofar as it is rendered to virgins, martyrs, or teachers of the faith, just as a denarius takes the name ‘debt’ from the fact that it is owed to someone, though in this case the debt and the denarius are altogether the same thing. This is not in such a way that the essential reward must be greater when it is called an aureole but rather that it corresponds to a more excellent act, not according to intensity of merit but according to the manner of meriting, such that although in two people there may be an equal clearness of the vision of God, in one it is called an aureole and not in the other, inasmuch as it corresponds to more excellent merit according to the manner of acting. But this seems to be contrary to the understanding of the GlossIbid. on Exodus 25.Exodus 25 has to do with the furnishings of the tabernacle. For if the golden crown and an aureole were the same, the aureole would not be said to be placed on top of the golden crown. And besides, since reward corresponds to merit, an excellence in reward must correspond to the excellence of merit that stems from the manner of acting. And we call this excellence an aureole. Hence an aureole must differ from the golden crown.  

Reply Obj. 1: Beatitude includes in itself all the goods necessary for man’s perfect life, which consists in man’s perfect activity. But some goods can be superadded, not as necessary for perfect activity in such a way that without them it cannot exist, but because with their addition beatitude has greater brilliance. Hence it pertains to the well-being of beatitude and to something of its comeliness, just as political happiness is adorned by nobility, physical beauty, and other such things, though it can exist without them, as is clear in the *Ethics* 1.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 1, ch. 8, 1099b1–5. And this is how an aureole is related to the beatitude of heaven.  

Reply Obj. 2: A person who keeps the counsels and the precepts always merits more than someone who only keeps the precepts, insofar as the character of merit is considered in works on the basis of the genus of the works, though not always as the character of merit is weighed on the basis of the root of charity. For it sometimes happens that one person keeps only the precepts with a greater charity than another keeps both the precepts and the counsels. But the converse is more often the case, since the proof of love is doing the work, as Gregory says.Gregory the Great, *Homilies on the Gospels* (CCSL 141), Bk. 2, homily 30, par. 1. It is not, therefore, the essential reward itself as more intense that is called an aureole, but rather what is superadded to the essential reward indifferently, whether the one possessing the aureole has a greater essential reward or a lesser or equal one to the essential reward of someone without an aureole.  

Reply Obj. 3: Charity is the principle of meriting, but our act is like the instrument by which we merit. Now to obtain the effect there is required not only due disposition in the one moving first but also right disposition in the instrument. And thus in the effect something follows on the part of the first principle, which is the principal effect, and something on the part of the instrument, which is secondary. This is why in the reward too there is something on the part of charity, namely the golden crown, and something from the genus of the activity, such as an aureole.  

Reply Obj. 4: All the angels merited their beatitude from the same genus of act, namely in the fact that they were turned toward God. And thus none of them has an individual reward that is not found in the others in some way. Men, on the other hand, merit beatitude by different kinds of acts. And thus the case is not similar. Still, what one person seems to have in a special way among men is possessed by all in a certain way as common, namely inasmuch as through perfect charity each considers the good of another as his own. This joy, however, by which one rejoices with another cannot be called an aureole, since it is not given as his reward for victory but rather has to do with someone else’s victory, whereas a crown is given to the victors themselves, not to those who rejoice with them in their victory.  

Reply Obj. 5: The excellence of merit arising from charity is greater than the excellence that arises from the genus of the act, just as the end that charity orders toward is more important than what is directed toward the end, which is what our acts are concerned with. Hence, too, the reward corresponding to merit by reason of charity, however little it be, is greater than any reward corresponding to the act by reason of its genus. And thus the aureole is spoken of in the diminutive in relation to the golden crown.  

Article 2  

***How it differs from a fruit***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the second question, we proceed thus. It seems that an aureole does not differ from a fruit. For different rewards are not owed for the same merit. But an aureole and hundredfold fruitSee Matthew 13:23 and 19:29. correspond to the same merit, namely virginity, as is clear in a Gloss on Matthew 13:8. Therefore, an aureole is the same as the fruit.  

Obj. 2: Augustine says in *On Virginity*Augustine, *On Holy Virginity* (CSEL 41), ch. 45. that the hundredfold fruit is due to a martyr and the same to a virgin. Therefore, fruit is a kind of reward common to both virgins and martyrs. But an aureole is also due to the same people. Therefore, an aureole is the same as a fruit.  

Obj. 3: In beatitude we only find two rewards: essential and accidental, which is added above the essential. But the reward superadded to the essential is said to be the aureole, as is clear from the fact that Exodus 25 says that the aureole is placed on top of the golden crown. But a fruit is not the essential reward, since then it would be due to all the blessed. Therefore, it is the same as an aureole.  

On the contrary (1), things that are not subdivided in the same way do not have the same nature. But a fruit and an aureole are not subdivided in the same way, since an aureole is subdivided into the aureole of virgins, martyrs, and teachers, whereas fruit is subdivided into the fruit of spouses, widows, and virgins. Therefore, a fruit and an aureole are not the same.  

Furthermore (2), if fruit and aureole were the same, then to whomever is due a fruit, an aureole would be due. But this is clearly false. For fruit is due to widowhood, but an aureole is not. Therefore, etc.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that a fruit is not due only for the virtue of continence.Parallels: *In IV Sent*. d. 33, q. 3, a. 3, ad 3; d. 49, q. 5, a. 2, qa. 1, ad 2.
