For on the passage: *there is one glory of the sun, and another glory of the moon, and another glory of the stars; for star differs from star in glory* (1 Cor 15:41), a Gloss says that the dignity of those who have hundredfold fruit is compared to the glory of the sun, those who have sixtyfold fruit to the glory of the moon, and those who have thirtyfold fruit to the glory of a star. But this differentiation of glories, according to the Apostle’s intention, pertains to every difference in beatitude. Therefore, the different fruits should not correspond to continence alone.  

Obj. 2: ‘Fruits’ are so named from ‘fruition.’ But fruition pertains to the essential reward, which corresponds to all the virtues. Therefore, etc.  

Obj. 3: Fruit is due to labor: *the fruit of good labors is renowned* (Wis 3:15). But there is greater labor in fortitude than in temperance or in continence. Therefore, the fruit does not correspond to continence alone.  

Obj. 4: It is more difficult not to exceed moderation in food, which is necessary for life, than in venereal pleasures, without which life can be preserved. And so the effort for parsimony is greater than that of continence. Therefore, the fruit corresponds more to parsimony than to continence.  

Obj. 5: Fruit implies refreshment. But refreshment is especially in the end. Since, then, the theological virtues have the end, namely God himself, as their object, it seems that the fruit should correspond to them most of all.  

On the contrary, a Gloss*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:131. on Matthew 15 assigns fruits to virginity, widowhood, and spousal continence, which are the subdivisions of continence.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that the three fruits are unfittingly assigned to the three subdivisions of continence.Parallel: *ST* I-II, q. 70, a. 3, ad 2. For there are twelve fruits of the Spirit: charity, joy, peace, patience, kindness, goodness, long-suffering, gentleness, faithfulness, modesty, continency, chastity (Gal 5:22–23). And thus it seems that we should not posit only three.  

Obj. 2: ‘Fruit’ refers to a specific reward. But a reward that is assigned to virgins, widows, and spouses is not specific, since all those who will be saved fall into one of these three categories because no one lacking continence will be saved and continence is exhaustively divided into these three classes. Therefore, fruits are unfittingly assigned to the three mentioned.  

Obj. 3: Just as widowhood surpasses spousal continence, so also virginity surpasses widowhood. But sixtyfold does not surpass thirtyfold in the same way that one hundredfold surpasses sixtyfold, either by arithmetical proportion, since sixtyfold is thirty greater than thirtyfold while one hundredfold is forty greater than sixtyfold, or by geometrical proportion, since sixtyfold is related to thirtyfold by a double proportion while one hundredfold is related to sixtyfold by a sesquitertian proportion because it contains the whole plus two thirds. Therefore, the fruits are not fittingly suited to the three degrees of continence.  

Obj. 4: The things said in Sacred Scripture have perpetuity: *heaven and earth shall pass away, but my words shall not pass away* (Lk 21:33). But the things made by human institution can be changed daily. Therefore, we should not take the account of what is said in Scripture from things of human institution. And so it seems that the reason Bede assigns concerning these fruits is unfitting, for he says that thirtyfold fruit is due to spouses, since when representing it on an abacus thirty is signified by touching together the thumb and index finger at their highest point so that in a certain way they kiss each other, and so the number thirty signifies the kisses of spouses. The number sixty, however, is signified by touching the index finger to the middle joint of the thumb, and in this way the oppression that widows suffer in the world is signified through the fact of the index finger’s lying on the thumb and pressing it down. When, finally, we reach one hundred by counting, we move from the left side to the right. Hence virginity is designated by the number one hundred, since it has a portion of the dignity of the angels, who are on the right, i.e., in glory, whereas we are on the left due to the imperfection of the present life.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, things that are said metaphorically can be taken in various ways according to the adaptation of different qualities of the thing from which the word is taken.  

Now since among physical things fruit is properly spoken of with reference to things born of the earth according to the different conditions that can be found in physical fruits, ‘fruit’ can be taken in a spiritual sense in different ways. For corporeal fruit is something sweet that gives refreshment as it comes into a man’s use. It is also the last thing at which nature’s activity arrives and what agriculture looks forward to in the scattering of seed or in whatever other ways. Thus sometimes fruit is taken in a spiritual sense as meaning what refreshes like the last end. And in this signification we are said to have the fruition of God perfectly in heaven but imperfectly on the way. And it is from this meaning that the ‘fruition’ that is a dowry is taken. But we are not speaking this way of the fruits at present. Sometimes, however, fruit is taken in a spiritual sense only as meaning what refreshes, even though it is not the last end. And in this way the virtues are called fruits, inasmuch as they refresh the mind with sincere sweetness, as AmbroseAmbrose, *On Paradise*, ch. 13 (PL 14:325); *On the Holy Spirit*, Bk. 1, ch. 12, PL 16:764. says. And this is how fruit is taken in Galatians 5:22–23: *the fruit of the Spirit is love, joy, peace*. But this is not the meaning we are now after, for this was dealt with in Book III, Distinctions 23–27.  

Spiritual fruit can be understood in another way after a likeness to corporeal fruit, inasmuch as corporeal fruit is a kind of benefit that is looked forward to from the work of agriculture in such a way that fruit is said to be the reward that a man obtains from the work of laboring in this life. And in this way every reward possessed in the future from our labors is called a fruit. This is the sense in which fruit is taken in Romans 6:22: *you have your fruit unto sanctification, and the end life everlasting*. This is also not the sense in which we are now treating of fruit. Rather, we are now treating of fruit inasmuch as it arises from a seed, because this is how the Lord speaks of fruit in Matthew 13:23, where he divides fruit into thirty, sixty, and a hundredfold.  

Now fruit can come out of seed insofar as the seminal power is efficacious for the conversion of the earth’s moisture into its own nature. And the more efficacious this power is and the more prepared the earth is, the richer the fruit that follows. Now the spiritual seed sown in us is the word of God. Hence the more a person is converted to spirituality, withdrawing from the flesh, the greater is the fruit of the word of God in him or her. In this regard, therefore, fruit differs from a golden crown and from an aureole. For the golden crown consists in the joy one has over God, the aureole in the joy one has over the perfection of works, but the fruit consists in the joy one has over the very disposition of the worker according to the degree of spirituality to which one has progressed from the seed of the word of God.  

Some distinguish between aureole and fruit, saying that an aureole is due to one who competes, according to 2 Timothy 2:5: *he is not crowned, unless he competes according to the rules*; whereas a fruit is due to one who labors, according to what is said in Wisdom 3:15: *the fruit of good labors is renowned*. Others, however, say that a golden crown regards conversion to God but that an aureole and fruit consist in what is directed toward the end, in such a way that fruit more principally regards the will and an aureole more regards the body. But since labor and competition are in the same thing in the same regard, and the reward of the body depends on the reward of the soul, according to this position there would only be a logical difference between fruit, golden crown, and aureole. And this cannot be the case, since there are some to whom fruit is assigned to whom an aureole is not assigned.  

Reply Obj. 1: It is not unfitting for different rewards to correspond to the same merit according to the different things in it. And so a golden crown corresponds to virginity to the extent that it is preserved for the sake of God by the command of charity, and an aureole corresponds to it to the extent that it is a kind of work of perfection having the character of a surpassing victory, and finally fruit corresponds to it insofar as through virginity man passes into a certain spirituality, withdrawing from carnality.  

Reply Obj. 2: Fruit in the proper sense, as we are now speaking of it, indicates not a reward common to martyrdom and virginity but rather one common to the three degrees of continence. Now the Gloss that states that the hundredfold fruit corresponds to martyrs takes fruit in a broad sense, insofar as any remuneration is called a fruit, so that in this way by hundredfold fruit is designated that remuneration which is due to any of the works of perfection.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though an aureole is an accidental reward superadded to the essential reward, not every accidental reward is an aureole but only the reward pertaining to the works of perfection by which a man is most greatly conformed to Christ in his perfect victory. For this reason it is not unfitting that there should be another accidental reward for withdrawal from carnal life, and this is called a fruit.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that as has been said, a fruit is a kind of reward that is due to a person from the fact that he passes from a carnal to a spiritual life. And thus it corresponds especially to that virtue which especially frees someone from subjection to the flesh. Now continence does this, since through sensual pleasures the soul is made especially subject to the flesh to the point that in the carnal act, according to Jerome,In fact, Origen, *On Numbers*, hom. 6, PG 12:610; cf. Jerome, *Epistle 22, To Eustochius*, PL 22:409. the spirit of prophecy neither touches the hearts of prophets nor is it possible to understand anything in the midst of that pleasure, as the Philosopher says in the *Ethics* 7.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 7, ch. 11, 1152b17–18. And thus this fruit corresponds more to continence than to another virtue.  

Reply Obj. 1: This Gloss takes fruit in a broad sense, in the way that any remuneration is given the name ‘fruit.’  

Reply Obj. 2: ‘Fruition’ is not derived from ‘fruit’ according to that likeness by which we are now speaking of fruit, as is clear from what has been said.  

Reply Obj. 3: Fruit, as we are now speaking of it, does not correspond to labor because of fatigue but rather insofar as seeds become fruitful through labor. And so even the crops themselves are called ‘labors’ inasmuch as one labors for their sake or they are acquired by labor. But the comparison to fruit, insofar as it arises from seed, fits better with continence than fortitude because a man is not subject to the flesh through the passions of fortitude as he is through the passions that continence concerns.  

Reply Obj. 4: Even though pleasures in food are more necessary than those in sexual matters, they are not as forceful. And thus the soul is not made so subject to the flesh by them.  

Reply Obj. 5: Fruit is not taken here as when one is said to enjoy by being refreshed in the end but in another way. And thus the argument does not follow.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that through continence, which the fruit corresponds to, as has been said, a person is led into a kind of spirituality, having left carnality behind. And thus in accord with the different modes of spirituality that continence produces, different fruits are distinguished. For there is a necessary spirituality and one that is superabundant. Necessary spirituality consists in the fact that uprightness of spirit is not perverted by the pleasures of the flesh. This happens when someone makes use of things delightful to the flesh according to the right order of reason. And this is the spirituality of spouses. Superabundant spirituality, however, is when a person altogether withdraws himself from such pleasures of the flesh, which suffocate the spirit.  

But this occurs in two ways: either with respect to each and every time, past, present, and future, and this is the spirituality of virgins; or with reference to a given time, and this is the spirituality of widows. And so to those preserving conjugal chastity thirtyfold fruit is given, to those preserving the chastity of widowhood, sixtyfold fruit, and to those preserving virginal chastity, hundredfold fruit, for the reason that Bede assigns, although another reason from the nature of numbers can also be assigned. For the number thirty arises from taking three times ten. Three is the “number of every thing,” as it says in *On the Heavens* 1,Aristotle, *On the Heavens*, Bk. 1, ch. 1, 268a5–15. and possesses in itself a kind of perfection common to all things, namely, that of the beginning, the middle, and the end. For this reason the number thirty is fittingly assigned to spouses, since in them, besides observing the Decalogue, which is signified by the number ten, there is only the common perfection without which there can be no salvation. The number six, however, from which the number sixty arises by taking it ten times, possesses a perfection from its parts, since it consists of all its parts added together.E.g., 6 is a perfect number in the sense that the sum of its factors other than itself (1, 2, and 3) equals 6. And so it fittingly corresponds to widowhood, in which is found perfect withdrawal from pleasures of the flesh as regards all circumstances, which are like the parts of a virtuous act. For widowhood enjoys the pleasures of the flesh with no person and in no place, and so on for all other circumstances, which was not the case in conjugal continence. But the number one hundred fittingly corresponds to virginity because ten, from which one hundred arises by multiplying it by itself, is the limit of numbers, and virginity likewise holds the limit of spirituality, since nothing of spirituality can be further added to it. For one hundred, being a square number, has the perfection of shape. A square shape is perfect according as it possesses equality in each part, having all equal sides. This is why it belongs to virginity, in which equal incorruption is found for all time.  

Reply Obj. 1: In that passage fruits are not taken in the way in which we are here speaking of fruits, as is clear from what was said.  

Reply Obj. 2: Nothing compels us to conclude that fruit is a reward that does not apply to all those who will be saved. For not only is the essential reward common to them all but also some accidental rewards, such as joy at those works without which there is no salvation. However, it may be said that these fruits do not apply to all who will be saved, as is clear in the case of those who lived incontinently and repent at the end. For to them no fruit is due, but only the essential reward.  

Reply Obj. 3: The difference between the fruits is taken more on the basis of the species or shapes of the numbers than their quantities, though a reason can also be assigned as to excess of quantity. For a married man abstains only from one who is not his own wife, whereas a widow abstains both from her own husband and anyone who is not her husband. And in this way we find there a kind of pattern of doubling, as sixty is double thirty. One hundred, however, adds forty on top of sixty, and forty arises from multiplying four by ten. Now the number four is the first solid and cubic number. And so an addition like this is appropriate for virginity, which adds perpetual incorruption above the perfection of widowhood.  

Reply Obj. 4: Even though the representation of numbers is of human institution, it is founded in a way on the very nature of things, inasmuch as according to the ordering of the fingers, joints, and points of contact, numbers are designated step by step.  

Article 3  

***The reasons why an aureole is due***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the third question, we proceed thus. It seems that an aureole is not due by reason of virginity. For where there is greater difficulty in a work, a greater reward is due. But widows suffer greater difficulty in abstaining from pleasures of the flesh than do virgins. For JeromeJerome, *Epistle 123, To Ageruchia*, PL 22:1047. says that the more difficult it is for someone to abstain from the lures of sensual pleasure the greater is the reward. And he is speaking for the encouragement of widows. The Philosopher, too, in *On Animals*,Aristotle, *History of Animals*, Bk. 7, ch. 1, 581b20. says that young women who have undergone corruption”Corruption” describes the loss of physical virginal integrity, but without the morally negative connotations it has in English. desire sexual intercourse more on account of recalling its pleasure. Therefore, an aureole, which is the greatest reward, is more due to widows than to virgins.  

Obj. 2: If an aureole is due to virginity, then where the most perfect virginity is, we would most of all find an aureole. But the most perfect virginity is in the Blessed Virgin. Hence, she is given the title ‘Virgin of Virgins.’ And yet she is not due an aureole since she underwent no struggle in remaining continent, since she was not infected by the corruption of the *fomes*. Therefore an aureole is not due to virginity.  

Obj. 3: A surpassing reward is not due to what is not praiseworthy at every time. But it would not have been praiseworthy to preserve virginity in the state of innocence, since at that time there was the precept for man: *be fruitful and multiply, and fill the earth* (Gen 1:28). Nor would it have been praiseworthy in the time of the law, when barren women were accursed. Therefore, an aureole is not due to virginity.  

Obj. 4: The same reward is not due to virginity preserved and virginity lost. But an aureole is sometimes due to virginity lost, as in the case of a woman who is unwillingly forced into prostitution by a tyrant because she professes Christ.Such cases are frequent in the lives of the saints, as narrated, e.g., in the ancient *Acts*, the *Martyrology*, and the *Golden Legend*. Therefore, an aureole is not due to virginity.  

Obj. 5: A surpassing reward is not due to what is in us by nature. But virginity is innate to every man, both good and wicked. Therefore, an aureole is not due to virginity.  

Obj. 6: Just as widowhood is related to sixtyfold fruit, so also virginity is related to hundredfold fruit and an aureole. But it is not every widow who is due sixtyfold fruit, but rather only one who vows widowhood, as some say. Therefore, it seems that an aureole is not due to every state of virginity but only to virginity that is preserved out of a vow.  

Obj. 7: A reward does not correspond to necessity, since all merit consists in the will. But some are virgins of necessity, such as those who are naturally impotent, or eunuchs. Therefore, virginity is not always due an aureole.  

On the contrary (1), Scripture says: *thou shalt make another little golden crown* (Ex 25:25). A Gloss adds: *to the crown pertains the new song that the virgins sing together before the Lamb, namely, those who follow the Lamb wheresoever he goes*. Therefore, the reward that is due to virginity is called an aureole.  

Furthermore (2), Isaiah 56:4 says: *thus says the Lord to the eunuchs*, and it goes on: *I will give . .* . *a name better than sons and daughters*. A Gloss adds: *it is a sign of their proper surpassing glory*. But by ‘eunuchs’ *who have made themselves eunuchs for the sake of the kingdom of heaven* (Matt 19:11), virgins are designated. Therefore, virginity is due a surpassing reward, and this is called an aureole.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that an aureole is not due to martyrs.Parallels: below, a. 5, qa. 1; *De veritate*, q. 26, a. 6, ad 3. For an aureole is a reward that is rendered to works of supererogation. Hence commenting on Exodus 25:25: *another little golden crown*, BedeBede, *On the Tabernacle* (CCSL 119A), Bk. 1, ch. 6. says: *this can rightly be understood of the reward of those who transcend the general commandments by free choice of a more perfect life*. But to die for the confession of the faith is sometimes a matter of necessity, not of supererogation, as is clear from what is said: *man believes with his heart and so is justified, and he confesses with his lips and so is saved* (Rom 10:10). Therefore, martyrdom is not always due an aureole.  

Obj. 2: According to Gregory,In fact, Gratian’s *Decretals*, Part 2, cause 23, qu. 6, appen. to can. 4, Iam vero (RF 1:949), after a canon attributed to Gregory the Great. services become all the more pleasing the more free they are. But martyrdom has a minimum of freedom, since it is a punishment inflicted violently by another. Therefore, an aureole, which corresponds to surpassing merit, is not due to martyrdom.  

Obj. 3: Martyrdom consists not only in externally suffering death but also in the interior will. Hence BernardBernard of Clairvaux, *Sermon on the Feast of Stephen, John, and the Holy Innocents* (*Bernardi opera*, ed. Leclercq, 4:270). distinguishes three kinds of martyrdom: that of will, not death, as in the case of John; that of will and death, as in the case of Stephen; and that of death but not will, as in the case of the Holy Innocents. If, then, an aureole were due to martyrdom, it would be more due to martyrdom of the will than to external martyrdom, since merit proceeds from the will. But this is not what is held. Therefore, an aureole is not due to martyrdom.  

Obj. 4: Affliction of the body is less than affliction of the mind, which occurs through internal pains and internal sufferings of the soul. But internal affliction is also a kind of martyrdom. Hence Jerome says in a *Sermon on the Assumption*: *I am right in saying that the Mother of God was a virgin and martyr, even though she finished out her life in peace. Hence ‘a sword will pierce your own soul,’*Jerome, *Epistle 9, to Paula and Eustochium*, PL 30:142. namely, a sword of pain at the death of her Son. Since, then, an aureole does not correspond to internal pain, neither should it correspond to external pain.  

Obj. 5: Penitence itself is a kind of martyrdom. Hence Gregory says: *though the occasion offered by persecution may be lacking, our peacetime has a martyrdom of its own, for even if we do not subject fleshly necks to the iron, we do indeed slay fleshly desires in our mind by the sword of the spirit*.Gregory the Great, *Homilies on the Gospels* (CCSL 141), Bk. 1, homily 3. But an aureole is not owed to a punishment that consists in external works. Therefore, neither is it due to every external martyrdom.  

Obj. 6: An illicit work should not be given an aureole. But it is illicit to hand oneself over for execution,*Manus inicere* and the corresponding *manus iniectio* is a technical term in Roman law, meaning “to lay hands” on someone so as to arrest him, especially for future execution. Throughout, Aquinas’s reflexive constructions involving *manus inicere* have been translated “to hand oneself over for execution.” as is clear from Augustine in the *City of God*.Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 1, chs. 17, 20, and 26. Yet the martyrdoms of some who laid hands on themselves when fleeing the madness of tyrants are celebrated in the Church, as is clear in church history in the case of certain women in Alexandria.See Eusebius, *History of the Church*, Bk. 8, ch. 12, PG 20:769; John Chrysostom, *Homily on the Holy Martyrs Bernice and Prosdoke*, PG 50:639; Ambrose, *On Virginity*, Bk. 3, ch. 7, PL 16:241. Therefore, martyrdom is not always due an aureole.  

Obj. 7: It has happened at times that someone is wounded for the faith and even survives for a time. It is obvious that such a person is a martyr and yet, so it seems, an aureole is not due to him because his struggle did not last to the point of death. Therefore, martyrdom is not always due an aureole.  

Obj. 8: Some are more afflicted in the loss of temporal things than even in the affliction of their own body, as is clear from the fact that they bear many afflictions in order to make a profit. If, then, they are stripped of their temporal goods for the sake of Christ, it seems that they are martyrs, and yet it does not seem that an aureole is due to them. Therefore, the same follows as before.  

Obj. 9: A martyr seems to be one who is killed for the faith. Hence Isidore says: *they are called ‘martyrs’ in Greek and ‘witnesses’ in Latin because they bear or bore sufferings for the sake of witness to Christ and fought for the truth even unto death*.Isidore, *Etymologies* (ed. Lindsay), Bk. 7, ch. 11. But there are some other virtues more excellent than faith, such as justice, charity, and so forth, which cannot exist without grace. Yet to these an aureole is not due. Therefore, it seems that neither is an aureole due to martyrdom.  

Obj. 10: Just as the truth of the faith is from God, so also is any truth, as Ambrose says,In fact, Ambrosiaster, *On I Corinthians*, on 12:3, PL 17:258. since all that is true, no matter who says it, is from the Holy Spirit. If, then, an aureole is due to one who undergoes death for the truth of the faith, for the same reason it should be due to those who undergo death for any other truth, which does not seem to be the case.  

Obj. 11: The common good is more important than a particular good. But if someone dies for the preservation of the republic in a just war he is not due an aureole. So, too, if he is killed for the preservation of his own faith, he is not due an aureole. And so an aureole is not due to martyrdom.  

Obj. 12: All merit proceeds from free will. But the Church celebrates the martyrdoms of some who did not possess the use of free will. Therefore, they did not merit an aureole. And so an aureole is not due to all martyrs.  

On the contrary (1), Augustine says in *On Holy Virginity*: *no one, as far as I am aware, has dared to put virginity before martyrdom*.Augustine, *On Holy Virginity* (CSEL 41), ch. 46. But an aureole is due to virginity. Therefore, one is also due to martyrdom.  

Furthermore (2), a crown is due to one who competes. But in martyrdom the struggle has special difficulty. Therefore, it is due a special aureole.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that an aureole is not due to teachers.Parallel: below, a. 5, qa. 1. For every reward that will be had in the future corresponds to an act of virtue. But to preach or to teach is not an act of any virtue. Therefore, an aureole is not due to teaching or preaching.  

Obj. 2: Teaching and preaching come from learning and study. But things that are rewarded in the future are not acquired by human study, since we do not merit by what we have naturally acquired. Therefore, for teaching and preaching no one merits an aureole in the future.  

Obj. 3: Exaltation in the future corresponds to humiliation in the present because *he who humbles himself will be exalted* (Lk 14:11). But there is no humiliation in teaching and preaching; in fact there is rather an occasion for pride. Hence a Gloss on Matthew 4 says that the devil has deceived many who are puffed up with the honor of teaching authority. Therefore, it seems that an aureole is not due to preaching and teaching.  

On the contrary (1), commenting on Ephesians 1:19: *that you may know . . . the immeasurable greatness of his power*, a Gloss says: *the holy teachers will have a sort of increase beyond what others will commonly have*.*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 114:590. Therefore, etc.  

Furthermore (2), commenting on Song of Songs 8:12: *my vineyard, my very own, is for myself*, a Gloss says: *he shows something of the unique reward that he prepares for his teachers*.*Glossa ordinaria*, PL 113:1166. Therefore, teachers will have a unique reward. And this we call an aureole.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, where there is a preeminent kind of victory a special crown is due. Hence since by virginity one gains a kind of unique victory in relation to the flesh, against which we continuously wage war, as is clear: *the desires of the Spirit are against the flesh* (Gal 5:17), a special crown is due to virginity, which is given the name ‘aureole.’ Everyone commonly holds this, but not everyone agrees on which virginity should have an aureole.  

For some say that an aureole is due to an act. Thus the woman who actually preserves virginity will possess an aureole, if she belongs to the number of those who will be saved. But this does not seem to be fitting, since according to this position women who have the intention of marrying and yet die before they marry will have an aureole.  

And so others say that an aureole is due to the state, not the act, so that only those virgins merit an aureole who have placed themselves in a state of preserving perpetual virginity by a vow. But this, too, does not seem fitting, since by an equal will one person can preserve virginity without vowing it as another does by vowing it.  

And thus we may say rather that all merit is due to an act of virtue under the command of charity. Now virginity pertains to the genus of virtue insofar as perpetual incorruption of mind and body falls under choice, as is clear from what has been said (Distinction 33). And thus an aureole is properly due only to those virgins who had the resolution of preserving virginity perpetually, whether they confirmed this resolution with a vow or not. And I say this to the extent that an aureole is taken in its proper sense as a reward rendered for a certain kind of merit, even if this resolution should at one time be broken, without losing the integrity of the flesh, as long as it is found at the end of life. For virginity of the mind can be recovered, although virginity of the flesh cannot be, as said above in Distinction 33. But if we take ‘aureole’ in a broad sense as meaning whatever joy they will have in heaven above the essential joy, then an aureole will also correspond to those incorrupt in the flesh, even if they did not have the resolution of preserving virginity perpetually. For there is no doubt that they will rejoice at the incorruption of the body just as the innocent rejoice at the fact that they were immune from sin, even if they did not have the opportunity to sin, as is clear in the case of baptized children. This is not, however, the proper sense of ‘aureole,’ but is taking it in a much broader sense.  

Reply Obj. 1: Other things being equal, in one respect virgins have a greater struggle in remaining continent, and in another respect widows do. For in virgins the desire for experience also inflames concupiscence, a desire that proceeds from a sort of curiosity by which it happens that a person is more eager to see what he or she has never seen before. And their concupiscence is sometimes increased, too, by thinking that there is greater pleasure than there truly is and by not considering the disadvantages that are connected with this kind of pleasure. And in this regard widows have less struggle, though they have a greater struggle on account of the memory of past pleasure. And in different people, one of these will predominate over the other, according to the different dispositions that people have. For some are moved more in the former respect and others in the latter. But whatever be the amount of the struggle, this much is certain, that the victory of virgins is more perfect than that of widows. For the most perfect kind of victory, and the most beautiful, is never to have surrendered to the enemy. And a crown is due not to the struggle but to victory in the struggle.  

Reply Obj. 2: Concerning this there are two opinions. Some say that the Blessed Virgin does not have an aureole as a reward for virginity, if an aureole is properly taken as regarding the struggle, though she does have something greater than an aureole on account of her most perfect resolution to preserve virginity. Others, however, say that she does have an aureole, even an aureole under its proper notion, and a most excellent one at that. For even though she did not *feel* the struggle, she did indeed *have* a struggle with the flesh, but from the strength of virtue held the flesh in check to the point that this sort of struggle could not be felt by her. But this does not seem fittingly said. For since the Blessed Virgin is believed to have been altogether immune from the inclination of the *fomes* on account of her perfect sanctification, it is not pious to hold that there was any struggle from the flesh in her, because such struggle is only from the inclination of the *fomes*. Nor can temptation from the flesh be without sin, as is clear from a Gloss on the passage: *a thorn was given me in the flesh* (2 Cor 12:7). So we should say that she does properly have an aureole, so that she in this regard she may be like the other members of the Church in whom virginity is found. And even though she had no struggle through temptation from the flesh, she did have a struggle through temptation from the enemy, who had no reverence even for Christ himself, as is clear in Matthew 4.  

Reply Obj. 3: Virginity is only due an aureole inasmuch as it adds a certain excellence above other grades of continence. But if Adam had not sinned, virginity would have added no perfection above spousal continence. For in that case there would have been honorable marriages and an immaculate marriage bed with none of the filth of concupiscence. Hence virginity would not have then been preserved and no aureole would be due to it. But after the change in the condition of human nature, virginity possesses a special comeliness and thus a special reward is rendered it. In the time of the law of Moses, too, when the worship of God was to be propagated also through the carnal act, it was not altogether praiseworthy to abstain from the commingling of the flesh. Hence a special reward would not be rendered to such a resolution unless it proceeded from a divine inspiration, as is believed of Jeremiah and Elijah, of whom we read nothing about being married.  

Reply Obj. 4: If a woman has been overcome by violence, she does not for this reason lose the aureole, as long as she keeps the resolution of perpetually preserving virginity and in no way consents to the act. Nor does she lose her virginity through such an act, as was said above in Distinction 33. And this I say, whether she is violently corrupted because of her faith, or any other cause. But if she undergoes this for the faith, it will be meritorious for her and will pertain to a kind of martyrdom. This is why St. Lucy said:See *Breviarium S. O. P*., 13 December, At Matins, 6th reading (I:947); cf. Mombritius, *Sanctuarium*, Passio S. Luciae (II:108.41). *if you cause me to be violated unwillingly, my chastity will receive a double crown*, not that she has two aureoles of virginity but rather because there will be a double reward, one for the virginity she kept and the other for the injury she suffered. I say, too, that if such a woman who is overcome by violence conceives, she does not lose the merit of virginity by this fact; however, she will not be made equal to the mother of Christ, in whom there was integrity of the flesh with the integrity of mind.  

Reply Obj. 5: Virginity is innate in us by nature as regards what is material in virginity. But the resolution of preserving perpetual incorruption, from which virginity has merit, is not innate but rather comes from the gift of grace.  

Reply Obj. 6: Sixtyfold fruit is not due to every widow but only to one who keeps the resolution of preserving widowhood, even if she does not make a vow, as was also said of virginity.  

Reply Obj. 7: If the impotent and eunuchs have the intention of preserving perpetual incorruption, even if the capability of coition were present, they should be called virgins and merit an aureole. For they make a virtue of necessity. If, on the other hand, they have the intention of marrying if they could, they do not merit an aureole. Hence Augustine says in *On Holy Virginity*: *for those for whom the male member itself is weakened so that they are not capable of generation, such as eunuchs, when they become Christians and keep the commandments of God though with the resolution that they would have spouses if they could, it is enough for them to be counted equal to the members of the faithful who are married*.Augustine, *On Holy Virginity* (CSEL 41), ch. 24.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that just as there is a kind of struggle in the spirit against the lower desires, so also there is in a human being a kind of struggle against sufferings inflicted externally. This is why just as there is a special crown, which is called an aureole, due to the most perfect victory in which we triumph over the desires of the flesh, namely to virginity, so also an aureole is due to the most perfect victory one can have over an external assault. But the most perfect victory in the case of external sufferings can be considered in two ways. First, from the magnitude of the suffering. Now among all sufferings inflicted externally, death holds the chief place, just as in internal passions the desires for sensual pleasures hold the chief place. And thus when someone gains victory in death and the things ordered to death, he conquers most perfectly. Second, the perfection of victory can be considered from the cause of the struggle, namely, when one is attacked for the most noble cause, which is, of course, Christ himself. And these are the two things which we find in martyrdom, which is death undergone for the sake of Christ. For it is not the punishment but the cause that makes a martyr. And thus martyrdom is due an aureole just as virginity is.  

Reply Obj. 1: To endure death for the sake of Christ, in itself, is a work of supererogation. For not everyone is bound to profess his faith before a persecutor. But it is necessary for salvation in a particular case, namely, when someone is apprehended by a persecutor and questioned about his faith, which he is then bound to confess. Nor does it follow that he does not merit an aureole. For the aureole is not due to the work of supererogation as supererogatatory but rather inasmuch as it has a kind of perfection. For this reason, when a perfection like this remains, even if it is not supererogatory, the person merits an aureole.  

Reply Obj. 2: Martyrdom is not due a reward insofar as it is inflicted from without but rather insofar as it is borne voluntarily. For we only merit through what is in us. And the more difficult what someone bears voluntarily is and the more naturally repugnant to the will, the more firmly fixed in Christ the will is shown to be that bears it for Christ’s sake. And thus a more excellent reward is due to it.  

Reply Obj. 3: There are some acts that contain a certain extreme pleasure or difficulty in the act itself. And in such cases the act always adds to the character of merit or demerit to the extent that in the act the will must be changed by the vehemence of the act from the state in which it previously was. And thus, other things being equal, one who actually carries out an act of lust sins more than one who only consents to the act because the will is increased in the act itself. Likewise, since the act of martyrdom contains the greatest difficulty, the will for martyrdom does not attain to that merit which is due to the act of the martyrs by reason of difficulty, though it can also arrive at a further reward, considering the root of merit. For one can will to undergo martyrdom out of a greater charity than another who actually undergoes it. For this reason a martyr in will can merit by his will an essential reward equal to or greater than what is due a martyr. But an aureole is due to the difficulty that is the struggle of martyrdom itself. Hence an aureole is not due to martyrs in will alone.  

Reply Obj. 4: Just as the pleasures of touch, which temperance concerns, hold a special place among all internal and external delights, so also pains of touch take precedence over all other pains. And thus an aureole is due to the difficulty that occurs in enduring pains of touch, for example, those consisting in blows and so forth, rather than to the difficulty of bearing internal pains. For these latter, however, one is not properly called a martyr, except by a kind of likeness. And this is the manner in which Jerome is speaking.  

Reply Obj. 5: The affliction of penitence is not, properly speaking, martyrdom because it does not consist in things ordered to the infliction of death, since it is ordered only to taming the flesh, and if it exceeds this measure it will be a blameworthy affliction. It is called martyrdom, then, because of a similarity in the affliction. This affliction exceeds the affliction of martyrdom in how long it lasts while martyrdom exceeds it in intensity.  

Reply Obj. 6: According to Augustine in the *City of God* 1,Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 1, chs. 17, 20, 26. it is not licit for anyone to lay hands on himself for any cause whatsoever, unless, perhaps, this occurs by divine inspiration in order to give an example of fortitude so that death may not be feared. Now those whose cases the objection brings forward are believed to have brought death on themselves by divine inspiration, and for this reason the Church celebrates their martyrdoms.  

Reply Obj. 7: If someone receives a fatal wound for the faith and lives on for awhile, there is no doubt that he merits an aureole, as is clear in the case of the blessed CeciliaSee *Breviarium S. O. P*., November 22, At Matins, 6th reading (II:1315); cf. Mombritius, *Sanctuarium*, Passio S. Caecilia (I:341.12). who lived on for three days, and in the case of the many martyrs who have died in prison. But even if he receives a wound that is not fatal and yet incurs death from it, he is believed fully to merit an aureole, even though some say that he does not merit an aureole if he incurs death from his own lack of care or negligence. For this negligence would not have led to his death if the wound he received for the faith had not been presupposed. And thus the wound received for the sake of Christ is for him the full occasion of his death. Hence he does not for this reason seem to lose the aureole, unless there is such negligence as would mean he is guilty of mortal sin, which in that case takes away both his aureole and his golden crown. If, however, from the fatal wound received he does not die because of some chance contingency, or even if he has received wounds that are not fatal and then dies while still imprisoned, he still merits an aureole. Hence the martyrdoms of some saints who died in prison from blows received long before are celebrated in the Church, as in the case of Pope Marcellus.See *Acta Sanctorum*, Acta altera S. Marcelli, January 16, Bk. 1, ch. 4 (BL 2:376a). Therefore no matter how affliction inflicted for Christ’s sake is drawn out unto death (whether death follows from it or not), a person does become a martyr by it and merits an aureole. If, however, it is not drawn out unto death, then one should not for this reason be called a martyr, as is clear in the case of Sylvester, whom the Church does not celebrate with solemnity as she does a martyr, because he finished out his life in peace, even though he bore certain sufferings before then.  

Reply Obj. 8: Just as temperance does not concern delights in money or honors or other such things, but is only in the pleasures of touch as though particular to it, so also fortitude concerns dangers of death as especially in its purview, as it says in the *Ethics* 3.Aristotle, *Nicomachean Ethics*, Bk. 3, ch. 6, 1115a24. And thus it is only to an injury inflicted on one’s own body from which death naturally follows that an aureole is due. Thus, whether one loses temporal goods or fame or anything of this sort for the sake of Christ, he does not for this reason properly become a martyr and does not merit an aureole. Nor can someone love external things more than his own body ordinately. But inordinate love does not assist in meriting an aureole. Nor, too, can the pain of losing things be equated with the pain of the slaying of the body and other such things.  

Reply Obj. 9: The sufficient cause for martyrdom is not only the confession of faith but also any other virtue, not political but infused, which has Christ as its end. For one becomes a witness to Christ by any act of virtue inasmuch as the works that Christ perfects in us are a testimony to his goodness. This is why certain virgins were killed for the virginity they wished to preserve, as blessed Agnes and some others, and their martyrdoms are celebrated in the Church.There are very many such saints. Among those with which Aquinas would have been familiar from their liturgical cult, we may cite St. Agnes, St. Agatha, and St. Dymphna. Aquinas in fact wore a first-class relic of St. Agnes around his neck, as we learn from his biographers.  

Reply Obj. 10: The truth of the faith has Christ as its end or object. And thus the confession of the faith merits an aureole if a punishment is added, not only on the part of the end but also on the part of the matter. The confession of any other truth, however, is not a sufficient cause for martyrdom by reason of the matter but only by reason of the end, as, for example, if one wished to be killed rather than sin against Christ by telling a lie of any sort.  

Reply Obj. 11: The uncreated good also exceeds every created good. Thus no created end, whether the common good or a private good, can bestow on an act goodness as great as the uncreated end, that is, when an act is performed for the sake of God. And thus when someone undergoes death for the common good not referred to Christ, he does not merit an aureole. But if this is referred to Christ, he will merit an aureole and will be a martyr, as, for example, if someone defends the republic from the assault of enemies who are bent on corrupting faith in Christ and undergoes death in such defense.  

Reply Obj. 12: Some say that in the Holy Innocents slain for Christ the use of reason was quickened by divine power, as also in John the Baptist when he was still in his mother’s womb. And in this regard they were truly martyrs both in will and in act, and they possess an aureole. But others say that they were martyrs in act alone, not in will. Bernard seems to be of this opinion, distinguishing the three kinds of martyrdom, as has been said. And in this regard, just as the Holy Innocents do not attain to the perfect notion of martyrdom but do possess something of martyrdom from the fact that they suffered for Christ, so also they possess an aureole, not according to the perfect notion but according to a certain participation, namely, inasmuch as they rejoice that they were killed in service to Christ, as it was said of baptized children that they will have a certain joy at their innocence and integrity of flesh.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that just as through martyrdom and virginity one gains a most perfect victory over the flesh and the world, so also a most perfect victory against the devil is gained when someone not only does not yield to the devil’s assault but even drives him out, and this not only from himself but also from others. Now this happens through preaching and teaching. And thus an aureole is due to preaching and teaching as to virginity and martyrdom. Nor should we say, as some say, that it is due to prelates alone, to whom it belongs to preach and teach *ex officio*, but rather we should say that it belongs to whoever licitly carries out this act. To prelates, however, even though they have the office of preaching, it is only due if they actually preach. For a crown is not due to a habitual condition but to an actual struggle, according to 2 Timothy 2:5: *he is not crowned, unless he competes according to the rules*.  

Reply Obj. 1: Preaching and teaching are acts of a virtue, namely mercy. Hence they are counted among the spiritual alms,That is, spiritual works of mercy. as is clear above from Distinction 25, Question 2, Article 3.  

Reply Obj. 2: Even though the capability of preaching and teaching sometimes comes from study, the practice of teaching and preaching proceeds from the will, which is informed by charity infused by God. And so its act can be meritorious.  

Reply Obj. 3: Exaltation in this life does not lessen the reward of the next life, except for one who seeks his own glory in such exaltation. But one who turns this exaltation to the benefit of others acquires a recompense for himself from it. For when it is said that an aureole is due to teaching, this must be understood as to that teaching that concerns what pertains to salvation, through which the devil is driven from the hearts of men as by spiritual arms, of which it is said: *the weapons of our warfare are not carnal but spiritual* (2 Cor 10:4).  

Article 4  

***Who deserves an aureole***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fourth question, we proceed thus. It seems that Christ is due an aureole. For an aureole is due to virginity, martyrdom, and teaching. But in Christ these three were preeminently present. Therefore, an aureole especially belongs to Christ.  

Obj. 2: Everything that is most perfect in human matters should be especially attributed to Christ. But the reward of an aureole is due to the most surpassing merits. Therefore, it is due to Christ.  

Obj. 3: CyprianCyprian, *On the Dress of Virgins*, PL 4:477. says that virginity bears the image of God. Therefore, the exemplar of virginity is in God. And so it seems that an aureole also belongs to Christ as God.  

On the contrary (1), an aureole is joy in conformity to Christ, as is said. But no one is conformed or likened to himself, as is clear from the Philosopher.Aristotle, *Metaphysics*, Bk. 9, ch. 3, 1054b6. Therefore, Christ is not due an aureole.  

Furthermore (2), Christ’s reward never increased. But Christ did not have an aureole from the moment of his conception, since at that time he had never struggled. Therefore, he never had an aureole afterward.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that the angels are due an aureole. For, as Jerome saysJerome, *Epistle 9, to Paula and Eustochium*, PL 30:131. when speaking of virginity: *to live apart from the flesh in the flesh is more the life of angels than of man*. And a Gloss*Glossa Lombardi*, PL 191:1596; cf. Augustine, *On Holy Virginity* (CSEL 41), ch. 13. on 1 Corinthians 7 says that virginity is the share of the angels. Since, then, an aureole corresponds to virginity, it seems that it is due to angels.  

Obj. 2: Incorruption of spirit is more noble than incorruption of flesh. But in the angels incorruption of spirit is found, since they never sinned. Therefore, they are more due an aureole than are men who are incorrupt in the flesh, since these latter have otherwise sinned.  

Obj. 3: An aureole is due to teaching. But the angels teach us by purifying, illuminating, and perfecting, as Dionysius says.Pseudo-Dionysius, *Celestial Hierarchy*, ch. 10, n. 3, PG 3:273. Therefore, they are due an aureole, or at least those who teach.  

On the contrary (1), *he is not crowned, unless he competes according to the rules* (2 Tim 2:5). But there is no struggle in the angels. Therefore, neither are they due an aureole.  

Furthermore (2), an aureole is not due to an act that is not carried out through the body. Hence an aureole is not due to those who love virginity, martyrdom, and teaching, if these are not externally present in them. But the angels are incorporeal. Therefore, they do not possess an aureole.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that an aureole is also due to the body. For the essential reward is of greater account than the accidental. But a dowry, which pertains to the essential reward, is not only in the soul but also in the body. Therefore, so is an aureole, which pertains to the accidental reward.  

Obj. 2: A punishment in soul and body corresponds to sin carried out through the body. Therefore, to a merit carried out through the body, a reward in soul and body is also due. But the meriting of an aureole is carried out through the body. Therefore, an aureole is also due to the body.  

Obj. 3: A certain beauty of virtue will be apparent in the martyrs’ bodies in their bodies’ very scars. Hence Augustine says: *I do not know how we shall be so overcome with love for the blessed martyrs that we will wish in that kingdom to see the scars of their wounds in their bodies, those which they bore for the name of Christ. And perhaps we will see them. For in them there will be not deformity but dignity, and though in the body a certain beauty not of the body but of virtue will shine forth*.Augustine, *The City of God* (CCSL 48), Bk. 22, ch. 19. Therefore, it seems that the aureole of the martyrs is in the body. And the same reason pertains in the other cases.  

On the contrary (1), the souls now in paradise possess aureoles, but they do not have bodies. Therefore, the proper subject of an aureole is not the body but the soul.  

Furthermore (2), all merit is from the soul. Therefore, the whole reward should be in the soul.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, there are two opinions about this. For some say that in Christ there is an aureole according to the proper notion of aureole, since we find in him struggle and victory and, consequently, a crown according to its proper notion. But, even though the notion of golden crown or crown applies to Christ, the notion of aureole does not apply to him, if we consider the matter carefully. For the very fact that when we say ‘aureole’ we use the diminutive implies something possessed by way of participation and not according to its fullness. Therefore, having an aureole belongs to those in whom there is a participation in more perfect victory by imitation of that in which the full character of perfect victory consists. And thus since in Christ the principal and full character of such victory is found, through whose victory all other victors are so constituted, as is clear: *be of good cheer, I have overcome the world* (Jn 16:33), and *the Lion of the tribe of Judah . . . has conquered* (Rev 5:5), it does not belong to Christ to have an aureole but something from which all aureoles originate. As Revelation 3:21 says: *he who conquers, I will grant him to sit with me on my throne, as I myself conquered and sat down with my Father on his throne*. Hence we should say, according to others, that even though what is in Christ does not have the character of an aureole, it is more excellent than every aureole.  

Reply Obj. 1: Christ was most truly a virgin, martyr, and teacher, but even so the accidental reward corresponding to these qualities in Christ does not have any noteworthy quantity in comparison to the magnitude of the essential reward. Hence he does not have an aureole under the notion of aureole.  

Reply Obj. 2: Although an aureole is given for what from our perspective is a most perfect work, nevertheless we say ‘aureole’ in the diminutive, as this signifies a kind of participation of a perfection deriving from another in whom the work is found in its fullness. And in this respect it pertains to a kind of diminution and so is not found in Christ, in whom every perfection is found most fully.  

Reply Obj. 3: Even though virginity has, in a way, an exemplar in God, it does not have an exemplar according to a single notion. For the incorruption of God, which virginity imitates, is not in God and in a virgin according to the same notion.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that the angels are not due an aureole. The reason for this is that an aureole properly corresponds to a kind of perfection in surpassing merit. But the things that pertain to the perfection of merit in human beings are connatural to angels or even regard their common state or their reward itself. And thus angels do not have aureoles on account of the things by which an aureole is due to men.  

Reply Obj. 1: Virginity is called the angelic life inasmuch as through grace virgins imitate what the angels have by nature. For it is not a matter of virtue in the angels that they abstain altogether from the pleasures of the flesh, since pleasures of this sort cannot exist in them.  

Reply Obj. 2: Perpetual incorruption of spirit in angels merits the essential reward. For it is necessary for salvation, since in them there can be no subsequent reparation after a fall to ruin.  

Reply Obj. 3: The acts in accord with which the angels teach us pertain to their glory and to their common state. Hence they do not merit an aureole through such acts.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that an aureole is properly in the mind, for it is joy over the works to which an aureole is due. But just as from the joy of the essential reward, which is a golden crown, a certain comeliness overflows into the body, which is the body’s glory, so also from the joy of an aureole a comeliness is reflected in the body in such a way that an aureole is principally in the mind but is reflected also in the flesh through a kind of overflowing.  

And in this way the reply to the objections is clear. It should be known, however, that the comeliness of the scars that will appear in the bodies of the martyrs cannot be called an aureole. For some martyrs in whom there will not be such scars will have an aureole, as, for example, those who were drowned or killed off by starvation or the squalor of imprisonment.  

Article 5  

***On the number of the aureoles***

***Quaestiuncula 1***

Obj. 1: To the fifth question, we proceed thus.Parallel: *Super II Tim*. 4, lec. 2. It seems that the three aureoles of virgins, martyrs, and preachers are not fittingly enumerated. For the aureole of martyrs corresponds to the virtue of fortitude, the aureole of virgins to the virtue of temperance, and the aureole of teachers to the virtue of prudence. Therefore, it seems that there should be a fourth aureole due to justice.  

Obj. 2: A GlossBede, *On the Tabernacle* (CCSL 119A), Bk. 1, ch. 6. on Exodus 25 says that the golden crown is added since through the Gospel eternal life is promised to those who keep the commandments: *if you would enter life, keep the commandments* (Mt 19:17). An aureole is added on top of this when it is said: *if you would be perfect, go, sell what you possess and give to the poor* (Mt 19:21). Therefore, an aureole is due to poverty.  

Obj. 3: By a vow of obedience one subjects himself wholly to God. Therefore, the utmost perfection consists in the vow of obedience. And thus it seems that an aureole is due to it.  

Obj. 4: There are also many other works of supererogation over which man will have special joy in the future. Therefore, there are many other aureoles besides these three.  

Obj. 5: Just as a person spreads the faith by preaching and teaching, so does someone who compiles written records. Therefore, a fourth aureole is due to such as these.  

Quaestiuncula 2  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that the aureole of virgins is the most important of all.Parallel: *ST* II-II, q. 152, a. 3. For it is said (Rev 14:3–4) of virgins that *it is these who follow the Lamb wherever he goes* and that *no one* else *could learn that song* that the virgins chanted. Therefore, virgins have the most excellent aureole.  

Obj. 2: CyprianCyprian, *On the Dress of Virgins*, PL 4:455. says of virgins that they are the most illustrious portion of Christ’s flock. Therefore, they are more due an aureole.  

Obj. 3: It seems that the aureole of martyrs is of greatest account. For HaymoHaymo, *On the Apocalypse*, Bk. 4, on 14:3, PL 117:1106. says that not all the virgins have precedence over spouses but rather those who are reckoned equal to the married martyrs for guarding virginity in the torment of suffering. Therefore, it is martyrdom that gives to virginity preeminence over other states. Therefore, martyrdom is due an aureole of greater account.  

Obj. 4: It seems that a teacher of the faith is due the aureole of greatest account. For the Church Militant has the Church Triumphant as exemplar. But in the Church Militant teachers are due the greatest honor: *let the elders who rule well be considered worthy of double honor, especially those who labor in preaching and teaching* (1 Tim 5:17). Therefore, in the Church Triumphant they are due an aureole of greater account.  

Quaestiuncula 3  

Obj. 1: Moreover, it seems that one person does not have an aureole (whether of virginity, martyrdom, or teaching) in a more excellent way than another. For those things that are at the limit of perfection are not intensified or diminished. But an aureole is due to works that are at the limit of perfection. Therefore, no aureole is intensified or diminished.  

Obj. 2: Virginity does not admit of more or less, since it implies a certain privation and negations are not more or less intense. Therefore, neither is the reward of virginity, namely the aureole of virgins, made more or less intense.  

On the contrary, an aureole is added on top of the golden crown. But the golden crown is more intense in one than another. Therefore, so too is an aureole.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 1  

I answer that, an aureole is a kind of privileged reward corresponding to a privileged victory, as is clear from what has been said. And thus the three aureoles are taken on the basis of privileged victories in the three struggles that assail every man.  

For in the struggle against the flesh, a person gains the greatest victory who abstains altogether from sexual pleasures, which are the greatest of this kind. And thus an aureole is due to virginity. Next, in the struggle by which we fight against the world, the chief victory is when we undergo persecution by the world to the point of death. Hence also martyrs, who gain victory in this struggle are due the second aureole. Finally, in the struggle by which we fight against the devil, the chief victory is when someone casts out the enemy not only from himself but also from the hearts of others, which occurs through preaching and teaching. And thus to teachers and preachers the third aureole is due.  

Some, however, distinguish the three aureoles on the basis of the three powers of the soul such that the three aureoles are said to correspond to the greatest acts of the soul’s three powers. For the greatest act of reason is to spread the truth of the faith also to others, and to this act is due the aureole of teachers. The greatest act of the irascible power is to overcome even death for the sake of Christ, and to this act is due the aureole of martyrs. Finally, the greatest act of the concupiscible power is to abstain thoroughly from the greatest pleasure, and to this is due the aureole of virgins. But others distinguish the three aureoles on the basis of the things by which we are conformed most nobly to Christ. For he was mediator between the world and the Father. He was, therefore, a teacher insofar as he manifested to the world the truth he had received from the Father. He was, in turn, a martyr to the extent that he bore persecution from the world. Finally, he was a virgin inasmuch as he preserved purity in himself. And thus teachers, martyrs, and virgins are conformed to him most perfectly. Hence an aureole is due to such people.  

Reply Obj. 1: We do not find a struggle in the act of justice as in the acts of other virtues. Nor is it true that teaching is an act of prudence, for it is rather an act of charity or mercy to the extent that such a habit inclines us to carry out the act of teaching, or even an act of wisdom as directing it. Or we may say, according to others, that justice encompasses all the virtues and thus a special aureole is not due to it. Now, how this can be true was said in Book III, Distinction 33, Question 3, Article 4.  

Reply Obj. 2: Poverty, although it is a work of perfection, does not hold the first place in any spiritual struggle. For the love of corporeal things assaults us less than the concupiscence of the flesh or persecution inflicted on one’s own body. Hence poverty is not due an aureole. Rather it is due judiciary power by reason of the humiliation consequent on poverty, as said above.Book IV, D. 47, Q. 1, Art. 2, qc 2. The Gloss cited, however, takes aureole in a broad sense for any kind of reward rendered for merit.  

A similar reply can be given to the third and fourth objections.  

Reply Obj. 5: An aureole is also due to those who write down sacred teaching, but this is not distinguished from the aureole of teachers. For to compose things in writing is a certain way of teaching.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 2  

To the second question, it should be said that the preeminence of one aureole over another aureole can be considered in two ways. First, on the side of the struggle, such that an aureole is said to be greater when it is due to a tougher struggle. And in this way the aureole of martyrs has preeminence over the other aureoles in one way and the aureole of virgins in another. For the struggle in martyrdom is tougher in itself and afflicts with greater strength, whereas the struggle of the flesh is more dangerous inasmuch as it lasts longer and looms much closer to us. Second, on the part of those things that the struggle pertains to. And in this way the aureole of teachers is greatest among the others. For this sort of struggle focuses on intelligible goods while the others have to do with sensible passions. But the eminence that is found on the part of the struggle is more essential to the aureole. For the aureole, in its proper notion, regards victory and struggle. The difficulty of the struggle, too, that is found on the part of the struggle itself is of greater account than what is found on our side according as it is closer to us. And thus, simply speaking, the aureole of martyrs is greatest among them all. And thus a Gloss on Matthew 5:10, *blessed are those who are persecuted*, says that in the eighth beatitude, which pertains to martyrs, all the others are brought to perfection. For this reason, too, when the Church lists out the saints, she orders martyrs before teachers and virgins.In, for example, the traditional Litany of the Saints chanted in the liturgy. But nothing prevents the other aureoles from being more excellent in some respect.  

And in this way the solution of the objections is clear.  

Response to Quaestiuncula 3  

To the third question, it should be said that since merit is in a certain way the cause of reward, rewards must be differentiated as merits are differentiated. For something is made more or less intense through its cause being made more or less intense. But the merit of an aureole can be greater or less. Thus an aureole, too, can be greater or less. It should be known, however, that the merit of an aureole can be made more intense in two ways: in one way on the part of the root and in another way on the part of the work. For it can be the case that there are two people, one of whom undergoes a greater torment of martyrdom out of less charity, or gives greater attention to preaching, or even removes himself more from the pleasures of the flesh. Therefore, the intensity of the aureole does not correspond to the intensity of merit that is found as coming from the root but rather this supplies the intensity of the golden crown. Instead, the intensity of the aureole corresponds to the intensity of merit from the genus of the act. This is how it can be the case that one who merits less in martyrdom as regards the essential reward will have a greater aureole for the martyrdom.  

Reply Obj. 1: The merits that should be awarded aureoles do not attain to the limit of perfection simply but rather in species, just as fire is the most subtle of bodies in its species. Hence nothing prevents one aureole from being more excellent than another, just as one fire is more subtle than another.  

Reply Obj. 2: One person’s virginity can be greater than another’s due to greater withdrawal from what is contrary to virginity in such a way that there is said to be a greater virginity in her who avoids more the occasions of corruption, just as privations can be made more intense, as when it is said that a person is more blind because he is further removed from sight.  

Exposition of the Text  

*“Distinct boundaries shall mark the two cities.”**Sententiae* IV, 49.1 (280). 1 citing Augustine, *Enchiridion*, ch. 111. This is said because the number of the elect and the damned is being increased until the day of judgment and also because glory and punishment can be increased, at least as regards incidental reward and punishment. But after the judgment they will not be able to be further increased.  

*The coin is one*.*Sent*. IV, 49.1 (280). 2. This is said because the object of all the blessed is the same.  

*“He lives blessedly indeed who lives as he wills, and wills nothing improperly*.”*Sent*. IV, 49.1 (280). 8 citing Augustine, *On the Trinity*, Bk. 13 ch. 8 n. 11. This opinion of Augustine harmonizes with the Philosopher’s opinion,Aristotle, *On the Soul*, Bk. 2, ch. 4, 415b9–12. such that by *living* the activity of life is understood, by *as he wills* it is shown that activity is not impeded, and by *and wills nothing improperly*, it is shown to be connatural because evils are contrary to nature.  

*And it is held back by this desire from tending with all its might to that highest heaven, until that desire comes to rest*.*Sent*. IV, 49.4 (283). 3. This being held back is understood as regards the fact that the soul separated from the body does not have perfect being and thus neither does it have the perfect activity by which it can be joined to God perfectly.
